Ibrahim's Star-Gazing and Claim of Illness: Refuting the Objections
Razi refutes two objections against Ibrahim's star-gazing and illness claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Ibrahim's actions against two objections: that looking at stars is forbidden and that claiming illness was a lie.

Core Idea
Not all star-gazing is forbidden, and a prophet never lies.

In this passage, Razi tackles two objections raised against Ibrahim's prophetic integrity. The first concerns his looking at the stars, which critics claim is a forbidden practice. The second targets his statement 'I am sick' as a lie. Razi offers multiple explanations to show that neither action was sinful. This lesson walks through each objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and note the two aspects of the objection: (1) star-gazing is forbidden, (2) 'I am sick' is a lie. Razi addresses each separately.
2
Explore Razi's answers for each objection
For the first objection, note Razi's arguments: star-gazing for tawhid is praiseworthy; Ibrahim may have had divine knowledge; he may have pretended to rely on stars to mislead. For the second, understand the three interpretations that avoid falsehood.
Razi provides multiple plausible explanations to show the prophet's actions were not sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: قوله تعالى: {فَنَظَرَ نَظْرَةً فِي النُّجُومِ فَقَالَ إِنِّي سَقِيمٌ} [الصافات: ٨٨-٨٩] والتمسك بها من وجهين. الأول: أن النظر في علم النجوم حرام. الثاني: أن قوله: إني سقيم كذب. والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن النظر في علم النجوم حرام مطلقا، بل من نظر فيها ليستدل بها على توحيد الله تعالى كان ذلك أعظم الطاعات، ولهذا السبب استحق إبراهيم المدح بالنظر في النجوم، وهو قوله: {فَلَمَّا جَنَّ عَلَيْهِ اللَّيْلُ رَأَى كَوْكَبًا} [الأنعام: ٧٦]. وأيضا: فلعل الله تعالى أخبر الخليل أنه مهما طلع النجم الفلاني فإنك تمرض، فنظر في النجوم، فلما رأى قربه قال: إني سقيم. وأيضا: لعله نظر في النجوم تشبها بأهل زمانه في الظاهر، وحكمه بأنه سقيم إيهام لقومه أنه إنما حكم بهذا الحكم بناء على النجوم. وأما الوجه الثاني - وهو قوله: إني سقيم أي كذب - فجوابه من وجوه: أحدها: لا نسلم بأنه كذب بل لعله سقيم في تلك الساعة. وثانيها: لعله إنما عرف أنه سيصير سقيما في الزمان الثاني، فقال: إني سقيم على تأويل إني أكون سقيما في ذلك الوقت، كما أنك إذا علمت أنك ستصير محموما وقت الظهر، ثم إن أحدا يدعوك إلى الضيافة بحيث تعلم أنه لا بد من الجلوس مع القوم إلى وقت الظهر، فتقول: إني محموم وتعني به أنك تكون محموما في ذلك الوقت. وثالثها: لعله أراد أنه سقيم القلب، والمراد ما في قلبه من الحزن والغم بسبب عنادهم.
The third objection: His saying, exalted: 'Then he looked a look at the stars and said, I am sick' (al-Saffat 88-89). And it is clung to from two aspects. The first: That looking into the science of stars is forbidden. The second: That his saying 'I am sick' is a lie. The answer concerning the first: We do not concede that looking into the science of stars is absolutely forbidden; rather, whoever looks into them to deduce thereby the oneness of Allah the Exalted, that is the greatest of acts of worship. For this reason Ibrahim deserved praise for looking at the stars, as in His saying: 'When the night covered him, he saw a star' (al-An'am 76). Also: Perhaps Allah the Exalted informed the Friend that whenever such-and-such a star rises, you will become sick, so he looked at the stars, and when he saw its nearness he said 'I am sick.' Also: Perhaps he looked at the stars outwardly resembling the people of his time, and his judgment that he was sick was to give his people the impression that he only made this judgment based on the stars. As for the second aspect – that his saying 'I am sick' is a lie – its answer is from aspects: One of them: We do not concede that it is a lie; rather, perhaps he was sick at that moment. Second: Perhaps he knew that he would become sick in the second time, so he said 'I am sick' with the interpretation 'I will be sick at that time,' just as when you know that you will become feverish at noon, then someone invites you to hospitality while you know that you must sit with the people until noon, so you say 'I am feverish' and mean by it that you will be feverish at that time. Third: Perhaps he meant that he was sick at heart, meaning what is in his heart of grief and sorrow due to their stubbornness.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections derived from Ibrahim's story: (1) looking into astrology is prohibited, and (2) saying 'I am sick' is a lie. Razi answers each. For the first, he states that looking into stars is not absolutely forbidden; if done to deduce evidence for God's oneness, it is the greatest act of worship. Moreover, Ibrahim may have been informed by God that a specific star's rising would coincide with his illness, so he looked and then said he was sick. Alternatively, he may have appeared to rely on astrology to mislead his people. For the second objection, Razi gives three interpretations: (a) he was actually sick at that moment; (b) he knew he would become sick later, so he said it with future intent, analogous to knowing you will have a fever at noon and saying 'I have a fever'; (c) he meant sick at heart due to grief over his people's stubbornness.

Key Terms
نظر في النجوم
تأويل
إيهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any star-gazing is sinful and forbidden in Islam.
Correction: Razi distinguishes between astrology for forbidden purposes and looking at stars to deduce evidence of Allah's oneness, which is an act of worship.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Razi's answers to the objection that Ibrahim's statement 'I am sick' was a lie?
A) He was actually sick at that moment
B) He meant he would be sick in the future
C) He meant sick at heart
D) He was lying to save himself

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that Ibrahim's actions were either permissible or had valid interpretations, thus maintaining his prophetic excellence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibrahim's actions were misinterpreted by his people. When have you been misunderstood for doing something righteous? How can you seek to clarify intentions without compromising truth?
?
Razi offers multiple interpretations to preserve truthfulness. In your own life, are you quick to assume the worst of others' ambiguous statements? How can you adopt the principle of giving benefit of the doubt?
?
Consider a time when you used ambiguous language to avoid a difficult situation. Was it truthful in spirit? How can you emulate Ibrahim's example of being truthful even when using indirect speech?

Defending Ibrahim's Star-Gazing as an Act of Tawhid
Razi refutes the objection that Ibrahim's star-gazing was sinful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Ibrahim's star-gazing and why the intention behind looking at stars determines its ruling.

Core Idea
Intentions transform actions: Ibrahim's star-gazing was an act of tawhid, not sin.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the prophethood of Ibrahim (AS): his looking at stars, which some claim is prohibited. Imam Razi provides a powerful refutation by showing that Ibrahim's action was either an act of tawhid, a response to divine inspiration, or a strategic pretense. Understanding this helps clarify that actions are judged by their intentions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening claim that looking into the science of stars is absolutely forbidden. Note that this is a common criticism.
The objection is stated in the first line of the passage.
2
Explore Razi's first answer
Razi rejects the absolute prohibition. He argues that if one looks at stars to derive evidence for Allah's tawhid, it becomes the greatest act of obedience.
This is supported by the Quranic verse about Ibrahim seeing a star.
3
Analyze the two additional interpretations
Razi offers two more possibilities: Ibrahim may have received divine prior knowledge that a star's rising would indicate his illness, or he may have pretended to rely on stars to blend in with his people (taqiyya).
Both interpretations remove any sin from Ibrahim's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن النظر في علم النجوم حرام. والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن النظر في علم النجوم حرام مطلقا، بل من نظر فيها ليستدل بها على توحيد الله تعالى كان ذلك أعظم الطاعات، ولهذا السبب استحق إبراهيم المدح بالنظر في النجوم، وهو قوله : فَلَمَّا جَنَّ عَلَيْهِ الَّيْلُ رَأَى كَوْكَبًا [الأنعام: ٧٦]. وأيضًا: فلعل الله تعالى أخبر الخليل الله أنه مهما طلع النجم الفلاني فإنك تمرض، فنظر في النجوم ، فلما رأى قربه قال : " إني سقيم " . وأيضًا: لعله نظر في النجوم تشبها بأهل زمانه في الظاهر، وحكمه بأنه سقيم إيهام لقومه أنه إنما حكم بهذا الحكم بناء على النجوم.
The first: that looking into the science of stars is forbidden. The answer to the first: we do not concede that looking into the science of stars is absolutely forbidden; rather, whoever looks into it to use it as evidence for the tawhid of Allah the Exalted, that is the greatest of acts of obedience. And for this reason Ibrahim (peace be upon him) deserved praise for looking into the stars, which is His saying: 'When the night covered him, he saw a star' [Al-An'am 76]. Also: perhaps Allah the Exalted informed the Friend of Allah (Ibrahim) that whenever a certain star rises, you will become ill, so he looked at the stars, and when he saw it rising, he said 'I am sick.' Also: perhaps he looked at the stars outwardly to resemble the people of his time, and his judgment that he was sick was a pretense to his people that he only made this judgment based on the stars.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that Ibrahim's looking at stars was sinful because it is prohibited in Islam. Razi refutes this by distinguishing between different intentions: if one looks at stars to derive evidence for God's existence, it is the greatest act of obedience. He provides three explanations for Ibrahim's action: first, he was praised for using stars for tawhid (as in Quran 6:76); second, he may have been informed by God that a star's rising would indicate illness, so he said 'I am sick' truthfully; third, he pretended to rely on astrology to mislead his people. In all cases, Ibrahim did not sin.

Key Terms
النظر في علم النجوم
الاستدلال
التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Looking at stars is always forbidden in Islam.
Correction: Razi shows that it depends on intention: using stars for tawhid is the greatest act of obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the ruling on looking into astronomy (علم النجوم) if done to derive evidence for tawhid?
A) It is absolutely forbidden
B) It is the greatest act of obedience
C) It is permissible but not recommended
D) It is only allowed for prophets

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's star-gazing was not a sin but a model of how to turn scientific observation into evidence for tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in an activity like studying science, observing nature, or even scrolling through social media, do you consciously pause to consider your intention? Could you transform that activity into an act of worship by connecting it to Allah's signs, as Ibrahim did with the stars? What would that require of you starting today?
?
Ibrahim's star-gazing was not a sinful act because his heart was fixed on tawhid. Is there an area of your life where you fear you are sinning because of an external action, but perhaps your intention could redeem it? Examine one such area right now—what is the real intention behind your action, and how can you align it with seeking Allah's pleasure?

Defending Ibrahim's Saying 'I Am Sick' – Three Interpretations
Razi provides three interpretations to show Ibrahim's statement was not a lie.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three valid interpretations that show Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'I am sick' was not a lie, preserving his truthfulness.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's 'I am sick' was truth, not a lie—via literal, future, or metaphorical interpretation.

The objection that Prophet Ibrahim (AS) lied when he said 'إني سقيم' (I am sick) challenges his prophetic truthfulness. Imam Razi provides three scholarly interpretations that demonstrate the statement could be truthful. Each interpretation respects the integrity of the prophet while offering a reasoned defense. Let's explore these answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Razi's three answers in the Arabic passage. Notice how each begins with a different perspective.
The passage is a direct response to a specific objection.
2
Identify the three answers
The text lists three answers using 'أحدها', 'ثانيها', 'ثالثها'. Find each one and note the type of interpretation.
First literal, second future prediction, third metaphorical.
3
Understand the logic of each
For each answer, ask: how does it show the statement is truthful? The commentary explains the reasoning.
Pay special attention to the analogy in the second answer.
4
Reflect on prophetic truthfulness
Consider why defending prophets from lying is important in Islamic theology. This connects to the concept of 'ismah (infallibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني : - وهو قوله : إني "سقيم " أي كذب - فجوابه من وجوه : أحدها: لا نسلم بأنه كذب بل لعله سقيم في تلك الساعة. وثانيها : لعله إنما عرف أنه سيصير سقيما في الزمان الثاني، فقال: إني سقيم على تأويل إني أكون سقيما في ذلك الوقت كما أنك إذا علمت أنك ستصير محموما وقت الظهر، ثم إن أحدا يدعوك إلى الضيافة بحيث تعلم أنه لا بد من الجلوس مع القوم، إلى وقت الظهر، فتقول : إني محموم وتعني به أنك تكون محموما في ذلك الوقت. وثالثها : لعله أراد أنه سقيم القلب، والمراد ما في قلبه من الحزن والغم، بسبب عنادهم.
As for the second aspect – and it is his saying 'I am sick,' meaning a lie – its answer is from several perspectives: First: we do not concede that it was a lie; rather, perhaps he was sick at that hour. Second: perhaps he knew that he would become sick in the next time, so he said 'I am sick' on the interpretation that I will be sick at that time, just as if you know that you will have a fever at noon, and then someone invites you to a gathering such that you know that you will have to sit with the people until noon, so you say 'I have a fever' meaning that you will have a fever at that time. Third: perhaps he intended that he was sick at heart, and what he meant is the sorrow and grief in his heart due to their obstinacy.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage gives three answers to the objection that Ibrahim lied when he said 'I am sick.' The first answer is literal: he may have actually been physically sick at that moment. The second uses a future interpretation: he knew he would be sick later, so he said it as a statement of future fact, similar to saying 'I have a fever' when expecting one. The third is metaphorical: he meant spiritual sickness due to grief over his people's disbelief. All three show that his statement was not a lie.

Key Terms
الكذب
التأويل
سقيم القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Ibrahim lied when he said 'I am sick' to avoid going with his people.
Correction: Razi shows the statement can be truthful through three interpretations, defending prophetic truthfulness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Razi's answers to the objection that Ibrahim lied when he said 'إني سقيم'?
A) He was actually physically sick at that moment
B) He meant he would be sick in the future
C) He was lying to protect himself
D) He meant he was sick at heart due to grief

Key Takeaway
Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'I am sick' can be understood literally, as a future prediction, or metaphorically, all preserving his truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you consider how your words might be interpreted by others? Ibrahim's example shows that even a seemingly simple statement can carry deeper meanings. Will you commit to being truthful in both your literal and figurative speech, guarding against any deception?
?
Ibrahim's heart was 'sick' with grief over his people's stubborn disbelief. When you see people around you rejecting truth or persisting in error, does your heart ache with sincere concern, or have you become numb? Will you let that grief move you to pity and sincere supplication?
?
Razi gave multiple valid interpretations to defend Ibrahim's truthfulness. Are you quick to assume the worst about others' statements, or do you seek charitable interpretations? Will you adopt the habit of giving fellow Muslims the benefit of the doubt, as the scholars did for the prophets?

Interpreting Ibrahim's Request to See Resurrection
Razi offers four interpretations for Ibrahim's request in Surah al-Baqarah 260.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Ibrahim's request to see resurrection through four distinct interpretations, clarifying that it does not imply doubt in Allah's power.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's request was about reassurance, not doubt.

Some critics argue that Ibrahim's request in Surah al-Baqarah 260 suggests doubt in Allah's ability to resurrect. Imam Razi responds by offering four plausible interpretations that explain the request as seeking confirmation, not questioning divine power. This lesson examines each interpretation and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the objection and the four interpretations Razi provides.
2
Identify the objection
Understand the doubt: some claim Ibrahim's request implies he did not believe in resurrection. Razi refutes this by showing alternative explanations.
3
Examine each interpretation
Go through each of the four interpretations Razi offers. For each, ask: what is the key idea and how does it preserve Ibrahim's status?
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle that prophets do not doubt Allah's power; their requests are for reassurance or specific knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : أرني كَيْفَ تُحْيِ الْمَوْتَى قَالَ أَوَلَمْ تُؤْمِنُ قال بلى ولكن ليَطْمَينَ فَلَى [البقرة: ٢٦٠]. والجواب : ليس في الآية ما يدل على أن هذه الطمأنينة مطلوبة فيما إذا رأى، وهو يحتمل وجوها : أحدها: أن النبي الله إذا جاءه الملك احتاج إلى أن يظهر الملك دليلا على كونه صادقًا - معجزة - وإلا لم يعرف الفرق بينه و بين الشيطان، فلعله طلب الإحياء والإماتة من جبريل الله ليعرف أنه صادق في كونه رسولا من الله تعالى إليه، وليطمئن قلبه على ذلك. وثانيها : روى جعفر الصادق له الله أنه قال : إن الله تعالى أوحى إليه إني إنسانًا خليلاً، وعلامته أني أحيي وأميت بدعائه، فلما ظهر من إبراهيم الظن أنواع الطاعات وقع في قلبه أنه ربما كان ذلك الإنسان أنا فطلب الإحياء والإماتة، حتى إنه إن وقع اطمأن قلبه على أن ذلك هو الخليل لا غيره. وثالثها: أن يقال : وقعت هذه الواقعة قبل النبوة. ورابعها أن يقال الغرض تكثير الدلائل ليكون العلم أبعد من الشكوك.
The fifth doubt: holding fast to His saying: 'Show me how You give life to the dead. He said: Do you not believe? He said: Yes, but so that my heart may be at rest' [Al-Baqarah 260]. The answer: There is nothing in the verse to indicate that this tranquility is sought when he sees, and it admits of interpretations: First: that when the prophet is visited by an angel, the angel needs to show a miracle as evidence of his truthfulness, otherwise the difference between him and the devil would not be known. So perhaps he (Ibrahim) requested giving life and death from Jibril in order to know that he is truthful in being a messenger from Allah, and so that his heart may be at rest. Second: Ja'far al-Sadiq narrated that Allah revealed to him: 'I am taking a human as a friend (khalil), and the sign is that he gives life and death by his prayer.' So when there appeared from Ibrahim various sorts of obedience, it entered his heart that perhaps that human was himself, so he requested giving life and death, so that if it occurred, his heart would be at rest that that person was the khalil, none other. Third: It is said that this event occurred before prophethood. Fourth: It is said that the purpose is to multiply proofs so that knowledge would be further from doubts.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The doubt claims that Ibrahim's request for a vision of resurrection implies doubt in God's power. Razi provides four interpretations showing otherwise. First, Ibrahim may have been testing an angel (Jibril) to confirm his truthfulness. Second, a narration from Ja'far al-Sadiq says Ibrahim wanted to confirm that he himself was the friend of Allah who could give life. Third, the event may have occurred before his prophethood. Fourth, it may have been to increase certainty through multiple proofs. None imply doubt in God's ability.

Key Terms
الطمأنينة
المعجزة
الخليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim's request indicates he doubted Allah's power to resurrect.
Correction: Razi's interpretations show Ibrahim sought reassurance, clarification, or additional evidence—not that he disbelieved.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of Razi's interpretations of Ibrahim's request to see resurrection?
A) He doubted the resurrection
B) He wanted to test Jibril's truthfulness
C) He wanted to prove his own prophethood
D) He wanted to show his people a miracle

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's request was a search for confirmation through signs, consistent with prophetic certainty, not a doubt in Allah's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for a sign or a deepening of certainty, is it because your faith is weak or because you want to move from knowledge to heartfelt tranquility? What would it take for your heart to be at rest like Ibrahim's?
?
Ibrahim's request was rooted in his desire to confirm a spiritual matter, not in doubt. Is there an area of your faith where you are holding back from seeking deeper confirmation because you fear it might expose a lack? Will you ask Allah for certainty even if it requires vulnerability?
?
The four interpretations all protect Ibrahim's high rank. Are you quick to judge others' spiritual struggles as signs of weakness, or do you, like Razi, seek charitable explanations that elevate their faith? How can you apply this to your own self-judgment today?

Explaining Ibrahim's Prayer for His Disbelieving Father
Razi defends Ibrahim's seeking forgiveness for his father despite its prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi resolves the apparent contradiction between Ibrahim's prayer for his father and the Quranic prohibition against seeking forgiveness for polytheists.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's prayer was not sinful because rulings vary across laws and he hoped for his father's faith.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against the sinlessness of prophets: if Ibrahim was a prophet, how could he pray for his disbelieving father when the Quran later forbids it? Imam Razi presents two compelling answers that protect Ibrahim's innocence while upholding the Quranic prohibition. We will see how the variation of divine laws and Ibrahim's hope for his father's faith provide the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the premise that Ibrahim's father was a disbeliever, and that the Quran prohibits seeking forgiveness for polytheists.
The objector uses two Quranic verses to establish the prohibition.
2
Find Razi's admission
Note that Razi concedes all premises except the absolute impermissibility of seeking forgiveness for a disbeliever.
This is how he narrows the debate.
3
Understand the first answer
Razi says the ruling varies by law; Ibrahim may not have had the prohibition in his own law until later revelation.
This respects both Ibrahim's act and the later Quranic command.
4
Understand the second answer
Ibrahim sought forgiveness hoping for his father's faith; when he despaired, he stopped. This shows his act was conditional, not sinful.
The verse in al-Mumtahanah excepts Ibrahim's statement, supporting this.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how Razi's answers defend prophetic sinlessness while preserving the Quran's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : أن إبراهيم الله استغفر لأبيه؛ لقوله تعالى حكاية عن إبراهيم سَأَسْتَغْفِرُ لَكَ رَبِّي [مريم : ٤٧] ولقوله : واغفر لأن إنهم كان من الضالين [الشُّعَرَاء : ٨٦] وأبوه كان كافرًا لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ لِأَبِيهِ وَازَرَ أَتَتَّخِذُ أَصْنَامًا وَالِهَةٌ إِنِّي أَرَبِّكَ وَقَوْمَكَ فِي ضَلَلٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٤] والاستغفار لأجل الكافر غير جائز ؛ لقوله تعالى : ما كان النبي والذين امنوا أَن يَسْتَغْفِرُوا لِلْمُشْرِكِينَ [التوبة: ١١٣] ولقوله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ كَانَتْ لَكُمْ أَسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ فِي إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَالَّذِينَ مَعَهُ إِذْ [ المُمتَحنَة : ٤] إلى قوله: ﴿ إلا قول إبراهيم لِأَبِيهِ لَأَسْتَغْفِرَنَّ لَكَ وَمَا أَمْلِكُ لَكَ مِنَ الله مِن شَيْءٍ [الممتحنة : ٤] فأمره بالتأسي بإبراهيم إلا في هذا القول، فثبت أن هذا كان من المعصية. والجواب: كل المقدمات مسلمة إلا قوله : لا يجوز الاستغفار للكافر فإن فيه جوابين : أحدهما أن ذلك مما يختلف باختلاف الشرائع، فلعل إبراهيم لم يجد في شرعه ما يمنعه عنه، فلا جرم استغفر لأبيه، فلما منعه الله منه سكت. وثانيهما : أنه إنما استغفر لأبيه لأنه كان يتوقع منه الإيمان فلما أيس منه.
The sixth doubt: That Ibrahim sought forgiveness for his father, due to His saying regarding Ibrahim: 'I will ask forgiveness for you from my Lord' [Maryam 47] and His saying: 'And forgive my father, for indeed he was of the straying' [Ash-Shu'ara 86]. And his father was a disbeliever due to His saying: 'And when Ibrahim said to his father Azar: Do you take idols as gods? Indeed, I see you and your people in manifest error' [Al-An'am 74]. And seeking forgiveness for a disbeliever is not permissible due to His saying: 'It is not for the Prophet and those who believe to ask forgiveness for the polytheists' [At-Tawbah 113] and His saying: 'There has been for you an excellent example in Ibrahim and those with him when [Al-Mumtahanah 4] up to His saying: 'Except the saying of Ibrahim to his father: I will surely ask forgiveness for you, but I possess nothing for you from Allah' [Al-Mumtahanah 4]. So He commanded to follow Ibrahim except in this statement. Thus it is established that this was from disobedience. The answer: All premises are conceded except his statement: 'It is not permissible to ask forgiveness for a disbeliever,' for there are two answers regarding it: First: that this varies with variations in laws; perhaps Ibrahim did not find in his law anything preventing him from it, so he sought forgiveness for his father, and when Allah forbade him, he desisted. Second: that he only sought forgiveness for his father because he expected faith from him; when he despaired of him, he stopped.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that Ibrahim's prayer for his disbelieving father was sinful because the Quran prohibits seeking forgiveness for polytheists. Razi responds by questioning only one premise: that seeking forgiveness for a disbeliever is absolutely impermissible. He gives two answers. First, the ruling may differ according to different pre-Islamic laws; Ibrahim may not have been prohibited in his time until later revelation forbade it. Second, Ibrahim prayed for his father hoping he would believe, and when he despaired of his faith, he stopped. The verse in al-Mumtahanah actually excepts Ibrahim's statement, indicating it was specific to his situation.

الكافر
الاختلاف بالشرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any prayer for a disbeliever is always prohibited
Correction: Razi shows the ruling varies by law and context; Ibrahim's prayer was not sinful because it was based on hope and before prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why was Ibrahim's seeking forgiveness for his father not necessarily sinful?
A) Because his father was not actually a disbeliever
B) Because the prohibition came later in revelation
C) Because Ibrahim was not a prophet at that time
D) Because seeking forgiveness for disbelievers is always allowed

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's prayer for his father was not a sin because divine laws vary and his hope for faith justified the prayer until despair.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for someone who is far from guidance, do you rely on genuine hope or do you give up when results don't come quickly? Ibrahim continued until Allah's command; what would it take for you to persist like that?
?
Ibrahim's prayer was based on hope, not disobedience. In your own life, are there prayers you have abandoned because of fear that they might be inappropriate or useless? Are you willing to take that risk for the sake of those you love?
?
The objection against Ibrahim assumed his action was sinful because it violated a general rule. How quick are you to judge others' actions as wrong without considering the possibility of exceptional circumstances or a higher purpose?

Fifth Objection: Ibrahim's Request and Its Refutation
Razi presents and refutes the objection that Ibrahim's request for a sign indicated doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibrahim's request to see the dead revived does not imply doubt, and how Razi provides four interpretations to defend prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's request was for reassurance, not because of doubt in God's power.

The fifth objection against prophetic infallibility cites Ibrahim's request in Quran 2:260 to see how God revives the dead, which some claim indicates doubt. Razi responds by showing that the verse admits multiple interpretations that all preserve Ibrahim's infallibility. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the four answers Razi provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Start by reading the fifth objection and the Quranic verse it relies on. Understand that the objector claims Ibrahim's request shows doubt.
The verse is 2:260.
2
Understand Razi's initial answer
Razi first points out that the verse does not require that reassurance be sought only when seeing. This opens the door for multiple interpretations.
3
Explore the first interpretation
Ibrahim may have asked Gabriel to perform a miracle (bringing life and death) to confirm he was a truthful messenger, distinguishing him from Satan.
This interpretation focuses on the need for a prophetic miracle.
4
Study the second interpretation from Ja'far al-Sadiq
God had revealed that He takes a human as a friend whose sign is giving life and death. Ibrahim, suspecting he might be that friend, asked to see the sign to confirm his status.
This interpretation is narrated from Ja'far al-Sadiq.
5
Consider the third and fourth interpretations
The third says this event occurred before Ibrahim's prophethood, so no fault. The fourth says the purpose was to multiply proofs to strengthen certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : أرني كَيْفَ تُحْيِ الْمَوْتَى قَالَ أَوَلَمْ تُؤْمِنُ قال بلى ولكن ليطمئن قلبي [البقرة: ٢٦٠]. والجواب : ليس في الآية ما يدل على أن هذه الطمأنينة مطلوبة فيما إذا رأى، وهو يحتمل وجوها : أحدها: أن النبي عليه السلام إذا جاءه الملك احتاج إلى أن يظهر الملك دليلا على كونه صادقًا - معجزة - وإلا لم يعرف الفرق بينه وبين الشيطان، فلعله طلب الإحياء والإماتة من جبريل عليه السلام ليعرف أنه صادق في كونه رسولا من الله تعالى إليه، وليطمئن قلبه على ذلك. وثانيها : روى جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه أنه قال : إن الله تعالى أوحى إليه إني إنسانًا خليلاً، وعلامته أني أحيي وأميت بدعائه، فلما ظهر من إبراهيم الظن أنواع الطاعات وقع في قلبه أنه ربما كان ذلك الإنسان أنا فطلب الإحياء والإماتة، حتى إنه إن وقع اطمأن قلبه على أن ذلك هو الخليل لا غيره. وثالثها: أن يقال : وقعت هذه الواقعة قبل النبوة. ورابعها أن يقال الغرض تكثير الدلائل ليكون العلم أبعد من الشكوك.
The fifth objection: They cling to His statement, 'Show me how You give life to the dead.' He said, 'Do you not believe?' He said, 'Yes, but so that my heart may be reassured' [2:260]. The answer: There is nothing in the verse that indicates that this reassurance is sought when he sees, and it admits of several aspects: First, that when the angel came to the prophet, he needed the angel to show a miracle as evidence of his truthfulness, otherwise the difference between him and Satan would not be known. Perhaps he asked for bringing to life and death from Gabriel to know that he was truthful in being a messenger from God to him, and so that his heart would be reassured. Second: Ja'far al-Sadiq narrated that God revealed to him, 'I have taken a human being as a friend, and his sign is that I give life and death at his prayer.' When Ibrahim showed many kinds of obedience, it occurred to his heart that perhaps that human was himself, so he asked for bringing to life and death so that if it happened, his heart would be reassured that that was the friend, not another. Third: It can be said that this event occurred before prophethood. Fourth: It can be said that the purpose was to multiply proofs so that knowledge might be further from doubts.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the doctrine of prophetic infallibility, followed by the author's refutation. The objector cites Quran 2:260, where Ibrahim asks God to show him how He revives the dead, implying that Ibrahim had doubt. The answer first denies that the verse indicates that reassurance is required only upon seeing; the verse may be interpreted in four ways. The first interpretation: When an angel comes to a prophet, the angel must perform a miracle to prove he is from God, otherwise the prophet could mistake him for Satan. Therefore, Ibrahim asked Gabriel to revive the dead as a proof of his authenticity, not because he doubted God. The second interpretation, from Ja'far al-Sadiq, is that God had told Ibrahim that He took a human as a friend, and the sign is that this friend could bring life and death. Ibrahim, suspecting that he himself might be that friend, asked to see the sign to confirm it. The third interpretation: This event occurred before Ibrahim's prophethood, so it does not imply fault. The fourth: The purpose was to increase the number of proofs, so that certain knowledge is further removed from doubts. Thus, the objection is answered in multiple ways, all preserving Ibrahim's infallibility.

Key Terms
طمأنينة
معجزة
خليل
نبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim's request indicated he had doubt in God's power to revive the dead.
Correction: Razi shows that the request was for reassurance regarding the messenger or his own status, not due to doubt in God's power.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation in the answer to the fifth objection, why did Ibrahim ask to see the dead revived?
A) Because he doubted God's power
B) To confirm that the angel Gabriel was a truthful messenger
C) To test whether God would respond
D) To show off his own power

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's request was for reassurance of the messenger or his own status, not due to any doubt in God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your faith been tested to the point that you needed a tangible sign or reassurance? Looking back, did that request indicate a weakness in your faith, or was it a step toward deeper certainty? What did you learn from Ibrahim's example about seeking confirmation without doubting God's power?
?
Have you ever mistaken a genuine spiritual experience for a satanic whisper, or vice versa? How does the need to distinguish between an angel and a messenger resonate with your own discernment between truth and deception in your spiritual life?
?
If you were in Ibrahim's position, suspecting that you might be God's beloved friend, would you have the courage to ask for a sign to confirm it? Or would fear of presumption hold you back? What does your answer reveal about your relationship with God? Now, commit to one concrete step this week to seek reassurance from God through prayer or reflection on His signs.

The Sixth Objection: Ibrahim's Prayer for His Father
Razi presents and refutes the sixth objection against Ibrahim's infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Ibrahim's infallibility against the objection that his prayer for his polytheist father was sinful.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's prayer was lawful in his time or based on hope, not sin.

The sixth objection challenges Ibrahim's prophethood by claiming his prayer for his polytheist father was a sin. The Quran condemns asking forgiveness for polytheists, yet Ibrahim did so. Razi responds by granting the premises but offering two explanations that clear Ibrahim of wrongdoing. Let's examine the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the scriptural evidence used against Ibrahim. The objector cites several Quranic verses.
Notice how the objector builds a case from verses that seem straightforward.
2
Identify the core claim
The objector argues that Ibrahim committed a sin by praying for a polytheist, since the Quran forbids that (9:113) and exempts Ibrahim's action from being followed (60:4).
3
Examine the two answers
Razi grants all premises except the absolute impermissibility. He provides two distinct answers: (1) different legal rulings across revelations, (2) Ibrahim's hope for his father's faith.
Each answer independently refutes the objection.
4
Connect to the broader principle
This shows that apparent violations of prophetic infallibility can be explained by varying laws or underlying intentions, not by sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : أن إبراهيم عليه السلام استغفر لأبيه؛ لقوله تعالى حكاية عن إبراهيم سَأَسْتَغْفِرُ لَكَ رَبِّي [مريم : ٤٧] ولقوله : واغفر لأبي إنه كان من الضالين [الشعراء : ٨٦] وأبوه كان كافرًا لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ لِأَبِيهِ آزَرَ أَتَتَّخِذُ أَصْنَامًا آلِهَةً إِنِّي أَرَاكَ وَقَوْمَكَ فِي ضَلَلٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٤] والاستغفار لأجل الكافر غير جائز ; لقوله تعالى : ما كان للنبي والذين آمنوا أن يَسْتَغْفِرُوا لِلْمُشْرِكِينَ [التوبة: ١١٣] ولقوله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ كَانَتْ لَكُمْ أُسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ فِي إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَالَّذِينَ مَعَهُ﴾ [الممتحنة : ٤] إلى قوله: ﴿إِلَّا قَوْلَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ لِأَبِيهِ لَأَسْتَغْفِرَنَّ لَكَ وَمَا أَمْلِكُ لَكَ مِنَ اللَّهِ مِنْ شَيْءٍ﴾ [الممتحنة : ٤] فأمره بالتأسي بإبراهيم إلا في هذا القول، فثبت أن هذا كان من المعصية. والجواب: كل المقدمات مسلمة إلا قوله : لا يجوز الاستغفار للكافر فإن فيه جوابين : أحدهما أن ذلك مما يختلف باختلاف الشرائع، فلعل إبراهيم لم يجد في شرعه ما يمنعه عنه، فلا جرم استغفر لأبيه، فلما منعه الله منه سكت. وثانيهما : أنه إنما استغفر لأبيه لأنه كان يتوقع منه الإيمان فلما أيس منه ترك الاستغفار، ويدل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا تَبَيَّنَ لَهُ أَنَّهُ عَدُوٌّ لِلَّهِ تَبَرَّأَ مِنْهُ﴾ [التوبة: ١١٤].
The sixth objection: That Ibrahim asked forgiveness for his father; due to God's statement narrating Ibrahim: 'I will ask forgiveness for you from my Lord' [19:47] and his statement: 'And forgive my father, for he was among the astray' [26:86]. And his father was a disbeliever due to God's statement: 'When Ibrahim said to his father Azar, 'Do you take idols as gods? I see you and your people in manifest error'' [6:74]. And asking forgiveness for the disbeliever is not permissible due to God's statement: 'It is not for the Prophet and those who believe to ask forgiveness for the polytheists' [9:113] and due to His statement: 'There has been for you an excellent example in Ibrahim and those with him' [60:4] up to His statement: 'Except for Ibrahim's statement to his father: 'I will surely ask forgiveness for you, but I have nothing for you from Allah'' [60:4]. So He commanded following Ibrahim except in this statement. Thus it is established that this was from disobedience. The answer: All premises are granted except his statement 'it is not permissible to ask forgiveness for the disbeliever', for it admits two answers: One: that this differs according to laws; perhaps Ibrahim did not find in his law what prohibited him, so he naturally asked forgiveness for his father; then when Allah prohibited him, he desisted. Second: that he only asked forgiveness for his father because he expected faith from him; when he despaired of him he stopped asking forgiveness, and His statement indicates it: 'When it became clear to him that he was an enemy of Allah, he disassociated from him' [9:114].
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection against Ibrahim's infallibility. The objector notes that Ibrahim prayed for forgiveness for his father, who was a polytheist, yet the Quran forbids asking forgiveness for polytheists (9:113) and exempts Ibrahim's prayer from being followed (60:4). Thus Ibrahim committed a sin. The author's answer grants all premises except the claim that asking forgiveness for a disbeliever is absolutely impermissible. He gives two responses. First, legal rulings differ across revealed laws; Ibrahim's law might not have prohibited it, so he acted per his law, and later when prohibited, he stopped. Second, Ibrahim prayed for his father because he hoped he would believe; once it became clear his father was an enemy of God, he stopped. This is supported by 9:114. Thus the objection is refuted without attributing sin.

Key Terms
استغفار
كافر
شرع
تبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim's action was sinful and he disobeyed.
Correction: The objection is refuted by showing that either Ibrahim's law permitted it or he only prayed hoping for his father's faith.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer to the sixth objection, why was Ibrahim's prayer for his father not a sin?
A) Because his father was not a disbeliever
B) Because his law allowed it, and he desisted when prohibited
C) Because he did not actually pray
D) Because all prophets can pray for anyone

Key Takeaway
Razi defends Ibrahim's infallibility by showing that his prayer was either lawful per his law or based on hope for his father's faith, not a sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend someone you love despite clear evidence of their wrongdoing, are you being like Ibrahim (hoping for their faith) or are you excusing sin? Ask Allah to show you the truth, even when it hurts.
?
Is there someone in your life you are still hoping will change, when all signs point to their stubbornness? Are you willing to follow Ibrahim's eventual disassociation when it becomes clear they are an enemy of Allah?
?
Think of a time you justified a questionable action because 'the context allowed it' or 'I had good intentions'. Were you sincere like Ibrahim, or were you deceiving yourself? Commit now to test your actions against Allah's clear commands, not your own desires.

Refuting the Objection from Ibrahim's Prayers
Razi refutes the objection that Ibrahim's prayers for guidance imply he was not already guided.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the prayers of Ibrahim (AS) do not imply sin or possibility of disbelief, and how the principle of prophetic infallibility resolves apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
Prophets' prayers of humility, not admissions of fault.

This lesson examines a common objection: if Ibrahim (AS) prayed for guidance and forgiveness, didn't he lack guidance and commit sin? The answer lies in a key principle: prophets cannot commit major sins. Their prayers are expressions of humility, not evidence of failure. Let's see how the scholars resolved this.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector uses three Quranic verses from Ibrahim's supplications to argue that prophets can sin or lack guidance. Read the verses carefully.
Note the logic: requesting what you already have is absurd; asking protection from sin implies possibility.
2
Examine each proof
Look at each of the three verses: 'make us Muslims', 'keep me and my sons from idolatry', 'forgive my misdeed'. Notice how the objector interprets them literally.
The objector's strength is that these are direct words of Ibrahim in the Quran.
3
Learn the answer
The response relies on consensus that prophets cannot commit disbelief after prophethood. Therefore these verses are interpreted as self-abasement and humility, not literal admissions.
This is a standard Ash'ari approach: apparent meanings that contradict essential doctrines are reinterpreted.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: How does the principle of prophetic infallibility (isma) guide interpretation? What does 'hudh an-nafs' (self-abasement) mean in this context?
This shows that theological commitments shape scriptural understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: رَبَّنَا وَاجْعَلْنَا مُسْلِمَيْنِ لَكَ [البقرة : ۱۲۸] والدعاء طلب، وطلب الحاصل محال وبقوله تعالى: وَاجْنُبْنِي وَبَنِيَّ أَنْ نَعْبُدَ الأَصْنَامَ [إبراهيم: ۳۵] ولولا جواز ذلك عليه وإلا لما طلب من الله تعالى أن يعصمه منه، وبقوله: وَالَّذِي أَطْمَعُ أَن يَغْفِرَ لِي خَطِيئَتِي يَوْمَ الدِّينِ [الشعراء: ٨٢] وهذا تصريح بإثبات الخطيئة. والجواب: لا نزاع بين الأمة أنه لا يجوز الكفر على الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - بعد نبوتهم، فكانت هذه الآية محمولة على هضم النفس، وإظهار الخضوع.
The seventh objection: They cling to His statement: 'Our Lord, make us both Muslims to You' [2:128] and supplication is a request, and requesting what is already obtained is impossible; and to His statement: 'And keep me and my sons from worshipping idols' [14:35]; for if that [i.e., idolatry] were not possible for him, he would not have asked God to protect him from it; and to His statement: 'And He who I hope will forgive me my misdeed on the Day of Judgment' [26:82]; and this is a clear admission of sin. The answer: There is no disagreement among the community that disbelief is not permissible for prophets after their prophethood. So these verses are to be interpreted as self-abasement and showing humility.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection: the Quran contains prayers from Ibrahim that seem to request what he already possesses (e.g., being Muslim) or to acknowledge sin. The objector argues that requesting what is already obtained is absurd, and asking for protection from idolatry implies it was possible, and admitting a misdeed implies sin. The author's answer states that there is consensus that prophets cannot commit disbelief after prophethood. Therefore, these verses must be interpreted as expressions of humility and self-abasement, not as literal indications of sin or possibility of disbelief. The prayers are acts of devotion, not evidence of fault.

Key Terms
هضم النفس
خضوع
خطيئة
دعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These prayers prove that prophets can sin or lack guidance.
Correction: The prayers are interpreted as acts of humility and self-abasement, not literal admissions of sin or deficiency.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi interpret Ibrahim's prayer 'Our Lord, make us both Muslims' in the answer to the seventh objection?
A) As a request for something he lacked
B) As self-abasement and humility
C) As proof that Ibrahim was not a Muslim
D) As a sign of doubt

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's prayers, though seemingly seeking guidance and forgiveness, are to be understood as acts of humility, not evidence of sin or deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever feel you are reminding Allah of your needs as if He does not know? Or do your prayers come from a place of deep humility, acknowledging that every good thing is from Him alone? How can you transform your supplications to reflect the same self-abasement (hudh an-nafs) that the prophets displayed?
?
Ibrahim asked Allah to protect him from worshiping idols. What are the 'idols' in your life—the things you fear losing more than Allah's pleasure? Are you genuinely asking Him to keep you from them, or do you secretly cling to them?
?
The objectors in this lesson twisted the prophets' words to accuse them of deficiency. Are there areas in your own life where you misuse verses or hadith to justify your own shortcomings? Will you commit to reading the Quran with the humble lens of the scholars, not as a weapon but as a mirror?

Eighth Objection: Ibrahim's Prayer for His Sons
Razi answers the objection that Ibrahim's prayer was not fully answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the prophethood of Ibrahim by interpreting the scope of his prayer and the definition of 'son' in a religious context.

Core Idea
A prophet's prayer is always answered—but only for those who are truly his in faith.

In this lesson, we address a subtle objection: Ibrahim's prayer to be kept from idolatry seems unanswered because some of his descendants worshipped idols. How can this be reconciled? Imam Razi offers two incisive arguments that clarify the meaning of 'sons' and the nature of divine response. Let's read his response carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the objection and the two-part answer. Then read the translation.
Notice the concise style of Razi's response.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The word 'sons' is general but is interpreted as specific to some sons. Why does Razi use this principle? This is a common usul al-fiqh tool.
3
Understand the second part of the answer
Razi cites the verse about Noah to show that a disbelieving child is not considered a true son. How does this strengthen the argument?
4
Reflect on the overall defense
Consider how these two arguments together protect Ibrahim's prophetic dignity. The objection is neutralized by showing the prayer is effectively answered for those who are truly his sons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : أنه قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: وَاجْنُبْنِي وَبَنِيَّ أَنْ نَعْبُدَ الأَصْنَامَ [إبراهيم: ٣٥] والله تعالى ما أجاب دعاءه في هذا المقام فكان هذا كسرًا لمصبَّه. والجواب: لفظ 'بني' وإن كان عاما إلا أنه محمول على البعض، وأيضًا الولد الكافر لا يسمى ابنا له ; لقوله تعالى في حق نوح: إِنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنْ أَهْلِكَ إِنَّهُ عَمَلٌ غَيْرُ صَالِحٍ [هود: ٤٦].
The eighth objection: That he said, 'And keep me and my sons from worshipping idols' [14:35], and God did not answer his prayer in this regard, so this was a break in his purpose? The answer: The word 'sons', although general, is carried on some [of them]; also, the disbelieving child is not called a son to him, due to God's statement concerning Noah: 'He is not of your family; he is a deed not righteous' [11:46].
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection: Ibrahim prayed that he and his sons be kept from idolatry, yet some of his descendants (like his father or others) worshipped idols, so the prayer was not fully answered, implying a weakness in Ibrahim's station. The author's answer has two parts. First, the word 'sons' in the verse is general in wording but is understood as applying only to some sons (those who remained monotheists). Second, a child who disbelieves is not considered a true son in the religious sense, as shown in the story of Noah (11:46) where God tells Noah that his drowning son is not of his family because of his unrighteous deed. Thus, the prayer is effectively answered for those who are truly his sons in faith.

Correction: The prayer is understood as applying only to believing offspring, and disbelievers are not considered true sons in the religious context.

Key Takeaway
The objection from Ibrahim's prayer is refuted by showing that the prayer is restricted to believing offspring, as faith defines true kinship.

Ya'qub's Love for Yusuf: The First Objection Answered
Razi presents and refutes the first objection against Ya'qub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Ya'qub's preferential love for Yusuf is not blameworthy, according to Imam Razi's theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Involuntary love and unforeseeable consequences absolve Ya'qub of blame.

Imam Razi now turns to the fifth story, that of Prophet Ya'qub, and presents three objections. The first objection challenges Ya'qub's apparent favoritism toward Yusuf, which led to great harm. In this lesson, we examine the objection and Razi's concise but powerful twofold answer, which highlights key theological principles about free will and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the structure: objection then answer. Notice the brevity and precision of Razi's response.
The answer has two distinct parts, separated by 'also'.
2
Analyze the objection
Identify the exact charge: Ya'qub's preference for Yusuf caused the brothers' jealousy and subsequent evil. Why is this a problem? It questions the prophet's justice and wisdom.
3
Explore the first part of the answer
Razi says 'the inclination of hearts is not within power.' This means love is involuntary, not a choice. How does this remove blame?
This connects to the Islamic doctrine that only voluntary actions are subject to moral evaluation.
4
Explore the second part of the answer
Razi adds that Ya'qub did not know the preference would lead to such great corruption. Why is ignorance of consequences relevant here?
This shows that blame requires both voluntary action and knowledge of the outcome.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Now combine both points: Ya'qub is not blameworthy because love is involuntary and he could not foresee the harm. This preserves his innocence and prophethood.
This lesson illustrates a key principle in Islamic theology: God does not hold people accountable for what is beyond their control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الخامسة : قصة يعقوب عليه السلام وفيها ثلاث شبهات : الشبهة الأولى: أن يعقوب لم رجَّح يوسف على سائر أولاده في المحبة حتى حصلت تلك المفسدة بسبب ذلك الترجيح ؟ والجواب : ميل القلوب لا يكون في القدرة وأيضًا: إنه لم يعلم أن ذلك القدر من الترجيح يتأدى إلى تلك المفسدة العظيمة.
The fifth story: The story of Ya'qub, and in it are three objections. The first objection: That Ya'qub preferred Yusuf over his other children in love until that corruption occurred because of that preference. The answer: The inclination of hearts is not within power; also, he did not know that that amount of preference would lead to that great corruption.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth story concerning Prophet Ya'qub, stating that three objections are raised. The first objection is that Ya'qub's preferential love for Yusuf caused the brothers' jealousy and subsequent evil acts (selling Yusuf into slavery). The answer is twofold. First, the inclination of the heart (love) is not under a person's control; it is involuntary, so Ya'qub is not blameworthy for feeling more love. Second, Ya'qub did not know that this degree of preference would lead to such great evil; he did not foresee the consequence, so he is not at fault. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Prophet Ya'qub sinned by showing favoritism and is therefore blameworthy for the resulting harm.
Correction: Razi clarifies that Ya'qub's love was involuntary and he could not foresee the outcome, so he is not at fault.

Checkpoint

A) Because he only pretended to love Yusuf
B) Because love is involuntary and he did not foresee the harm
C) Because all prophets are allowed to have favorites
D) Because Yusuf was older than the others

Key Takeaway
Prophet Ya'qub's love for Yusuf is not blameworthy because it was involuntary and its consequences were unforeseeable, thus preserving his moral innocence and prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defending Ya'qub: Error in Judgment vs. Error in Faith
Razi refutes the objection that the brothers' accusation of 'old error' was correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi distinguishes between religious error and ordinary mistake in judgment to defend prophets, and how he refutes the accusation of takfir using the legal nature of faith.

Core Idea
Calling a prophet 'erring' can mean a simple mistake, not religious deviation.

In this passage, Razi tackles a second objection about Prophet Ya'qub: his sons accused him of being in 'old error' (Quran 12:95). An objector might take this to mean Ya'qub was in religious error, which is impossible for a prophet. Razi clarifies that 'error' here means deviation from correct judgment—like excessive love—not error in faith. He then handles a subtle objection: if the brothers called him wrong, that would be calling a prophet a disbeliever, which would make them disbelievers. Razi responds by noting that disbelief rulings depend on divine law, not every mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's initial answer
Start by reading the objection: the brothers described Ya'qub as being in 'old error'. Then read Razi's answer: 'error' here means deviation from correctness, not religious error. Understand the distinction.
Note the Quranic verse reference: Yusuf 12:95.
2
Follow the dialectical exchange
Next, trace the hypothetical objection (la yuqalu): if the brothers described him as not correct in actions, they are calling him a disbeliever, so they themselves would be guilty of takfir. Then see Razi's response: judgment of disbelief is a legal matter; perhaps it was not disbelief in their religion, or they meant excessive love.
This is a typical kalam dialectical structure: objection and answer.
3
Reflect on the legal principle
Notice how Razi invokes the principle that rulings of disbelief (kufr) and faith (iman) are based on divine law (shar'), not purely rational judgment. This safeguards against hasty takfir.
This principle is important in Islamic theology to avoid excommunicating others based on misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن إخوة يوسف وصفوا أباهم بالضلال القديم لقولهم : لله إنك لفي ضلالك القَدِيمِ [يُوسُف : ٩٥] . والجواب : ليس المراد منه الضلال في الدين بل هو في العدول عن الصواب. لا يقال : لما وصفوه بذلك فقد وصفوه بأنه غير مصيب في أفعاله، فيلزم ذلك فقد كفر، فيلزم القول بتكفير إخوة يوسف ; لأنا نقول : الحكم بالكفر والإيمان أمر شرعي فلعل ذلك ما كان كفرًا في دينهم، أو نقول كان مرادهم وصف يعقوب بالغلو في الحب، وذلك ليس بذنب ; لأن هذا ليس في قدرته، فلم
The second objection: That the brothers of Yusuf described their father as being in old error by their statement: 'By Allah, you are indeed in your old error' [12:95]. The answer: What is meant by it is not error in religion, but rather deviation from correctness. It cannot be said: When they described him thus, they described him as not being correct in his actions, and consequently they disbelieved, which would necessitate declaring the brothers of Yusuf disbelievers; for we say: The judgment of disbelief and faith is a legal matter; perhaps that was not disbelief in their religion, or we say that their intent was to describe Ya'qub as extreme in love, and that is not a sin because it is not within his power; so...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection regarding Ya'qub: his sons accused him of being in 'old error' (Quran 12:95). The objector argues that if the brothers called him astray, then he must have been in error. The author's answer first clarifies that 'error' here does not mean religious error but deviation from correct judgment (i.e., Ya'qub's excessive love for Yusuf). Then there is a dialectical objection: if the brothers described him as not right in his actions, then they are calling him a disbeliever, which would mean they themselves disbelieve by accusing a prophet. The author responds by noting that rulings of disbelief and faith are based on specific religious laws; perhaps in their religion, such a description was not considered disbelief. Alternatively, they meant he was extreme in love, which is not a sin because it is involuntary. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
ضلال
عدول عن الصواب
تكفير
شرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the brothers' accusation proves Ya'qub was in religious error, implying a prophet can fall into religious error.
Correction: Razi clarifies that 'error' here means a mistake in judgment (like excessive love), not error in faith. Prophets are infallible in religious matters but can have human errors in judgment.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi interpret the brothers' statement 'you are in your old error'?
A) As a religious error that made Ya'qub a sinner
B) As a deviation from correct judgment, not religious error
C) As a false accusation that made the brothers disbelievers
D) As a sign that Ya'qub repented

Key Takeaway
Razi defends prophetic infallibility by showing that a simple mistake in judgment is not religious error, and that rulings of disbelief depend on divine law, not every mistake.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone accuses you of being wrong, do you immediately assume they are attacking your character or faith? How can you learn from this lesson to distinguish between a critique of your judgment and a condemnation of your beliefs? What step will you take next time you feel attacked?

What would change if you saw mistakes as opportunities for growth rather than signs of spiritual failure? Commit to one specific way you will respond to your next mistake.
?

How can you apply the lesson's nuance to ensure you don't wrongly condemn others? Before your next argument, what will you remind yourself of from this lesson?

The Seventh Objection: Prophets Seeking Forgiveness
Razi presents the seventh objection and its refutation regarding prophets' requests for forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection against prophetic infallibility based on verses of seeking forgiveness, and Razi's refutation that such verses are expressions of humility, not actual sin.

Core Idea
Prophetic requests for forgiveness are acts of humility, not admissions of sin.

This passage presents a key objection against the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (isma). The objectors cite several Quranic verses where prophets ask for forgiveness or protection from sin, arguing that such requests would be pointless if prophets were sinless. Razi refutes this by appealing to the unanimous consensus of the Muslim community that prophets are protected from disbelief after prophethood, and explains these verses as manifestations of deep humility and self-abasement before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three Quranic verses cited.
2
Identify the objection
The objection uses three verses to argue that prophets could sin. Identify each verse and the reasoning behind the objection.
Think about why the objectors think these verses imply sin.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi's answer is concise. It rests on the consensus that prophets cannot commit kufr after prophethood. The verses are interpreted as self-deprecation (hadm al-nafs) and humility.
Consider why interpreting these verses as humility makes sense doctrinally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿رَبَّنَا وَاجْعَلْنَا مُسْلِمَيْنِ لَكَ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ۱۲۸] والدعاء طلب، وطلب الحاصل محال وبقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاجْنُبْنِي وَبَنِي أَن نَعْبُدَ الأَصْنَامَ [إبراهيم: ۳۵] ولولا جواز ذلك عليه وإلا لما طلب من الله تعالى أن يعصمه منه، وبقوله: ﴿وَالَّذِي أَطْمَعُ أن يَغْفِرَ لِي خَطِيقِ يَوْمَ الدِّينِ الله [الشعراء: ٨٢ وهذا تصريح بإثبات الخطيئة. والجواب: لا نزاع بين الأمة أنه لا يجوز الكفر على الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - بعد نبوتهم، فكانت هذه الآية محمولة على هضم النفس، وإظهار الخضوع.
The seventh objection: They clung to His saying, 'Our Lord, make us both submissive to You' [Qur'an 2:128]. Supplication is a request, and requesting what is already attained is impossible. And by His saying, 'And keep me and my sons away from worshipping idols' [Qur'an 14:35]. If that were not permissible for him, he would not have asked God to protect him from it. And by His saying, 'And who I aspire that He will forgive my sin on the Day of Judgment' [Qur'an 26:82]. This is an explicit affirmation of sin. The answer: There is no dispute among the Umma that disbelief (kufr) is not permissible for prophets after their prophethood. So these verses are interpreted as self-deprecation and showing humility.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection against prophetic infallibility based on verses where prophets ask for forgiveness or protection from sin. The objectors argue that such requests imply that sin was possible or actual. Razi's refutation hinges on consensus that prophets are infallible after prophethood; thus these verses are understood as expressions of humility and self-abasement, not of actual sin.

كفر
هضم النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that prophets seeking forgiveness implies they committed sins.
Correction: The passage explains that such requests are acts of humility and self-abasement, not admissions of actual sin.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The seventh objection shows that apparent scriptural evidence against prophetic infallibility is resolved by interpreting the verses as expressions of profound humility, in line with the consensus on prophetic protection from sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on a prayer you often make (e.g., 'Guide us to the straight path') – do you truly feel lacking in guidance, or do you recite it as a habit? How can you infuse your dua with the same sincerity and self-awareness the prophets showed?

Eighth Objection: Unanswered Prayer Does Not Diminish Prophetic Status
Razi refutes the claim that an unanswered prayer undermines prophetic status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends prophetic status against the objection that an unanswered prayer implies failure, using the examples of Ibrahim and Nuh.

Core Idea
An unanswered prayer does not undermine a prophet; the request's scope is limited to the righteous.

This lesson addresses a specific objection that challenges the prophetic status of Ibrahim (AS) based on an apparently unanswered prayer. Razi provides a two-part rebuttal: first, the term 'sons' is general but contextually limited; second, a disbelieving child is not considered a true son. The passage demonstrates how theological reasoning resolves apparent contradictions in scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure: an objection followed by a two-part answer.
Notice how Razi first presents the objection clearly before dismantling it.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
Focus on Razi's first point: the word 'bani' (sons) is general in form but interpreted as particular, meaning only righteous sons are intended.
This is a common usul al-fiqh principle: العام المخصوص.
3
Examine the scriptural proof
Razi cites the verse about Nuh to support that a disbelieving child is not considered a true son. Reflect on how this Qur'anic precedent resolves the objection.
"He is not of your family" shows that spiritual kinship overrides biological ties in such contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : أنه قال [إبراهيم؟] : وَاجْنُبْنِي وَبَنِى أَن نَعْبُدَ الْأَصْنَام له [إبراهيم: ٣٥] والله تعالى ما أجاب دعاءه في هذا المقام فكان هذا كسرًا لمصبه. والجواب: لفظ " بني " وإن كان عاما إلا أنه محمول على البعض، وأيضًا الولد الكافر لا يسمى ابنا له ؛ لقوله تعالى في حق نوح : وإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنْ أَهْلِكَ إِنَّهُ عَمل غيْرُ صَلِح [هود : ٤٦].
The eighth objection: That he [Ibrahim?] said, 'Keep me and my sons away from worshipping idols' [Qur'an 14:35]. And God did not answer his prayer in this regard, so this was a break to his intention. The answer: The word 'sons' (bani), although general, is interpreted as particular. Also, a disbelieving child is not called his son, as God said regarding Nuh: 'Indeed, he is not of your family; indeed, he is [one of] righteous deed' [Qur'an 11:46].
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that God did not answer Ibrahim's prayer to protect all his sons from idolatry, implying that Ibrahim's request was invalid or his status diminished. Razi responds that the term 'sons' (bani) is general in wording but applies only to those who are righteous, as established by the verse about Nuh, where a disbelieving son is excluded from familial affiliation.

Key Terms
عصمة
ابن
كافر
عام
مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a prophet's prayer is not answered exactly as requested, the prophet must be imperfect or the request was invalid.
Correction: The prayer may be conditional (e.g., only for righteous offspring) or the terms used have a restricted meaning, as shown here. The prophet remains sinless (isma) and his prayer is answered in the best way.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why was Ibrahim's prayer to keep his sons from idolatry not apparently answered for all his descendants?
A) Ibrahim's prayer was not sincere
B) The prayer only applied to his righteous sons
C) God changed His mind
D) Ibrahim was not a prophet at that time

Key Takeaway
A prophet's prayer is always answered in the best way; apparent non-answer is due to conditional interpretation or limited scope of terms, not any deficiency in the prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's Defense of Ya'qub's Preference for Yusuf
Razi responds to the objection against Ya'qub's preference for Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet's natural preference for one child is not sinful, based on Razi's two arguments: the involuntary nature of love and the limits of prophetic knowledge.

Core Idea
Natural love is beyond human control, and prophets are not required to predict unforeseeable harm.

In this passage, Razi addresses a common objection against Prophet Ya'qub (Jacob). Critics ask: how could a prophet show favoritism to Yusuf (Joseph) at the cost of causing his other sons' jealousy and eventual wrongdoing? Razi's answer is both simple and profound. Let's examine his two-fold defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Understand the critic's question: why did Ya'qub prefer Yusuf in love, leading to great mischief?
2
Examine Razi's first defense
Razi argues that the inclination of hearts (mayl al-qulub) is not within one's ability (qudra). Love is involuntary and not subject to moral blame.
3
Consider the second defense
Razi adds that Ya'qub did not know that his degree of preference would lead to that harm. Prophets do not have inherent foreknowledge of future contingent events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الخامسة : قصة يعقوب المنية وفيها ثلاث شبهات : الشبهة الأولى: أن يعقوب لم رجّحَ يوسف على سائر أولاده في المحبة حتى حصلت تلك المفسدة بسبب ذلك الترجيح ؟ والجواب : ميل القلوب لا يكون في القدرة وأيضًا: إنه لم يعلم أن ذلك القدر من الترجيح يتأدى إلى تلك المفسدة العظيمة.
The fifth story: The story of Ya'qub [the Prophet?] and in it there are three objections: The first objection: That Ya'qub preferred Yusuf over all his other children in love until that great mischief occurred as a result of that preference? The answer: Inclination of hearts is not within one's ability. Also, he did not know that such a degree of preference would lead to that great mischief.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage from the story of Ya'qub. The objector questions why Ya'qub showed favoritism toward Yusuf, which led to his brothers' jealousy and subsequent harm. Razi gives two defenses: first, natural love is involuntary and not subject to command; second, Ya'qub did not know that his preference would result in such evil—prophets are not required to know the unseen future.

Correction: Razi explains that natural love is involuntary, and Ya'qub could not foresee the harm, so he is not blameworthy.

Checkpoint

C) He did not actually love Yusuf more
D) The other brothers were not righteous

Key Takeaway
Ya'qub's preference for Yusuf was not a sin because love is involuntary and the future consequences were hidden from him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If natural feelings are beyond your control, what does that mean for your relationship with Allah? Are you grateful for the love He placed in your heart, even if it leads to pain? Commit to thanking Allah for a feeling you once resented.

Accusation of Old Error in Yusuf's Story
Razi answers an objection from Yusuf's brothers' accusation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the infallibility of prophets by reinterpreting the accusation of 'old error' as a misjudgment rather than religious error.

Core Idea
Accusing a prophet of error is not kufr if the error is non-religious misjudgment.

In this passage, Razi tackles a challenge from the story of Yusuf: the brothers accused Ya'qub of being in 'old error.' If prophets are infallible, how could this be? Razi carefully distinguishes between religious error and mere mistakes in judgment, showing that the brothers' words do not violate prophetic infallibility. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the brothers' statement in the Qur'an (12:95) and Razi's initial answer.
Notice the wording: 'By Allah, indeed you are in your old error.'
2
Understand Razi's first answer
Razi says 'dalal' here means turning away from correctness, not error in religion.
3
Consider the potential implication
If it were a sin, the brothers would have disbelieved by accusing a prophet. Razi addresses this.
This is a hypothetical objection Razi raises himself.
4
Examine the two responses
Razi gives two reasons: either their law did not consider it kufr, or they meant excessive love, which is not a sin.
Both preserve Ya'qub's infallibility.
5
Reflect on prophetic infallibility
Consider how this interpretation maintains the doctrine of isma.
The key is distinguishing types of error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن إخوة يوسف الوصفوا أباهم بالضلال القديم لقولهم : لله إنك لفي ضلالك القَدِيمِ ﴾ [يُوسُف : ٩٥] . والجواب : ليس المراد منه الضلال في الدين بل هو في العدول عن الصواب. لا يقال : لما وصفوه بذلك فقد وصفوه بأنه غير مصيب في أفعله ، في الرسول ذلك فقد كفر، فيلزم القول بتكفير إخوة يوسف ؛ لأنا نقول : الحكم بالكفر والإيمان أمر شرعي فلعل ذلك ما كان كفرًا في دينهم، أو نقول كان مرادهم وصف يعقوب بالغلو في الحب، وذلك ليس بذنب ؛ لأن هذا ليس في قدرته، فلم يكن وصفهم إياه بذلك قدحًا منهم في عصمته.
The second objection: That Yusuf's brothers described their father as being in 'old error' by their saying, 'By Allah, indeed you are in your old error' [Qur'an 12:95]. The answer: The intended meaning is not error in religion, but rather turning away from correctness. It cannot be said: When they described him thus, they described him as not being correct in his actions, and if that is a sin then they have disbelieved, so one must say that Yusuf's brothers are disbelievers. For we say: The judgment of disbelief (kufr) and belief (iman) is a legal matter, so perhaps that was not disbelief in their religion. Or we say: Their intention was to describe Ya'qub as excessive in love, and that is not a sin because it is not within his ability. So their describing him thus was not an attack on his infallibility (isma).
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Yusuf's brothers accused Ya'qub of being in 'old error' (dalal), which might imply a sin. Razi clarifies that 'dalal' here means being mistaken in judgment, not in religion. He then anticipates an objection: if the brothers accused him of a sin, they would be disbelievers. Razi responses with two possibilities: either their accusation was not considered disbelief in their law, or they were referring to excessive love, which is not a sin as it is beyond control.

Key Terms
ضلال
عصمة
كفر
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'dalal' in the Qur'an always means religious error or unbelief.
Correction: Razi shows that 'dalal' can mean a simple mistake in judgment, as in the context of Ya'qub's story.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what did the brothers mean by calling Ya'qub 'in old error'?
A) He was an unbeliever
B) He was mistaken in his opinion
C) He had committed a major sin
D) They were lying

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the brothers' accusation refers to a mistake in judgment, not religion, and does not undermine prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'ضلال' (dalal) to describe someone's actions, do you ever pause to consider whether you are labeling a religious error or simply a mistake in judgment? How could this awareness change the way you speak about others' faults?
?
Think of someone in your life whose deep emotion you have misjudged as a flaw. What would it mean for you to offer them the same charitable interpretation Razi gave to the brothers' statement?
?
Razi reminds us that the judgment of kufr and iman is Allah's legal domain, not ours. Is there a person or group you have mentally labeled as 'disbelievers' due to a single statement or action? How can you entrust judgment to Allah and focus on your own heart's accountability?
