Ya'qub's Grief and Prophetic Patience
Razi defends Ya'qub's intense grief as permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ya'qub's intense grief over Yusuf was not a sin, based on the distinction between recommended and obligatory patience.

Core Idea
Recommended patience is not mandatory; showing grief is not a sin.

The story of Ya'qub weeping until his eyes turned white raises a question: should prophets remain completely stoic? Razi addresses this objection by clarifying that patience is recommended, not obligatory, and that Ya'qub actually concealed most of his sorrow. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection against Ya'qub
Read the objection that Ya'qub's weeping was excessive and unbecoming of a prophet known for patience.
2
Understand Razi's first answer
Razi says endurance (tajallud) is recommended (mandub), not obligatory. Leaving a recommended act is not a sin.
This shows prophets are not required to be completely stoic.
3
Understand Razi's second answer
Ya'qub only showed a little of his grief; he concealed more. So his visible grief was minimal.
This addresses the claim of 'excess'.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
The key is the difference between wajib and mandub. Not achieving a recommended level is not a sin.
This protects the sinlessness of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لم أسرف في البكاء حتى ابيضت عيناه من الحزن، ومن شأن الأنبياء التجلد والتصبر ؟ والجواب : التجلد على المصائب مندوب إليه، وليس بواجب، وترك المندوب ليس بمعصية وأيضا : فلأنه إنما كان يظهر من حزنه شيئًا قليلاً، والذي كان يخفيه كان أكثر ذلك.
The third objection: Why did he [Ya'qub] go to excess in weeping until his eyes turned white from grief, while prophets are characterized by firmness and patience? The answer: Endurance (tajallud) in calamities is recommended (mandub), not obligatory, and leaving a recommended act is not a sin. Also, he only showed a little of his grief, and what he concealed was more than that.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents an objection that Ya'qub's extreme grief and weeping over the loss of Yusuf was unbecoming of a prophet, who should be patient. Razi's defense is twofold: first, the recommended level of patience is not mandatory, and failing to attain it is not a sin; second, what was visible of his grief was minimal—he actually suppressed most of his sorrow.

Key Terms
تجلد
صبر
مندوب
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets must never show any emotion or grief, and any display of sorrow is sinful.
Correction: Razi explains that patience is recommended, not obligatory, so showing grief is not a sin. Prophets are human and can feel emotions as long as they do not commit sin.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say Ya'qub's weeping was not sinful?
A) He was commanded to weep
B) Patience is only recommended, not obligatory
C) He faked his grief
D) The weeping was a miracle

Key Takeaway
Ya'qub's grief was not sinful because patience is recommended, not obligatory, and he concealed most of his sorrow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a deep loss, do you suppress your grief because you think showing emotion is weak or a sign of weak faith? How would your heart change if you accepted that patience is recommended, not obligatory, and your tears do not diminish your trust in Allah? What will you do the next time grief strikes?
?
Think of a person you have observed struggling with a calamity. Have you ever silently judged them for not being 'patient enough'? After learning that endurance is recommended and not obligatory, will you now reconsider that judgment? What specific thought will you replace it with?
?
Ya'qub concealed most of his grief. Do you tend to display more or less of your inner sorrow than is healthy? Are you hiding pain from Allah or from people? This lesson affirms that some concealment is fine, but are you hiding it from the One who already knows? What step will you take today to be honest with yourself and with Allah about your true state?

Why Yusuf Accepted Slavery: Three Prophetic Considerations
Razi provides three justifications for Yusuf's silence during slavery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational explanations Imam Razi offers for why Prophet Yusuf (AS) did not reveal his identity while enslaved.

Core Idea
Prophetic silence in hardship may reflect divine wisdom, not weakness.

The story of Prophet Yusuf (AS) being sold into slavery raises a question: why did a prophet of God accept such a degrading situation without declaring his true status? Imam Razi provides three possible answers that protect the integrity of prophethood while acknowledging the reality of the situation. This lesson explores those answers and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that Razi introduces the objection before giving three distinct answers.
The three answers are introduced with 'أحدها', 'ثانيها', 'ثالثها'.
2
Identify the three reasons
List the three reasons Razi gives: (1) Yusuf may not have been a prophet at that time, (2) the law of that time may have required silence, (3) he may have spoken but been ignored.
Each reason is a complete defense of Yusuf's action.
3
Reflect on the principle of differing laws
Consider the second reason: that showing freedom can differ across divine laws. This shows that prophetic actions are judged by their own context, not by later standards.
This principle is important in defending prophets against anachronistic criticism.
4
Connect to prophethood timing
The first reason implies that prophethood is not necessarily lifelong from birth. Yusuf may have become a prophet after the slavery period.
This protects the concept that prophets are infallible only after receiving revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة السادسة : قصة يوسف العلي وفيها سبع شبهات : الشبهة الأولى : لم صبر على العبودية، ولم يشرح للقوم حال نفسه ؟ والجواب من وجوه : أحدها : لعله ما كان نبيا في ذلك الوقت، فلما خاف على نفسه من القتل صبر على الرق. وثانيها : أن إظهار الحرية أمر يجوز أن يختلف باختلاف الشرائع، فلعل الله أمره بالسكوت عنه في ذلك الوقت كما امتحن إبراهيم وإسماعيل بالنار والذبح. وثالثها : لعله شرح لهم ذلك لكنهم لم يلتفتوا إليه.
The sixth story: The story of Yusuf the High, and in it there are seven objections: The first objection: Why did he bear slavery and not explain his situation to the people? The answer has several aspects: One: Perhaps he was not a prophet at that time, so when he feared for his life from being killed, he accepted slavery. Two: That showing freedom is something that may differ according to different laws (shara'i'), so perhaps God commanded him to remain silent at that time, just as He tested Ibrahim and Isma'il with fire and sacrifice. Three: Perhaps he explained to them but they did not pay attention.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Yusuf, while a prophet, remained silent about his true identity when sold into slavery. Razi offers three possible justifications: (1) Yusuf might not yet have been a prophet at that stage of his life; (2) the legal ruling of his time may have required concealment; (3) he may have tried to explain but was ignored.

Key Terms

نبوة
شرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Yusuf's silence during slavery implies that he accepted injustice or failed in his prophetic duty.
Correction: Razi shows that there are three valid reasons for his silence, including that he may not have been a prophet at that time, or that God commanded it, or that he was ignored.

Checkpoint

A) He was not a prophet at that time
B) He was commanded by God to remain silent
C) He did not know he was a prophet

Key Takeaway
Prophet Yusuf's silence during slavery can be explained by three plausible reasons that uphold the integrity of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are misunderstood or mistreated, do you assume that silence means you are weak, or can you pause to consider that Allah may have a hidden wisdom in your quietness? What is one situation this week where you can choose to trust Allah's plan instead of defending yourself?
?
Yusuf could have insisted on his freedom but remained silent. Is there an area of your life where you are clinging to your 'rights' or reputation, perhaps even against what Allah has commanded you today? What would it cost you to let go and obey Him in silence?
?
Razi says that obligations can differ by time and law. Ask yourself: Are you following Allah's commands as they apply to you now, or are you judging yourself and others by standards that may not be from this time? What would change if you truly embraced that Allah's wisdom is specific to each moment?

Four Testimonies of Yusuf's Innocence
Razi cites four testimonies to Yusuf's innocence from the Qur'anic story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends the prophetic innocence of Yusuf (AS) by citing multiple testimonies from the Qur'anic narrative.

Core Idea
Four Qur'anic witnesses clear Yusuf from every suspicion.

Imam al-Razi addresses the second objection against prophethood, which uses the Yusuf and Zulaykha story to suggest that Yusuf sinned. He responds by assembling four distinct testimonies from the same story — each one attesting to Yusuf's purity. The passage is a model of how internal Qur'anic evidence resolves doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Razi begins by stating the objection, then gives a general answer before listing four testimonies.
The passage is concise but packed with Qur'anic references.
2
Identify the four testimonies
Identify each of the four testimonies: the husband, the witness from the family, the women of Egypt, and Yusuf himself. Note the different Qur'anic verses cited for each.
Each testimony comes from a different source, strengthening the proof.
3
Reflect on the argumentative strategy
Consider why Razi uses multiple testimonies. How does this multiplicity refute the objection? The answer: it shows that all parties involved in the event confirmed Yusuf's innocence.
This is a standard kalam method: rebutting doubt with cumulative evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : قصته مع زليخا وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ هَمَّتْ بِهِ وَهَمَّ بِهَا لَوْلَا ان را بُرْهَانَ رَبِّهِ ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٤]. والجواب : أن كل من له تعلق بتلك الواقعة قد شهد على طهارة يوسف وبراءته عن التهمة. فأحدها : أن زوج المرأة شهد بذلك ، فقال : إِنَّهُ مِن كَيْدِكُنَّ إِنَّ كَيْدَكُنَّ عَظِيمٌ يُوسُفُ أَشْرِضْ عَنْ هَذَا وَاسْتَغْفِرِى لِذَنْبِكِ إِنَّكِ كُنتِ مِنَ الْخَاطِعِينَ ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٩ - ٢٨] وثانيها : الشهود قال الله تعالى : ﴿وَشَهِدَ شَاهِدٌ مِنْ أَهْلِهَا [يُوسُف : ٢٦] . وثالثها: قول نساء مصر : حَشَ لِلَّهِ مَا عَلِمْنَا عَلَيْهِ مِن سوء [يُوسُف : ٥١] ورابعها: قول يوسف ال : وهِيَ رَوَدَتْنِي عَن نَّفْسِي [يُوسُف : ٢٦] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿رَبَ السِّجْنُ أَحَبُّ إِلَى مِمَّا يَدْعُونَنِي إِلَيْهِ ﴾ [يُوسُف : ٣٣] وفي أية أخرى : وذلك علَمَ أَنِّي لَمْ أَخُنْهُ بِالْغَيْبِ [يُوسُف : ٥٢] فلو قصد المعصية لكان قد خانه بالغيب.
The second objection: His story with Zulaykha, which is His saying: 'And she certainly desired him, and he would have desired her if he had not seen the proof of his Lord' [Qur'an 12:24]. The answer: Everyone connected to that incident witnessed Yusuf's purity and innocence from the accusation. One of them: The woman's husband testified to that, saying: 'Indeed, it is of your plotting; indeed your plotting is great. Yusuf, turn away from this, and ask forgiveness for your sin; indeed you are of the sinners' [Qur'an 12:28-29]. Two: The witnesses - God said: 'And a witness from her family testified' [Qur'an 12:26]. Three: The statement of the women of Egypt: 'God forbid, we know no evil against him' [Qur'an 12:51]. Four: The statement of Yusuf: 'She sought to seduce me' [Qur'an 12:26] and in another verse: 'My Lord, prison is more beloved to me than that to which they invite me' [Qur'an 12:33] and in another verse: 'That was that he might know that I did not betray him in [his] absence' [Qur'an 12:52]. So had he intended disobedience, he would have betrayed him in his absence.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage refutes any suspicion of sin against Yusuf in the incident with Zulaykha (Potiphar's wife). Razi lists four lines of testimony that confirm his innocence: (1) the husband of the woman declared Yusuf's innocence and blamed the women; (2) a witness from her own family spoke; (3) the women of Egypt testified that they knew no evil of him; and (4) Yusuf's own statements throughout the narrative show his purity and his choice of prison over sin. The final remark: if he had intended sin, he would have betrayed his master, but he did not.

Key Terms
عصمة
تهمة
شهادة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic phrase 'and he would have desired her' (12:24) implies that Yusuf (AS) actually intended to sin.
Correction: The desire was conditional and prevented by divine proof; the context and multiple testimonies show Yusuf's innocence. Razi's answer uses the very same story's witnesses to prove purity.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Yusuf's innocence is established by multiple internal witnesses from the Qur'an itself, leaving no room for doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When people speak about you behind your back, do you have a track record and witnesses that would testify to your integrity, like Yusuf had? What are you doing today to build such a reputation?
?
Yusuf chose prison over sin. Is there a 'prison' you are avoiding that might actually be better for your soul than the 'freedom' of disobedience? What is holding you back from choosing the harder but purer path?
?
If you were falsely accused tomorrow, would your first reaction be to defend yourself or to trust that Allah will bring out the truth through others? How can you cultivate the same calm faith Yusuf displayed?

Prophet's Grief: Recommended vs Obligatory Patience
Razi answers an objection about a prophet's excessive weeping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet's display of grief is not sinful, based on the distinction between recommended and obligatory actions.

Core Idea
Endurance in calamities is recommended, not obligatory.

This passage presents a question about the limits of a prophet's emotional expression. The objector argues that prophets must be firm and patient, but Razi's answer clarifies that patience in such situations is recommended, not obligatory—and therefore, not showing it is not a sin. This distinction helps us understand the human aspects of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the third objection: it questions why a prophet wept excessively until his eyes turned white, given that prophets are supposed to be firm and patient.
Notice the objector's assumption: that a prophet must always show patience.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The answer states that being firm (tajallud) in calamities is recommended (mandub), not obligatory (wajib). Therefore, leaving it is not a sin.
This is the key doctrinal point: not all praiseworthy acts are obligatory.
3
Consider the second part of the answer
Razi adds that the prophet only showed a small amount of grief outwardly; his hidden grief was greater. So even the outward display was minimal.
This reinforces that the prophet's behavior was not excessive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لم أسرف في البكاء حتى ابيضت عيناه من الحزن، ومن شأن الأنبياء التجلد والتصبر ؟ والجواب : التجلد على المصائب مندوب إليه، وليس بواجب، وترك المندوب ليس بمعصية وأيضا : فلأنه إنما كان يظهر من حزنه شيئًا قليلاً، والذي كان يخفيه كان أكثر ذلك.
The third objection: Why did he indulge in weeping until his eyes turned white from grief, while the character of prophets is to be firm and patient? The answer: Being firm in calamities is recommended, not obligatory, and leaving what is recommended is not a sin. Also, because he only showed a small part of his grief, while what he concealed was more than that.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The objector questions why a prophet would weep so much that his eyes turned white, claiming that prophets should be patient. The answer distinguishes between wajib (obligatory) and mandub (recommended). Patience in calamities is recommended, not obligatory, so not doing it is not a sin. Furthermore, the prophet only showed a small amount of grief; his hidden grief was greater. So his outward display was minimal and acceptable.

Key Terms
التجلد
التصبر
مندوب
واجب
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets must never show grief or sadness because they must be patient at all times.
Correction: Patience in calamities is recommended, not obligatory. Showing some grief is not sinful, especially if it is minimal and does not indicate lack of faith.

Checkpoint
Why is the prophet's weeping not considered a sin according to the answer?
A) Weeping is forbidden for prophets
B) He was forced to weep
C) Endurance is recommended not obligatory
D) He repented

Key Takeaway
A prophet's display of grief is not sinful because endurance in calamities is recommended, not obligatory, and the prophet showed only minimal outward grief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity, do you feel guilty if you show grief openly? How does knowing that endurance is recommended, not obligatory, free you from that guilt? Will you now allow yourself to grieve without accusing yourself of weak faith?
?
The prophet concealed most of his grief. What grief are you hiding from others? Are you putting up a false front of complete composure? What would it look like to be honest about your pain without sinning?
?
The objection assumed prophets must be perfectly composed. In your life, which area do you assume perfection is required when it is only recommended? How does that assumption burden you, and what would change if you accepted the recommended as recommended?

Razi's Three Answers to Yusuf's Silence
Razi gives three answers to the objection about Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three plausible reasons why Prophet Yusuf endured slavery without revealing his true status, as argued by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Yusuf's silence had valid reasons; prophethood is not always immediate.

The objection that Yusuf should have spoken up seems strong, but Imam Razi shows it proves nothing. He offers three distinct possibilities, each grounded in reason and scripture. This passage teaches us to consider context and divine wisdom before questioning the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi first states the objection, then presents three answers.
The passage is concise; each answer is a separate possibility.
2
Examine each answer
For each of the three answers, identify the key reasoning: timing of prophethood, variation in divine laws, and possibility of unheeded explanation.
Compare with the stories of Ibrahim and Isma'il mentioned in the second answer.
3
Reflect on the apologetic method
Consider how multiple plausible defenses protect the prophet's integrity. This is a common kalam technique to refute objections.
The objection is not decisive because alternative explanations exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : لم صبر على العبودية، ولم يشرح للقوم حال نفسه ؟ والجواب من وجوه : أحدها : لعله ما كان نبيا في ذلك الوقت، فلما خاف على نفسه من القتل صبر على الرق. وثانيها : أن إظهار الحرية أمر يجوز أن يختلف باختلاف الشرائع، فلعل الله أمره بالسكوت عنه في ذلك الوقت كما امتحن إبراهيم وإسماعيل بالنار والذبح. وثالثها : لعله شرح لهم ذلك لكنهم لم يلتفتوا إليه.
The first objection: Why did he endure slavery and not explain his situation to the people? The answer has several aspects: First, perhaps he was not a prophet at that time, so when he feared being killed, he endured slavery. Second, that revealing freedom is something that may vary according to different laws (shara'i'), so perhaps God commanded him to remain silent about it at that time, just as He tested Ibrahim and Isma'il with fire and sacrifice. Third, perhaps he did explain to them but they paid no attention.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer with multiple points. Razi offers three possible reasons why Yusuf did not reveal he was freeborn or a prophet. Each reason is plausible: (1) temporal context—he wasn't yet a prophet; (2) difference in divine laws—God may have commanded silence; (3) he actually did explain but was ignored. This shows that the objection is not valid.

Key Terms
العبودية
الرق
الشرائع
النبي
امتحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Yusuf was a prophet from birth and should have acted like one.
Correction: Prophethood can be granted later; at the time of slavery, Yusuf may not yet have been a prophet.

Checkpoint

A) He was not yet a prophet
B) God commanded him to remain silent
C) He explained but was not listened to
D) He was too weak to speak

Key Takeaway
Razi's three answers show that Yusuf's silence is not a valid argument against his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you endured a difficult situation without revealing your true identity or status? Were you afraid, or were you obeying a command from Allah? How can Yusuf's example teach you to trust Allah's timing and wisdom even when others misunderstand you?
?
The second answer says that God may command silence in one situation but not in another. Are you willing to obey Allah even when His commands seem to go against what people expect of you? What situation in your life right now might require such silent obedience?
?
Yusuf's explanation may have been ignored. When you share the truth with others and they pay no attention, do you become discouraged? How does the possibility that Yusuf's words were ignored affect your patience in da'wah?

Seven witnesses of Yusuf's innocence
Razi enumerates seven witnesses and refutes the objection against Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses seven Quranic testimonies to refute the accusation that Prophet Yusuf intended sin with Zulaykha.

Core Idea
Seven witnesses, from humans to God and Iblis, affirm Yusuf's purity.

This passage addresses a common objection against the prophethood of Yusuf, based on the verse 'she intended him and he would have intended her'. Razi counters by listing seven distinct testimonies from the Quranic story, each declaring Yusuf's innocence. The cumulative evidence is so overwhelming that even the accuser Zulaykha and Iblis himself are forced to admit his purity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Razi first states the objection, then immediately lists seven witnesses in a numbered fashion.
The objection is from 'ahl al-hashw' who misinterpret the verse 'wa hamma biha'.
2
Identify each witness
Go through the list of seven witnesses one by one. For each, note who they are and what they said or did. This will help you appreciate the breadth of testimony.
The witnesses are: husband, family witness, Egyptian women, Yusuf himself, Zulaykha, God, and Iblis.
3
Understand the cumulative argument
Reflect on how the seven testimonies build a case from multiple angles: human testimony, personal confession, divine declaration, and even the devil's admission. This shows the objection is baseless.
Razi concludes that the criticism is false from every angle.
4
Analyze the rhetorical conclusion
Examine the final lines where Razi addresses the 'ahl al-hashw'. He presents a dichotomy: if they follow God, they must accept His testimony; if they follow Iblis, they must accept Iblis's word. This cleverly forces the reader to choose sides.
The term 'ahl al-hashw' refers to ignorant literalists who reject rational interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : قصته مع زليخا وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ هَمَّتْ بِهِ وَهَمَّ بِهَا لَوْلَا أَن رَّأَى بُرْهَانَ رَبِّهِ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٤]. والجواب : أن كل من له تعلق بتلك الواقعة قد شهد على طهارة يوسف وبراءته عن التهمة. فأحدها : أن زوج المرأة شهد بذلك ، فقال : ﴿إِنَّهُ مِن كَيْدِكُنَّ إِنَّ كَيْدَكُنَّ عَظِيمٌ يُوسُفُ أَعْرِضْ عَنْ هَذَا وَاسْتَغْفِرِى لِذَنْبِكِ إِنَّكِ كُنتِ مِنَ الْخَاطِعِينَ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٩ - ٢٨]. وثانيها : الشاهد قال الله تعالى : ﴿وَشَهِدَ شَاهِدٌ مِنْ أَهْلِهَا﴾ [يُوسُف : ٢٦]. وثالثها: قول نساء مصر : ﴿حَاشَ لِلَّهِ مَا عَلِمْنَا عَلَيْهِ مِن سوء﴾ [يُوسُف : ٥١]. ورابعها: قول يوسف عليه السلام : ﴿هِيَ رَوَدَتْنِي عَن نَّفْسِي﴾ [يُوسُف : ٢٦] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿رَبَّ السِّجْنُ أَحَبُّ إِلَيَّ مِمَّا يَدْعُونَنِي إِلَيْهِ﴾ [يُوسُف : ٣٣] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿ذَلِكَ لِيَعْلَمَ أَنِّي لَمْ أَخُنْهُ بِالْغَيْبِ﴾ [يُوسُف : ٥٢] فلو قصد المعصية لكان قد خانه بالغيب. وخامسها : اعتراف زليخا بذلك : ﴿وَلَقَدْ رَوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ فَاسْتَعْصَمَ﴾ [يوسف: ٣٢] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿الْآنَ حَصْحَصَ الْحَقُّ أَنَا رَوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ، وَإِنَّهُ لَمِنَ الصَّدِقِينَ﴾ [يوسف: ٥١]. وسادسها : شهادة رب السماوات والأرض بذلك وهي قوله : ﴿وَرَوَدَتْهُ الَّتِي هُوَ فِي بَيْتِهَا عَن نَّفْسِهِ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٣] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿كَذَلِكَ لِنَصْرِفَ عَنْهُ السُّوءَ وَالْفَحْشَاءَ إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يُوسُف : ٢٤]. وسابعها : إقرار إبليس بأن يوسف كان مبراً عن تلك التهمة : لأنه قال ﴿فَعِزَّتِكَ لَأُغْوِيَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ إِلَّا عِبَادَكَ مِنْهُمُ الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [ص: ٨٢ - ٨٣] فأقر أنه لا تعلق له بالمخلص، والله تعالى شهد بأن يوسف كان من المخلصين كما قال: ﴿إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٤]. فهذا الطعن الذي قاله أهل الحشو باطل من كل الوجوه، فإن كان الحشو على دين الله وجب عليه أن يقبل قول الله وشهادته وإن كان على دين إبليس وجب عليه أن يقبل قول إبليس.
The second objection: His story with Zulaykha, namely the Almighty's saying: 'And she certainly intended him, and he would have intended her, if not that he saw the proof of his Lord' [Yusuf: 24]. The answer: Everyone connected to that incident testified to Yusuf's purity and innocence of the accusation. First: the woman's husband testified to that, saying: 'Indeed, it is of your plotting. Indeed, your plotting is great. Yusuf, turn away from this, and ask forgiveness for your sin. Indeed, you were of the sinners' [Yusuf: 28-29]. Second: the witness — Allah said: 'And a witness from her family testified' [Yusuf: 26]. Third: the saying of the women of Egypt: 'Far be it from Allah! We have not known any evil upon him' [Yusuf: 51]. Fourth: the saying of Yusuf, peace be upon him: 'She sought to seduce me from myself' [Yusuf: 26], and in another verse: 'O my Lord, prison is more beloved to me than that to which they invite me' [Yusuf: 33], and in another verse: 'That was so he might know that I did not betray him in [his] absence' [Yusuf: 52]; if he intended sin, he would have betrayed him in absence. Fifth: Zulaykha's confession to that: 'And certainly she sought to seduce him, but he firmly refused' [Yusuf: 32], and in another verse: 'Now the truth has become clear. I sought to seduce him, and indeed he is of the truthful' [Yusuf: 51]. Sixth: the testimony of the Lord of the heavens and the earth to that, namely His saying: 'And she sought to seduce him from himself' [Yusuf: 23], and in another verse: 'Thus it was that We might turn away from him evil and immorality. Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants' [Yusuf: 24]. Seventh: Iblis's acknowledgment that Yusuf was innocent of that accusation, because he said: 'By Your might, I will surely mislead them all, except Your chosen servants among them' [Sad: 82-83]; so he admitted that he has no power over the chosen ones, and Allah testified that Yusuf was among the chosen ones as He said: 'Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants' [Yusuf: 24]. So this criticism that the people of hashw (ignorant anthropomorphists) made is false from every angle. If the hashwi follows God's religion, he must accept God's word and testimony; if he follows Iblis's religion, he must accept Iblis's word.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection using multiple testimonies. Razi lists seven witnesses, each from the Quranic story, building a cumulative case for Yusuf's innocence. The first three are human testimonies; the fourth is Yusuf's own statement; the fifth is Zulaykha's confession; the sixth is God's own testimony; the seventh is even Iblis's admission. The conclusion is powerful: the accusation comes from 'ahl al-hashw' (ignorant literalists), and their argument is refuted by all these witnesses. The final remark is rhetorical: if the hashwi is a true believer, he should accept God's testimony; if he follows Iblis, he should accept Iblis's word—implying that rejecting these testimonies aligns one with Iblis.

الشبهة
الهم
البرهان
الشهادة
أهل الحشو
المخلصين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Prophet Yusuf actually intended to commit sin with Zulaykha, based on the phrase 'wa hamma biha' in the verse.
Correction: The seven witnesses, including God and even Iblis, affirm Yusuf's purity. His 'intention' was a natural human inclination that was immediately overcome by divine proof, not a sinful resolve.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that all parties in the story, from humans to God and Iblis, testified to Yusuf's innocence, making the accusation false from every angle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Yusuf's innocence was witnessed by every party in the story, even by the one who tried to seduce him and by Iblis himself. Think of the last time you defended someone's honour. Did your testimony match what you truly knew, or did you stay silent out of fear? Write down the name of one person whose reputation you can restore this week, and commit to speaking up.
?
God Himself testified that Yusuf was among His chosen servants. Yet you live as if your own reputation depends on what others think, not on God's witness. What if you knew that God has already testified about your character in the heavens? Would you still live the same way today?
?
Even Iblis had to admit defeat regarding Yusuf. What area of your life do you keep hidden because you know your enemy could use it against you? Sit with that question until you are honest enough to write it down, and then ask God to make you one of His mukhlasin.

Three Divine Proofs of Yusuf's Innocence
Imam Razi enumerates three proofs of Yusuf's innocence from Quran and Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs from Quran and Iblis that establish the Prophet Yusuf's innocence from sin.

Core Idea
Divine testimony, confession, and even Iblis affirm Yusuf's purity.

Imam Razi presents three powerful proofs that the Prophet Yusuf was innocent of any sin with Zulaykha. These proofs come from Zulaykha's own confession, Allah's testimony, and even the admission of Iblis. Together, they form an irrefutable case for Yusuf's moral purity.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage listing the fifth, sixth, and seventh proofs. Notice how each proof is grounded in a Quranic verse.
The proofs are presented in order: Zulaykha, Allah, Iblis.
2
Understand Zulaykha's confession
Zulaykha admits she desired Yusuf and that he remained chaste. This is a strong witness against her own claim.
3
Understand Allah's testimony
Allah declares that He turned away evil from Yusuf and that Yusuf is among His chosen servants (mukhlasin).
The term 'mukhlasin' means chosen and protected from sin.
4
Understand Iblis's admission
Iblis states he cannot mislead Allah's chosen servants. Since Allah declared Yusuf chosen, Iblis cannot lead him into sin.
This is a proof by the enemy's own admission.
5
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how these three independent sources all point to Yusuf's innocence. Why is this stronger than a single proof?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : اعتراف زليخا بذلك : ﴿وَلَقَدْ رَاوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ فَاسْتَعْصَمَ﴾ [يوسف: 32] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿الْآنَ حَصْحَصَ الْحَقُّ أَنَا رَاوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ وَإِنَّهُ لَمِنَ الصَّادِقِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 51]. وسادسها : شهادة رب السماوات والأرض بذلك وهي قوله : ﴿وَرَاوَدَتْهُ الَّتِي هُوَ فِي بَيْتِهَا عَن نَّفْسِهِ﴾ [يوسف: 23] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿كَذَلِكَ لِنَصْرِفَ عَنْهُ السُّوءَ وَالْفَحْشَاءَ إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 24]. وسابعها : إقرار إبليس بأن يوسف كان مبرأً عن تلك التهمة لأنه قال : ﴿فَبِعِزَّتِكَ لَأُغْوِيَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ إِلَّا عِبَادَكَ مِنْهُمُ الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [ص: 82-83] فأقر أنه لا تعلق له بالمخلصين، والله تعالى شهد بأن يوسف كان من المخلصين كما قال: ﴿إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 24]. فهذا الطعن الذي قاله أهل الحشو باطل من كل الوجوه، فإن كان الحشو على دين الله وجب عليه أن يقبل قول الله وشهادته وإن كان على دين إبليس وجب عليه أن يقبل قول إبليس.
And the fifth: the confession of Zulaykha to that: 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her if it were not that he saw the proof of his Lord...' and in another verse: 'Now the truth has become manifest: I did desire him, and indeed he is of the truthful.' And the sixth: the testimony of the Lord of the heavens and the earth to that, which is His saying: 'And she, in whose house he was, desired him...' and in another verse: 'Thus We turned away from him evil and immorality. Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' And the seventh: the admission of Iblis that Yusuf was innocent of that accusation, because he said: 'By Your might, I will surely mislead them all, except Your chosen servants among them.' So he admitted that he has no influence over the chosen ones, and Allah the Exalted testified that Yusuf was among the chosen ones, as He said: 'Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' So this accusation that the Hashwiyya made is false from all aspects. For if the Hashwi is upon the religion of Allah, he must accept the word of Allah and His testimony; and if he is upon the religion of Iblis, he must accept the word of Iblis.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof and refutation. Imam Razi presents three of the seven proofs (the fifth, sixth, and seventh) that the Prophet Yusuf was innocent of sinning with Zulaykha. The fifth proof is Zulaykha's own confession: in one verse she admits she desired him and he remained chaste, in another she confesses she was the one who desired him. The sixth proof is Allah's own testimony that He turned away evil and immorality from Yusuf and that Yusuf was among His chosen servants (mukhlasin). The seventh proof is the admission of Iblis, who declared he cannot mislead Allah's chosen servants, and Allah declared Yusuf among them. After listing these proofs, Razi refutes the Hashwiyya, a group that claimed Yusuf committed sin. He argues that the accusation is false from all angles: if the Hashwi follows Allah's religion, he must accept Allah's testimony; if he follows Iblis's religion, he must accept Iblis's admission. Thus, the accusation is refuted.

barhan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Yusuf intended to sin or committed a sin.
Correction: The Quran and even Iblis affirm his innocence and chosen status.

Checkpoint

A) Zulaykha's confession
B) Allah's testimony

D) The testimony of the brothers

Key Takeaway
Three independent sources—Zulaykha, Allah, and Iblis—all testify to Yusuf's innocence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are falsely accused, do you have any witness—human, divine, or even from your enemies—that can testify to your innocence? If not, what does that reveal about your connection to Allah?
?
Allah turned away evil from Yusuf because he was among the mukhlasin. What are you doing today to be among those whom Allah protects from sin, not just outwardly but in the hidden desires of your heart?
?
Iblis admitted he has no power over the chosen servants. If you were to face Iblis today, would he say the same about you, or would he find an opening? Where is the crack in your defenses?

Refuting the Hashwiyya: Accepting Divine Testimony
Imam Razi refutes the Hashwiyya using a logical dilemma based on the proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Hashwiyya by demanding they accept either Allah's testimony or Iblis's admission.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya must accept divine testimony or be followers of Iblis.

After presenting the proofs, Imam Razi directly refutes the Hashwiyya, a group that claimed Yusuf sinned. He argues that their accusation is false from every angle: if they follow Allah, they must accept His testimony; if they follow Iblis, they must accept Iblis's admission. Either way, Yusuf's innocence is established.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage where Imam Razi refutes the Hashwiyya. Note his logical argument.
2
Understand the accusation
The Hashwiyya claimed Yusuf committed sin. Imam Razi says this is false from all aspects.
This is the backdrop of the refutation.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Imam Razi presents a dilemma: if the Hashwi follows Allah's religion, he must accept Allah's testimony; if he follows Iblis's religion, he must accept Iblis's admission. Both point to innocence.
This forces the opponent to choose a side.
4
Conclude the argument
Since both options lead to the same conclusion, the accusation is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : اعتراف زليخا بذلك : ﴿وَلَقَدْ رَاوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ فَاسْتَعْصَمَ﴾ [يوسف: 32] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿الْآنَ حَصْحَصَ الْحَقُّ أَنَا رَاوَدتُّهُ عَن نَّفْسِهِ وَإِنَّهُ لَمِنَ الصَّادِقِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 51]. وسادسها : شهادة رب السماوات والأرض بذلك وهي قوله : ﴿وَرَاوَدَتْهُ الَّتِي هُوَ فِي بَيْتِهَا عَن نَّفْسِهِ﴾ [يوسف: 23] وفي آية أخرى : ﴿كَذَلِكَ لِنَصْرِفَ عَنْهُ السُّوءَ وَالْفَحْشَاءَ إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 24]. وسابعها : إقرار إبليس بأن يوسف كان مبرأً عن تلك التهمة لأنه قال : ﴿فَبِعِزَّتِكَ لَأُغْوِيَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ إِلَّا عِبَادَكَ مِنْهُمُ الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [ص: 82-83] فأقر أنه لا تعلق له بالمخلصين، والله تعالى شهد بأن يوسف كان من المخلصين كما قال: ﴿إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصِينَ﴾ [يوسف: 24]. فهذا الطعن الذي قاله أهل الحشو باطل من كل الوجوه، فإن كان الحشو على دين الله وجب عليه أن يقبل قول الله وشهادته وإن كان على دين إبليس وجب عليه أن يقبل قول إبليس.
And the fifth: the confession of Zulaykha to that: 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her if it were not that he saw the proof of his Lord...' and in another verse: 'Now the truth has become manifest: I did desire him, and indeed he is of the truthful.' And the sixth: the testimony of the Lord of the heavens and the earth to that, which is His saying: 'And she, in whose house he was, desired him...' and in another verse: 'Thus We turned away from him evil and immorality. Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' And the seventh: the admission of Iblis that Yusuf was innocent of that accusation, because he said: 'By Your might, I will surely mislead them all, except Your chosen servants among them.' So he admitted that he has no influence over the chosen ones, and Allah the Exalted testified that Yusuf was among the chosen ones, as He said: 'Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' So this accusation that the Hashwiyya made is false from all aspects. For if the Hashwi is upon the religion of Allah, he must accept the word of Allah and His testimony; and if he is upon the religion of Iblis, he must accept the word of Iblis.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof and refutation. Imam Razi presents three of the seven proofs (the fifth, sixth, and seventh) that the Prophet Yusuf was innocent of sinning with Zulaykha. The fifth proof is Zulaykha's own confession: in one verse she admits she desired him and he remained chaste, in another she confesses she was the one who desired him. The sixth proof is Allah's own testimony that He turned away evil and immorality from Yusuf and that Yusuf was among His chosen servants (mukhlasin). The seventh proof is the admission of Iblis, who declared he cannot mislead Allah's chosen servants, and Allah declared Yusuf among them. After listing these proofs, Razi refutes the Hashwiyya, a group that claimed Yusuf committed sin. He argues that the accusation is false from all angles: if the Hashwi follows Allah's religion, he must accept Allah's testimony; if he follows Iblis's religion, he must accept Iblis's admission. Thus, the accusation is refuted.

Key Terms
Hashwiyya
mukhlasin

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Hashwiyya's accusation has any merit.
Correction: Imam Razi shows it is false regardless of the perspective.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what must a Hashwi who follows Allah's religion do?
A) Reject Allah's testimony
B) Accept Iblis's word
C) Accept Allah's word and testimony
D) Follow the Hashwiyya

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya's accusation collapses because both divine and satanic testimony support Yusuf's innocence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Hashwiyya were accused of being on Iblis's religion because they rejected divine testimony. Is there any testimony from Allah in the Quran or Sunnah that you have been reluctant to accept fully? What would it take for you to submit completely?

Refutation of Third Objection: Yusuf's Statement on the Soul
Imam Razi answers the objection from Yusuf's verse by clarifying the intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends prophetic infallibility by distinguishing between natural inclination and sinful resolve.

Core Idea
Yusuf's statement is an admission of human weakness, not sin.

The third objection against prophetic infallibility cites Yusuf's own words: 'I do not exculpate myself; the soul is prone to evil.' How can a prophet admit such a thing? Imam Razi answers by carefully distinguishing between the soul's natural inclination and the firm resolve to commit disobedience. This lesson explains that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the clear structure: the objection cites a verse, and the answer explains its intended meaning.
The verse is from Surah Yusuf, Ayah 53.
2
Identify the key distinction
Imam Razi differentiates between natural desire (mayl/du'aa') and firm resolve ('azm) to sin. Find these terms in the Arabic and understand their significance.
This distinction is crucial for preserving 'isma.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this interpretation protects the doctrine of prophetic infallibility. Yusuf is not confessing sin, but acknowledging the innate human struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: قول يوسف عليه السلام: ﴿وَمَا أُبَرِّئُ نَفْسِي إِنَّ النَّفْسَ لَأَمَّارَةٌ بِالسُّوءِ﴾ [يوسف: 53]. والجواب : المراد منه الدعاء والميل لا العزم على المعصية.
The third objection: The saying of Yusuf, peace be upon him: 'And I do not exculpate myself. Indeed, the soul is ever inclined to evil.' The answer: What is meant by it is [natural] inclination and desire, not the determination to commit disobedience.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector cites Yusuf's own statement 'I do not exculpate myself; the soul is prone to evil' as evidence that Yusuf admitted to having sinful tendencies or possibly committing a sin. Imam Razi answers that Yusuf's words refer to the innate human inclination (al-du'aa') and natural desire (al-mayl) for things, not to a firm resolve ('azm) to commit an act of disobedience. Thus, Yusuf is not confessing to a sinful act, but merely acknowledging the weakness of the human soul in its natural state. This preserves the doctrine of prophetic infallibility ('isma).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Yusuf admitted to sinning or having sinful intent, which would contradict his prophethood.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that Yusuf was referring to natural human inclination, not deliberate resolve to sin, thus preserving his infallibility.

Checkpoint

B) As a reference to natural inclination not sinful resolve

D) As a metaphor

Key Takeaway
Yusuf's statement about the soul is an acknowledgment of human inclination, not an admission of sin, thereby upholding prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If prophets like Yusuf admit their soul's inclination, how much more should you be humble about your own? In what area of your life are you pretending to be 'exculpated' when you are not?

Refutation of Fourth Objection: Yusuf's Love for Prison
Imam Razi answers an objection about Yusuf's preference for prison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Yusuf's statement 'prison is more beloved to me' does not indicate love of sin, because it is a comparative preference in a conflict.

Core Idea
Preferring the lesser evil is not loving evil.

Imam Razi addresses a subtle objection: does Yusuf's choice of prison over sin imply he loved prison, which itself might be a sin? The answer hinges on the difference between absolute love and comparative preference. This passage clarifies a key principle in evaluating prophetic statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the objector frames Yusuf's statement as an expression of love for prison, then concludes that loving prison (a sin) is sinful.
The objector assumes 'more beloved' means absolute love.
2
Identify Imam Razi's response
Focus on the answer: Yusuf did not say 'I love prison' absolutely. He said it is 'more beloved' in the context of a conflict between two alternatives.
The key term is 'ta'arud' (conflict).
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on the logical difference between absolute and comparative statements. The objection confuses the two. Razi's answer shows that choosing the lesser evil is not sinful.
This principle applies broadly in Islamic ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : أن حبس يوسف عليه السلام في السجن كان معصية فلم قال : ﴿رَبِّ السِّجْنُ أَحَبُّ إِلَيَّ مِمَّا يَدْعُونَنِي إِلَيْهِ﴾ [يوسف: 33] ومحبة المعصية معصية. والجواب : أنه لم يقل : إن أحب هذا السجن بل قال : لما وقع التعارض بين الفاحشة وبين هذا السجن فهذا السجن أحب إليَّ.
The fourth objection: That Yusuf's imprisonment in jail was a sin, so why did he say: 'My Lord, prison is more beloved to me than that to which they invite me'? And love of sin is itself a sin. The answer: He did not say 'I love this prison' absolutely. Rather, he said: when there was a conflict between immorality and this prison, then this prison is more beloved to me.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector argues that Yusuf's statement 'Prison is more beloved to me than what they invite me to' implies that he loved prison, and since prison is a sin (perhaps because it is a place of punishment or involves harm), loving it would be sinful. Imam Razi's answer is precise: Yusuf did not make an absolute statement of love for prison. Instead, he made a comparative statement: when forced to choose between committing immorality (the sin of adultery) and going to prison, he chose the lesser evil. The phrase 'more beloved' indicates a preference in a comparison, not an absolute love. Thus, there is no implication of loving sin.

Key Terms
hubb
fahisha
sijn
ta'arud

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That preferring a lesser sin is sinful because any preference for something sinful indicates love of sin.
Correction: Razi shows that a comparative preference in a conflict does not imply absolute love for the preferred option; it is a choice between two evils.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why is Yusuf's statement 'prison is more beloved to me' not an instance of loving sin?
A) Because prison is not a sin
B) Because it is a comparative statement between two options
C) Because he was joking
D) Because he actually loved prison

Key Takeaway
Yusuf's statement expresses a comparative preference to avoid a greater sin, not an absolute love for prison.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between sin and hardship, do you genuinely consider the hardship as 'more beloved' to you than displeasing Allah, or do you secretly harbor a desire for the sin? Yusuf's words expose the heart's true inclination. What do your choices in such moments reveal about your love for Allah?
?
Think of a 'prison' in your life—a struggle or sacrifice you endure to avoid a certain sin. Is your heart content with that hardship, or do you resent it? Yusuf's contentment was rooted in taqwa. What would need to change in you to reach that same level of contentment?

Defending Yusuf's Cup Stratagem
Razi answers the fifth objection about Yusuf's cup stratagem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the prophetic act of Yusuf by showing it was a lawful stratagem with justifications including divine command and prior knowledge of his brother.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions are justified by purpose, command, and context.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a moral objection to Prophet Yusuf's actions. The objector questions the deception of placing a cup in his brother's bag. Razi provides three justifications that transform the apparent deception into a lawful and even praiseworthy act. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the objection and answer sink in.
Notice the concise style of Razi's response.
2
Identify the three justifications
Razi gives three distinct points in his answer. List them: legitimate purpose, possible divine command, and a narration about informing the brother.
Each point independently refutes the objection.
3
Reflect on the moral force
Consider how each justification addresses the concern of deception and grief. How does the third point (informing the brother) eliminate the charge of causing grief?
4
Apply the lesson
Think about broader principle: how should we understand actions of prophets that might seem questionable? Razi shows the need to examine intent and context.
This connects to the broader theme of 'ismah (prophetic infallibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : لم جعل السقاية في رحل أخيه ؟ والجواب : أن الغرض منه التسبب إلى أن أخاه عنده، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك بأمر الله تعالى، وروي أنه أعلم أخاه بذلك، ليجعله طريقا إلى حبسه عنده، وعلى هذا الوجه لا يكون ذلك سببًا لإدخال الغم في قلب أخيه.
The fifth objection: Why did he place the drinking cup in his brother's saddlebag? The answer: The purpose of it was the means to have his brother with him. And it is possible that this was by the command of Allah the Exalted. And it is narrated that he informed his brother of that, in order to make it a way to detain him with him. On this view, it is not a cause for bringing grief to his brother's heart.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The objector questions the morality of Yusuf's act of placing the cup in his brother's bag, which led to his brother being accused of theft. Imam Razi gives three points in the answer. First, the purpose was to keep his brother with him, which was a legitimate objective. Second, it is possible that this was done by Allah's direct command, making it an act of obedience. Third, there is a narration that Yusuf actually informed his brother of the plan beforehand, so the brother was not surprised or grieved. On this view, no actual grief was caused. Thus, the act is justified and does not involve deception or harm.

Key Terms

rahil
tasabbub
amr Allah
ghamm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Yusuf deceived his brother and caused him grief without justification.
Correction: Razi shows that Yusuf's act was a lawful stratagem either by Allah's command or with his brother's prior knowledge, thus no actual deception or grief occurred.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi's defense shows that prophetic actions are justified by purpose, divine command, and context, removing any moral objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Did you consider their feelings? How can you follow Yusuf's example of ensuring your means are lawful and your intentions clear to avoid causing grief?
?
Razi mentions that Yusuf's action might have been by Allah's command. When you do something that seems questionable, do you first ask if it aligns with what Allah has commanded? Or do you rely solely on your own reasoning? This lesson challenges you to base your actions on divine guidance, not just your own logic. Will you seek Allah's command before acting?
?
The objector focused on the apparent act, but Yusuf had a deeper purpose. Are you quick to judge others' actions without knowing their intentions or the full context? How can you cultivate the habit of assuming good intentions, as Razi demonstrates by defending Yusuf?

Exonerating Yusuf: Three Interpretations
Imam Razi gives three interpretations to absolve Yusuf of falsely accusing his brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi offers three plausible interpretations to reconcile the caller's announcement with Yusuf's prophetic integrity.

Core Idea
Prophetic innocence demands careful exegesis of ambiguous texts.

In explaining the story of Yusuf, a potential objection arises: did Yusuf order the caller to falsely accuse his brothers? Imam Razi provides three nuanced interpretations that preserve Yusuf's truthfulness as a prophet. By exploring these, we see how theological principles guide the interpretation of scriptural accounts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get familiar with the structure and key terms like 'nida'' and 'sariqun'.
The passage lists three aspects (wujuh).
2
Identify each interpretation
Go through each of the three interpretations Razi mentions. Note what each one proposes and how it absolves Yusuf.
The interpretations involve the caller's authority, the object of theft, and the grammatical mood.
3
Compare with the literal translation
Read the literal translation and see how it maps onto the Arabic. Notice the Quranic example cited (al-An'am 76).
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: why does Razi go to such lengths? What does this tell us about the importance of prophetic infallibility ('isma)?
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can distinguish the interpretations from a false claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نداء المنادي بأنهم لسرقون ففيه وجوه : الأول : لعل ذلك النداء ما كان بأمره بل نادى بذلك واحد من القوم لما فقدوا الصواع. الثاني : هب أنه كان بأمره لكنه لم يناد بأنكم سرقتم الصواع، بل نادى بأنكم سارقون، ولعل المراد أنهم سرقوا يوسف من أبيه. الثالث : لعل المراد من الكلام الاستفهام وإن كان ظاهره الخبر والمعنى إنكم لسارقون؟ فأسقط ألف الاستفهام، كما في قوله : ﴿هَذَا رَبِّي﴾ [الأنعام: 76].
As for the call of the caller that they are thieves, it has several aspects: The first: Perhaps that call was not by his order; rather, one of the people called out that when they lost the cup. The second: Granted that it was by his order, but he did not call out 'you stole the cup'; rather, he called out 'you are thieves', and perhaps what is meant is that they stole Yusuf from his father. The third: Perhaps what is meant by the speech is the interrogative form, even though its apparent form is declarative, and the meaning is 'Are you thieves?' So the interrogative alif was omitted, as in His saying: 'This is my lord' [as an interrogative].
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This is a multi-aspect answer to an implicit objection: that Yusuf ordered the caller to announce that his brothers were thieves, which would be a false accusation. Imam Razi provides three possible interpretations. First, it is possible that the call was not ordered by Yusuf at all; rather, one of the Egyptian people spontaneously called out when they realized the cup was missing. Second, even if it was ordered by Yusuf, the caller did not say 'you stole the cup' but said 'you are thieves,' which could be interpreted allegorically: they had previously 'stolen' Yusuf from his father (i.e., taken him away). So the announcement was true in a metaphorical sense. Third, the apparent declarative statement may actually be a rhetorical question, with the alif of interrogation omitted (as in the Qur'anic example 'This is my lord' meaning 'Is this my lord?'). Thus, the caller's words could mean 'Are you thieves?' which is a question, not an accusation. These interpretations exonerate Yusuf from making a false accusation.

Key Terms
nida'
sariqun
isti'fham
suwa'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet Yusuf falsely accused his brothers of theft.
Correction: Razi shows that the announcement can be interpreted in ways that preserve Yusuf's truthfulness, such as it being a question or referring to a different theft.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the interpretations Razi gives for the caller's announcement?
A) The call was not ordered by Yusuf
B) The call meant they had stolen Yusuf from his father
C) The call was a literal question
D) The call was a lie told by Yusuf

Key Takeaway
Razi provides three exegetical strategies to defend the integrity of Prophet Yusuf against the charge of false accusation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a report about a prophet that seems to contradict their innocence, do you instinctively assume the worst or do you pause to seek a valid interpretation? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's choice of messengers?
?
Imagine you were accused of something you didn't do, but the evidence appeared against you. Would you search for alternative explanations as Razi did for Yusuf, or would you despair? How can this lesson train you to give others the benefit of doubt?
?
The third interpretation involves a subtle linguistic shift. When you encounter a verse that troubles your heart, are you willing to dig deeper into language and context to preserve your faith, or do you prefer the comfort of a superficial reading? Commit now to one concrete step to deepen your Quranic understanding.

Objection on Yusuf's Statement 'I Do Not Acquit Myself'
Objection and answer concerning Yusuf's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends prophetic infallibility against the apparent admission of sin in Yusuf's statement.

Core Idea
Prophet Yusuf's words acknowledge human weakness, not a sinful resolve.

This passage presents a common objection against prophetic infallibility: Prophet Yusuf himself says 'I do not acquit myself,' seemingly admitting sin. Imam Razi responds by distinguishing between natural inclination and deliberate resolve. This distinction is crucial for understanding how prophets are protected from sin while still experiencing human tendencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise structure: objection followed by answer.
The verse citation is from Surah Yusuf.
2
Identify the objection
The objector uses Yusuf's statement to argue that even a prophet admits to having a soul that commands evil, implying he is not sinless.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi clarifies that Yusuf's words refer to natural inclination (al-mayl) and desire, not the resolve (al-azm) to commit sin. This preserves infallibility.
Inclination is involuntary, while resolve is a deliberate choice.
4
Reflect on the defense of infallibility
Consider why this distinction is necessary: prophets can feel temptations but never resolve to sin. This aligns with their protected status.
This is a standard kalam defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: قول يوسف عليه السلام : وَمَا أُبَرِى نَفْسِي إِنَّ النَّفْسَ لَأَمَارَةُ بِالسُّوء [يوسف: ٥٣]. والجواب : المراد منه الدعاء والميل لا العزم على المعصية.
The third doubt: The saying of Yusuf, peace be upon him: 'And I do not acquit myself. Indeed, the soul is a persistent enjoiner of evil' [Yusuf: 53]. The answer: what is meant by it is the inclination and desire, not the resolve to disobey.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the apparent admission of sin by Prophet Yusuf. The objector uses Yusuf's statement 'I do not acquit myself' to argue that he acknowledges having a sinful nature. The answer clarifies that the phrase refers to natural human inclinations and desires, not an actual intention to commit sin. Thus, it does not contradict prophetic infallibility.

Key Terms
نفس
أمارة بالسوء
عزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Prophet Yusuf was admitting to actual sin or a sinful nature that contradicts infallibility.
Correction: Yusuf's statement refers to natural human inclination, not an intention to sin. Prophets are protected from resolving to sin.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say is meant by Yusuf's statement 'I do not acquit myself'?

B) Inclination and desire, not resolve
C) Pride in his own purity
D) A rhetorical question

Key Takeaway
Prophets are infallible from actual sin, but they experience natural human inclinations without acting on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often confuse your inclinations with sinful intentions, leading you to despair or self-condemnation? How can today's lesson change the way you judge your inner thoughts?
?
When you feel a desire for something wrong, do you immediately label yourself as sinful, or do you pause and distinguish between the passing thought and the resolve to act? Which path leads to true self-accountability?
?
Yusuf (peace be upon him) acknowledged the soul's pull toward evil without acting on it. In what area of your life are you making excuses for your actions by blaming your 'nafs' instead of owning your choice? What will you do about it starting today?

Defending Yusuf's Preference for Prison
Imam Razi answers the fourth doubt on Yusuf's preference for prison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Prophet Yusuf's statement about preferring prison by distinguishing absolute love from relative preference.

Core Idea
Preference due to conflict is not love of sin.

Imam Razi addresses an objection that Prophet Yusuf's statement shows love for imprisonment, which would be a sin. The objection misunderstands the nature of preference. Razi clarifies that Yusuf did not love prison for itself but chose it as the lesser evil when faced with a choice between prison and sin. This lesson examines that reasoning and its defense of prophetic integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the doubt carefully: It claims that Yusuf's statement 'Prison is more beloved to me' implies love of sin, which is itself a sin.
Notice how the objection assumes absolute love.
2
Read Razi's answer
In the answer, note the distinction: Yusuf did not say he loves this prison absolutely. He said that when there was a conflict (ta'arud) between prison and sin, prison became more beloved to him relative to that sin.
The key word is 'lammā waqa'a al-ta'āruḍ' – when there was a conflict.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider the principle: Choosing the lesser evil when forced between two options does not mean one loves that evil. It is a relative preference to avoid a greater harm.
This principle protects the infallibility of prophets.
4
Apply to other contexts
Think of similar scenarios in Islamic theology where a prophet's action might appear censurable but is actually a relative preference to avoid a greater sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : أن حبس يوسف عليه السلام في السجن كان معصية فلم قال : رَبِّ السّجْنُ أَحَبُّ إِلَيَّ مِمَّا يَدْعُونَنِي إِلَيْهِ [يوسف: ٣٣] ومحبة المعصية معصية. والجواب : أنه لم يقل : إن أحب هذا السجن بل قال : لما وقع التعارض هذا السجن فهذا السجن أحب إليَّ.
The fourth doubt: That Yusuf's imprisonment in prison was a sin, so why did he say: 'My Lord, prison is more beloved to me than that to which they invite me' [Yusuf: 33]? And love of sin is sin. The answer: He did not say that he loves this prison; rather he said: when there was a conflict between this prison and that sin, this prison is more beloved to me.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that Yusuf's statement shows love for prison, which is a punishment and thus sinful. The answer clarifies the context: Yusuf did not express absolute love for prison, but a relative preference. When faced with a choice between prison and committing a sin, he chose prison as the lesser of two evils. This does not entail loving sin.

Key Terms
حب السجن
محبة المعصية
التعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Yusuf loved prison as an object of enjoyment, which would be sinful.
Correction: Yusuf only preferred prison as the lesser evil to avoid sin; it was a relative preference, not absolute love.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi explain Yusuf's statement 'Prison is more beloved to me'?
A) Yusuf loved prison itself
B) It was a figure of speech
C) It was a relative preference due to conflict with sin
D) It was a mistake

Key Takeaway
Prophet Yusuf's preference for prison was a relative choice to avoid sin, not an endorsement of imprisonment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between a sin and a difficult but permissible option, do you honestly prefer the difficult option to avoid sin, or do you sometimes rationalize the sin? What would it take for you to develop the same mindset as Yusuf?
?
Think of a recent decision where you chose a 'lesser evil'—did you do it reluctantly or with a genuine preference for avoiding the greater sin? What does that say about your love for obedience?
?
This lesson shows that Yusuf's preference was relative, not absolute. Are there areas in your life where you treat a relative preference as an absolute one, either by over-justifying a choice or by condemning something as sinful that is not? How can you apply the principle of tafḍīl to bring more clarity to your daily choices?

Justifying Yusuf's Act: The Fifth Doubt
Imam Razi defends Yusuf's placing of the cup as a lawful means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Yusuf's action of placing the cup as a legitimate means, not deception.

Core Idea
Yusuf's act was a lawful means, not deceit.

This lesson addresses a common objection: why did Prophet Yusuf place the cup in his brother's bag? Imam Razi provides a rational and scriptural defense, showing that the act was a permissible stratagem, possibly commanded by Allah and known to the brother beforehand. The lesson clarifies the ethical and theological reasoning behind prophetic actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Pay attention to the doubt and the response given by Imam Razi.
The objection is in the form of a question.
2
Analyze the justification
Identify the key points in the answer: the purpose was to keep the brother, it could be by Allah's command, and the brother was informed.
These points remove any element of deception.
3
Reflect on the ethical implication
Consider how this defense shows that prophets act righteously even when their actions may seem ambiguous.
This relates to the broader theme of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : لم جعل السقاية في رحل أخيه ؟ والجواب : أن الغرض منه التسبب إلى أن يبقى أخاه عنده، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك بأمر الله تعالى، وروي أنه أعلم أخاه بذلك، ليجعله طريقا إلى حبسه عنده، لا وعلى هذا الوجه لا يكون ذلك سببًا لإدخال الغم في قلب أخيه.
The fifth doubt: Why did he place the cup in his brother's saddlebag? The answer: The purpose of it was to cause his brother to remain with him, and it is possible that that was by the command of Allah the Exalted, and it is narrated that he informed his brother of that, to make it a means to detain him with him. And in this way it is not a cause for entering grief into his brother's heart.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection about Yusuf's apparent trickery in placing the cup. The answer states that his intention was legitimate: to keep his brother Benjamin with him, possibly by divine command. A tradition mentions he informed Benjamin beforehand, so no deception or grief was caused. Thus, the act was permissible.

Key Terms
السقاية
التسبب
حبس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Yusuf tricked his brother unjustly.
Correction: Yusuf acted with a valid purpose (to keep him), possibly by divine command, and informed his brother, so no deception occurred.

Checkpoint

A) To steal the cup
B) To cause his brother to remain with him
C) To test his brothers
D) To punish his brother

Key Takeaway
Yusuf's placing of the cup was a permissible means to keep his brother, not a sinful deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Three Explanations for the Accusation of Theft
Imam Razi offers three explanations for the accusation of theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three interpretations Imam Razi provides to defend Prophet Yusuf from the accusation of falsely calling his brothers thieves.

Core Idea
Imam Razi offers three ways to understand the call, all preserving Yusuf's truthfulness.

In the story of Yusuf, a caller announces that Yusuf's brothers are thieves. This appears to be a false accusation if Yusuf ordered it. Imam Razi provides three explanations to show that Yusuf did not lie. Let's explore these defenses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of the three explanations.
2
Understand the first explanation
The first possibility is that the caller acted without Yusuf's command. This removes the accusation from Yusuf.
3
Understand the second explanation
Even if Yusuf ordered it, the wording 'you are thieves' could refer to stealing Yusuf earlier, not the cup.
4
Understand the third explanation
The statement could be a rhetorical question, with the alif of interrogation dropped, comparing to a Quranic verse.
5
Reflect on the overall defense
Consider how these interpretations protect Yusuf's prophethood and integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نداء المنادي بأنهم لسرقون ففيه وجوه : الأول : لعل ذلك النداء ما كان بأمره بل نادى بذلك واحد من القوم لما فقدوا الصواع. الثاني : هب أنه كان بأمره لكنه لم يناد بأنكم سرقتم الصواع، بل نادى بأنكم سارقون، ولعل المراد أنهم سرقوا يوسف من أبيه. الثالث : لعل المراد من الكلام الاستفهام وإن كان ظاهره الخبر والمعنى إننكم لسارقون؟ فأسقط ألف الاستفهام، كما في قوله : هَذَا رَبِّ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٦]
As for the call of the caller that they are thieves, there are aspects: First: Perhaps that call was not by his command, but a man from the people called that when they lost the cup. Second: Suppose it was by his command, but he did not call out 'You stole the cup,' rather he called out 'You are thieves,' and perhaps what is meant is that they stole Yusuf from his father. Third: Perhaps what is meant by the speech is the interrogative even though its apparent form is declarative, meaning 'Are you thieves?' so the interrogative alif was dropped, as in His saying: 'This is my Lord' [al-An'am: 76].
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides three possible explanations for the troubling call accusing Yusuf's brothers of theft. The first possibility absolves Yusuf of responsibility by suggesting the caller acted on his own. The second allows that if Yusuf ordered it, he did not explicitly say 'you stole the cup' but 'you are thieves,' which could refer to a past event (stealing Yusuf). The third interprets the statement as a rhetorical question, comparing to a Quranic usage where 'hadha rabbi' is interpreted interrogatively. These defenses uphold Yusuf's integrity.

Key Terms
نداء
سرقون
استفهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Prophet Yusuf falsely accused his brothers of stealing the cup.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the call can be interpreted in ways that do not make Yusuf a liar; it may have been from someone else, or meant something else, or been a rhetorical question.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the explanations Imam Razi gives for the call 'You are thieves'?
A) The caller acted without Yusuf's command
B) The call meant they stole Yusuf from his father
C) It was a direct and literal accusation
D) It may have been a rhetorical question

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi provides three interpretations that preserve Yusuf's truthfulness, showing the depth of Islamic scholarly defense of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a prophet's action that seems to contradict their infallibility, do you instinctively seek a deeper explanation as Imam Razi did, or do you let doubt creep in? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
Imam Razi offered three possible interpretations to defend Yusuf. Are you willing to put that same intellectual effort into defending the honor of the prophets when you encounter difficult passages? What holds you back?
?
If you were in Yusuf's position and people misunderstood your actions, would you have the patience and wisdom to allow for multiple valid interpretations, or would you insist on your own version of events? How does this passage challenge your natural defensiveness?

Yusuf's Silence: Divine Command or Callousness?
Imam Razi answers the sixth doubt regarding Yusuf's failure to inform his father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi explains that Yusuf's failure to inform his father was an act of obedience to a divine command.

Core Idea
Yusuf's silence was a divine decree, not a human failing.

This short passage addresses a common doubt about the Prophet Yusuf: why did he let his father suffer in grief? Imam Razi provides a concise answer rooted in divine decree, showing that Yusuf's seemingly harsh silence was itself an act of worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection

The doubt assumes Yusuf had the ability to inform and should have done so.
2
Read the answer

The answer uses 'perhaps' (لعل) to indicate possibility, not certainty.
3
Connect to prophethood
