Defending the Prostration to Yusuf
Imam Razi defends the prostration incident by redirecting worship to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the prophetic integrity of Yusuf (AS) by reinterpreting the prostration of his parents as an act of gratitude to Allah, not worship of Yusuf.

Core Idea
The prostration was to Allah, not Yusuf — a lesson in theological interpretation.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a significant objection concerning the story of Prophet Yusuf: how could he allow his parents to prostrate to him? This objection challenges both tawhid and filial respect. Razi's response demonstrates a nuanced approach to Qur'anic interpretation, showing that apparent difficulties can be resolved by understanding the context and intent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the two-fold problem: prostration to other than Allah is forbidden, and using parents as servants is disrespectful.
2
Examine Razi's answer
Note how he reinterprets the action: they prostrated to Allah for Yusuf's sake, as gratitude for finding him.
3
Consider the counter-argument
The opponent cites Yusuf's dream as literal prostration to him. Razi refutes this by reinterpreting the dream's fulfillment.
4
Reflect on the outcome
This explanation preserves both tawhid and the respect due to parents, showing the depth of Islamic theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَفَعَ أَبَوَيْهِ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ وَخَرُوا لَهُ سُجَدَا [يوسف: ١٠٠] فكيف رضي بأن يسجدوا له؟ وذلك محذور من وجهين: الأول : أن السجود لغير الله لا يجوز الثاني : أن استخدام الأبوين لا يجوز. والجواب : المعنى وخروا لأجله سجدًا لله، فإن من فقد ولده ثم وجده فإنه يسجد لله تعالى شكرًا لنعمة الجدان. لا يقال : هذا التأويل يدفعه قوله تعالى : وَقَالَ يَا أَبَتِ هَذَا تَأْوِيلُ رُؤْيَايَ مِن قَبْلُ قَدْ جَعَلَهَا رَبِّ حَقًّا [يوسف: ١٠٠] وتلك الرؤيا هي قوله إني رَأَيْتُ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ كَوْكَبًا وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ رَأَيْتُهُمْ لِي سَاجِدِينَ [يوسف: ٤] . لأنا نقول : لا نسلم أن تأويل رؤياه ما ذكرتم بل تأويل رؤياه بلوغه إلى أرفع المنازل، ولما رأى أبويه على أشرف الأحوال في الدارين كان ذلك مصداقا لرؤياه المتقدمة.
The seventh doubt: The saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And he raised his parents upon the throne, and they bowed down to him in prostration' [Yusuf: 100]. So how did he consent that they prostrate to him? That is problematic from two aspects: first, prostration to other than Allah is not permissible; second, employing one's parents is not permissible. The answer: The meaning is that they prostrated for his sake, prostrating to Allah. For whoever loses his child then finds him, he prostrates to Allah the Exalted in gratitude for the blessing of finding. It cannot be said: This interpretation is refuted by His saying: 'And he said, O my father, this is the interpretation of my vision from before; my Lord has made it truth' [Yusuf: 100], and that vision is his saying: 'Indeed, I saw eleven stars and the sun and the moon, I saw them prostrating to me' [Yusuf: 4]. For we say: We do not concede that the interpretation of his vision is what you mentioned. Rather, the interpretation of his vision is his attaining the highest stations. And when he saw his parents in the most honorable state in both abodes, that was a confirmation of his earlier vision.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defends the prostration incident from two objections: prostration to anyone but Allah is shirk, and using parents as servants is disrespectful. The answer reinterprets the prostration as being to Allah in gratitude, with Yusuf as the occasion. A counter-argument from the interpretation of Yusuf's dream is addressed: the dream's fulfillment was not literal prostration to Yusuf, but the attainment of high status for his parents, which was realized when they were honored.

Key Terms
سجود
تأويل الرؤيا
شكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Yusuf allowed his parents to worship him as an act of respect.
Correction: The prostration was directed to Allah as an act of gratitude, with Yusuf as the occasion, not the object.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi interpret the prostration of Yusuf's parents?
A) They prostrated to Yusuf as a sign of respect
B) They prostrated to Allah with Yusuf as an intermediary
C) They prostrated to Allah for Yusuf's sake
D) It was a cultural custom

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that careful interpretation preserves both the Oneness of Allah and the honor of prophets and parents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah for a blessing, do you ever find yourself directing that gratitude toward the person who was the means, rather than to Allah alone? How can Yusuf's example reorient your heart?
?
Have you ever misread a dream or ambition as a promise of personal glory, only to realize later that Allah meant something far greater and more humbling? What would it mean for you to reinterpret your goals as 'attainment of high station' in Allah's sight, not worldly status?
?
Are you willing to examine your own acts of apparent devotion—prayer, charity, even teaching—to see if they are truly for Allah or subtly for the approval of others? What will you change today to purify your intention?

Imam Razi's Refutation of the Objection on Prophet Ayyub's Statement
Imam Razi refutes the claim that Ayyub's statement implies prior sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Ayyub's mention of 'punishment' does not imply prior sin, and how contextual analysis preserves prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Context saves the Prophet's honor: praise at the end negates blame at the beginning.

A common objection against the infallibility of prophets is that Prophet Ayyub (Job) complained of 'punishment' from Satan, which seems to admit prior sin. Imam Razi refutes this by pointing to the immediate context of the verse. This lesson shows how careful attention to the Qur'an's own structure resolves apparent difficulties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the objection and the answer.
Pay attention to the word 'adhab (punishment) and the praise at the end.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that 'punishment' only follows sin, so Ayyub must have sinned. Note this reasoning.
3
Analyze the answer
Imam Razi argues that the verse ends with Allah praising Ayyub as 'patient' and 'excellent servant'. This praise cannot coexist with blame at the beginning.
Thus, 'punishment' is reinterpreted as Satan's whispers, not divine retribution.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how contextual coherence is used to defend prophetic infallibility. This is a common tafsir method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة السابعة : قصة أيوب عليه السلام وهي أنه تعالى حكى عنه أنه قال : إِنِّي مَسَّنِيَ الشَّيْطَانُ بِنُصْبٍ وَعَذَابٍ [ص: ٤١] العذاب لا يكون إلا من الذنب كالعقاب وهذا يدل على تقدم الذنب. والجواب: أنه تعالى مدحه في آخر الآية بقوله : إِنَّا وَجَدْنَاهُ صَابِرًا نِعْمَ الْعَبْدُ إِنَّهُ أَوَّابٌ [ص: ٤٤] وإذا كان آخر الآية مدحًا امتنع أن يكون أول الآية ذما، فثبت أن المراد من العذاب تلك الوساوس الموحشة التي كان يلقيها الشيطان في قلبه.
The seventh story: The story of Ayyub (Job), peace be upon him. That Allah the Exalted narrated from him that he said: 'Indeed, Satan has touched me with hardship and punishment' [Sad: 41]. Punishment ('adhab) does not occur except from sin, like retribution, and this indicates the prior existence of sin. The answer: That Allah the Exalted praised him at the end of the verse by saying: 'Indeed, We found him patient. An excellent servant. Indeed, he was repeatedly returning [to Allah]' [Sad: 44]. And when the end of the verse is praise, it is impossible that the beginning of the verse be blame. So it is established that what is meant by 'punishment' is those frightening whispers that Satan was casting into his heart.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Prophet Ayyub's complaint of 'punishment' implies he committed a sin. The answer uses a contextual argument: the very same verse ends with Allah praising Ayyub as patient and excellent. If the first part were a blameworthy admission of sin, the praise would be contradictory. Therefore, 'adhab here means the psychological suffering from Satan's insinuations, not punishment for sin. This preserves Ayyub's infallibility.

Key Terms
عذاب
ذنب
وساوس
أواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Prophet Ayyub admitted to sin because he mentioned 'punishment' from Satan.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the context of praise in the same verse necessitates that 'punishment' here means psychological distress from Satan's whispers, not punishment for sin.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that Ayyub's mention of 'punishment' does not indicate prior sin?
A) Because punishment is not always from sin
B) Because the verse ends with praise of Ayyub
C) Because Ayyub was an infallible prophet
D) Because Satan cannot cause real punishment

Key Takeaway
Prophetic statements must be interpreted in light of their full Qur'anic context; praise at the end of a verse can clarify the meaning of earlier parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that seems to imply something negative about a prophet, do you immediately assume negative or do you pause to seek the full context? How might your rushed judgment offend the honor of Allah's chosen servants?
?
Prophet Ayyub's pain was dismissed by some as punishment for sin. Have you ever assumed that someone's suffering is due to their own fault? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your assumptions about others' trials?
?
The verse ends with praising Ayyub's patience. In your own life, when you are in hardship, do you complain in a way that could be misinterpreted, or do you turn to Allah with patience and return to Him repeatedly (awwab)? What would your speech reveal about your faith?

Shu'ayb's Former Religion: Abrogation Not Disbelief
Razi explains that Shu'ayb's former religion was abrogated, not false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Shu'ayb's statement 'Allah saved us from it' does not imply he was previously a disbeliever, but refers to a true religion that was later abrogated.

Core Idea
A true religion can be abrogated; Shu'ayb's refusal shows obedience, not sin.

Imam Razi addresses a subtle objection: if Shu'ayb says Allah saved him from his former religion, doesn't that mean he was once a disbeliever? The answer hinges on the concept of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic theology. This lesson unpacks how a valid divine law can be superseded, clearing any doubt about the prophets' innocence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall sense.
Pay attention to the phrase 'after Allah saved us from it'.
2
Identify the objection
The disbelievers demand Shu'ayb return to their religion. Shu'ayb refuses, saying Allah saved him from it. The apparent problem is that this sounds like an admission of former disbelief.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi explains that Shu'ayb's former religion was a true divine law that was later abrogated. After abrogation, it is no longer valid, so returning to it would be wrong.
The key concept is naskh (abrogation).
4
Reflect on theological significance
This explanation preserves the sinlessness of prophets and shows how rational argumentation (kalam) can resolve apparent scriptural difficulties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الثامنة : قصة شعيب عليه السلام فإنه تعالى حكى عن الكفار أنهم قالوا لَنُخْرِجَنَّكَ يَا شُعَيْبُ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا مَعَكَ مِن قَرْيَتِنَا أَوْ لَتَعُودُنَّ فِي مِلَّتِنَا قَالَ أَوَلَوْ كُنَّا كَارِهِينَ قَدِ افْتَرَيْنَا عَلَى اللَّهِ كَذِبًا إِن عُدْنَا فِي مِلَّتِكُم بَعْدَ إِذْ نَجَّانَا اللَّهُ مِنْهَا [الأعراف: ۸۸ - ۸۹] وهذا اعتراف من شعيب بأن الله خلصه من ملته التي هي الكفر، وأيضًا: امتنع من العود إلى تلك الملة، وهذا يدل على أنه كان فيها. والجواب: يحتمل أن تلك الملة كانت حقة، ثم إن شعيبا كان عليها ثم إنه تعالى نسخها بشريعة شعيب، ثم إن القوم كانوا يطالبون شعيبا بأن يعود إليها.
The eighth story: The story of Shu'ayb, peace be upon him. For Allah the Exalted narrated from the disbelievers that they said: 'We will surely drive you out, O Shu'ayb, and those who have believed with you from our town, or you must return to our religion.' He said: 'Even if we were averse? We would have invented against Allah a lie if we returned to your religion after Allah had saved us from it' [al-A'raf: 88-89]. And this is an admission from Shu'ayb that Allah saved him from his religion which is disbelief. Also, he refrained from returning to that religion, and this indicates that he was in it. The answer: It is possible that that religion was true, and then Shu'ayb was upon it, then Allah the Exalted abrogated it with the shari'ah of Shu'ayb, and then the people were demanding that Shu'ayb return to it.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Prophet Shu'ayb's words imply he was previously a disbeliever, since he says 'after Allah saved us from it.' The answer explains that the 'religion' he was saved from was a true, divine religion that was later abrogated. Shu'ayb followed it before his own prophethood, and after abrogation, it became invalid. So when the people demanded he return to it, he refused because it was no longer Allah's law. Thus, no sin is implied.

Yusuf's Silence: Divine Wisdom in Trial
Razi answers the objection that Yusuf should have informed his father of his survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophets act only by divine command, and that apparent hardships may be part of a greater divine plan.

Core Idea
Prophets obey Allah even in painful silence.

The objection raises a natural human question: why wouldn't Yusuf relieve his father's grief by sending news? Razi's answer is terse but profound — it points to divine command. This lesson unpacks that brief exchange, showing how kalam defends prophetic actions through submission to Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a question ('why did he not...?') followed by a short answer ('perhaps God forbade him').
The answer uses 'لعل' (perhaps), which signals a likely reason, not certainty.
2
Understand the objection's force
The objection assumes that a prophet should naturally seek to relieve suffering. It challenges Yusuf's behavior as seeming heartless or negligent.
This reflects a common human expectation about prophetic conduct.
3
Analyze the answer's logic
Razi's answer invokes divine prohibition and a greater wisdom: intensifying Jacob's trial. This reframes the action as obedience, not neglect.
The trial of Jacob is seen as a means of spiritual elevation.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
This passage teaches that prophetic actions are not judged by human norms alone; they are bound by revelation. What seems cruel may be mercy in disguise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : لِمَ لم يُعْلِم أباه بخبره حتى يزول غمه؟ والجواب : لعل الله تعالى نهاه عن ذلك تشديدا للأمر على يعقوب.
The sixth objection: Why did he not inform his father of his news so that his grief would be removed? The answer: Perhaps God Almighty forbade him from that, to intensify the matter for Jacob.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector asks why Yusuf (Joseph) did not inform his father Ya'qub (Jacob) that he was alive, which would have relieved his grief. Razi answers that it was likely due to a divine prohibition, and God's wisdom in intensifying the trial for Jacob.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Yusuf was heartless or negligent in not informing his father.
Correction: Razi clarifies that Yusuf's action (or inaction) was by divine command, and the trial was part of Allah's greater plan.

Checkpoint

A) Yusuf forgot to do so.
B) God forbade him from that to intensify the trial on Jacob.
C) Yusuf was angry with his father.

Key Takeaway
The silence of a prophet may be a command from Allah, and apparent harshness can be a divine mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Seventh Objection on Yusuf: Prostration Explained
Razi answers an objection on Yusuf's prostration and reinterprets the dream.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi resolves the apparent problem of prostration to Yusuf by reinterpreting the act and the dream.

Core Idea
The prostration was to God in gratitude, not to Yusuf.

This lesson tackles a classic objection concerning the story of Yusuf: how could his parents prostrate to him? Razi provides a nuanced answer that harmonizes the verse with the principle of tawhid. Through careful analysis of the Quranic context, he shows that the prostration was an act of worship directed solely to God, with Yusuf as the cause for gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection presented: prostration is for God alone, and using parents as servants is not allowed. Notice the two specific reasons given.
This is a typical kalam objection based on apparent contradiction with core beliefs.
2
Examine Razi's answer
Razi says the prostration was actually to God for Yusuf's sake (i.e., in gratitude). Understand how this resolves the first objection and the second objection about employing parents.
The answer reframes the act as an expression of thanks, not servitude.
3
Consider the sub-objection
A potential counter-argument is raised: Yusuf's dream interpretation seems to conflict with this answer. Read the sub-objection carefully.
The sub-objection connects the prostration verse to the earlier dream.
4
Analyze the final counter-answer
Razi denies that the dream's interpretation is about prostration to him; rather, it signifies high rank. See how this fits with the parents' noble state.
This completes the defense by harmonizing both verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَفَعَ أَبَوَيْهِ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ وَخَرُوا لَهُ سُجَدَا﴾ [يوسف: ١٠٠] فكيف رضي بأن يسجدوا له؟ وذلك محذور من وجهين: الأول: أن السجود لغير الله لا يجوز الثاني: أن استخدام الأبوين لا يجوز. والجواب: المعنى وخروا لأجله سجدًا لله، فإن من فقد ولده ثم وجده فإنه يسجد لله تعالى شكرًا لنعمة الجدان. لا يقال: هذا التأويل يدفعه قوله تعالى: ﴿قَالَ يَا أَبَتِ هَذَا تَأْوِيلُ رُؤْيَايَ مِن قَبْلُ قَدْ جَعَلَهَا رَبِّي حَقًّا﴾ [يوسف: ١٠٠] وتلك الرؤيا هي قوله: ﴿إِنِّي رَأَيْتُ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ كَوْكَبًا وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ رَأَيْتُهُمْ لِي سَاجِدِينَ﴾ [يوسف: ٤]. لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن تأويل رؤياه ما ذكرتم بل تأويل رؤياه بلوغه إلى أرفع المنازل، ولما رأى أبويه على أشرف الأحوال في الدارين كان ذلك مصداقا لرؤياه المتقدمة.
The seventh objection: His statement: {And he raised his parents upon the throne and they fell down before him prostrating} [Yusuf: 100]. How could he be pleased that they prostrate to him? This is objectionable from two aspects: First, prostrating to other than God is not permissible. Second, employing one's parents is not permissible. The answer: The meaning is that they fell down prostrating to God for his sake, for one who loses his child and then finds him prostrates to God Almighty in gratitude for the blessing of reunion. It is not said: This interpretation is contradicted by His statement: {He said: O my father, this is the interpretation of my dream from before; my Lord has made it truth} [Yusuf: 100], and that dream is his statement: {Indeed I saw eleven stars and the sun and the moon; I saw them prostrating to me} [Yusuf: 4]. But we say: We do not concede that the interpretation of his dream is what you mentioned; rather the interpretation of his dream is his attainment to the highest stations, and when he saw his parents in the most honorable states in both realms, that was a verification of his earlier dream.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the prostration of Yusuf's parents to him in the Quranic story. The objector cites two reasons why this is problematic: prostration is only for God, and using parents as servants is not allowed. Razi answers that the prostration was actually to God in gratitude, with Yusuf as the occasion. Then a sub-objection is raised: the interpretation of Yusuf's dream (where the stars, sun, and moon prostrated to him) contradicts this interpretation. Razi responds by reinterpreting the dream: it signifies Yusuf reaching high rank, and seeing his parents in a noble state fulfills that dream. The lesson shows a typical kalam method of handling scriptural difficulties: lexical reinterpretation and appeal to broader context.

Key Terms
السجود
التأويل
الرؤيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the prostration was directly to Yusuf as an act of worship.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the prostration was to God in gratitude, not to Yusuf.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what was the real object of the prostration in the story of Yusuf?
A) Yusuf himself.
B) God Almighty.
C) The throne of Egypt.
D) Jacob and Rachel.

Key Takeaway
Razi's method shows how scriptural difficulties are resolved through reinterpretation while preserving core theological principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to contradict a core Islamic belief, do you rush to assume a literal interpretation, or do you seek a deeper understanding as Razi did? What does your approach reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom? This week, when you face a confusing passage, pause and ask yourself: 'Could there be another interpretation that upholds Allah's perfection?'
?
The prostration was an act of gratitude for being reunited with Yusuf. Think of a blessing you received recently—did it move you to physically humble yourself before Allah in gratitude? If not, what is holding you back? Commit to a specific act of sujūd shukr (prostration of gratitude) the next time you experience a joy.

Prophet Ayyub's Torment: Punishment or Devil's Whisper?
Razi answers an objection about Ayyub's torment implying sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Prophet Ayyub's sinlessness by showing that his reported 'torment' refers to the devil's whisperings, not punishment for a sin.

Core Idea
Ayyub's torment was not punishment but devilish whisperings, because God praised him in the same verse.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the infallibility of prophets based on the story of Ayyub. The objector argues that Ayyub's complaint to God about Satan's torment implies a prior sin. Imam Razi responds by pointing to the immediate Quranic context where God praises Ayyub, clarifying that the torment is not retributive but rather unsettling whisperings from the devil.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objector's claim and Razi's counter-argument.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Understand the logic of the objection
The objector equates 'torment' (عذاب) with punishment (عقاب), which only comes after sin. If Ayyub experienced torment, he must have sinned.
This is a common logical inference: cause and effect.
3
Analyze Razi's contextual proof
Notice how Razi uses the end of the verse to override the beginning. He argues that since God praised Ayyub, the entire verse must be commendatory, so the torment cannot be blameworthy punishment.
This is a principle of contextual consistency in Quranic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة السابعة : قصة أيوب الكلية وهي أنه تعالى حكى عنه أنه قال: ﴿إِنِّي مَسَّنِيَ الشَّيْطَانُ بِنُصْبٍ وَعَذَابٍ﴾ [ص: ٤١] العذاب لا يكون إلا من الذنب كالعقاب وهذا يدل على تقدم الذنب. والجواب: أنه تعالى مدحه في آخر الآية بقوله: ﴿نِعْمَ الْعَبْدُ إِنَّهُ أَوَّابٌ﴾ [ص: ٤٤] وإذا كان آخر الآية مدحًا امتنع أن يكون أول الآية ذما، فثبت أن المراد من العذاب تلك الوساوس الموحشة التي كان يلقيها الشيطان في قلبه.
The seventh story: The story of Ayyub (Job) in its entirety. He (God) narrated from him that he said: {Indeed, Satan has touched me with hardship and torment} [Sad: 41]. Torment only comes from sin, like punishment, and this indicates the precedence of sin. The answer: He (God) praised him at the end of the verse with His statement: {How excellent a servant! Indeed, he was a frequent returner} [Sad: 44]. And when the end of the verse is praise, it is impossible for the beginning to be blame. Thus it is established that what is meant by torment is those terrifying whisperings which Satan used to cast into his heart.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector argues that Ayyub's statement 'Satan has touched me with torment' implies that the torment is a punishment for sin, so Ayyub must have committed a sin. Razi answers by pointing to the same chapter (Sad) where God praises Ayyub at the end of the passage. Since the overall context is praise, the 'torment' cannot be punishment; rather it refers to the devil's whisperings that caused him distress. This shows Razi's use of contextual consistency in Quranic interpretation.

Key Terms
العذاب
الذنب
الوساوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Ayyub's torment was punishment for a sin he committed.
Correction: Razi shows that the torment was the devil's whisperings, as God praised Ayyub in the same verse, so it cannot be punishment.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi deny that Ayyub's torment was punishment for sin?
A) Because Ayyub was not a prophet at that time.
B) Because God praised Ayyub in the same passage, making blame impossible.
C) Because the devil cannot cause punishment.
D) Because Ayyub repented before the torment.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends Ayyub's sinlessness by reinterpreting his 'torment' as devilish whisperings, using the contextual praise in the same verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a severe trial, do you immediately assume it is due to a sin you committed? How does Ayyub's example challenge that assumption? Will you now pause and consider that it might be a test or a whisper from Satan, and what will you do differently next time hardship strikes?
?
The verse ends with God praising Ayyub as 'a frequent returner' (أواب). Is your response to hardship turning you towards God in repentance or away from Him in despair? What one concrete change will you make today to become a frequent returner when you face difficulties?
?
If you were to confess your trials to God as Ayyub did, would you blame yourself for sins you think caused them? Or can you trust that God's praise of you might be present even in your lament? Are you willing to let go of self-condemnation and instead see your trials as whisperings to be overcome by turning back to Him?

Shu'ayb's Objection and the Principle of Abrogation
Razi answers the objection that Shu'ayb's statement implies previous idolatry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Prophet Shu'ayb against the objection that he was once an idolater by invoking the principle of abrogation (naskh).

Core Idea
Prophets are never idolaters; former true religions can be abrogated.

In this passage, Razi addresses a potential objection against the prophethood of Shu'ayb. The disbelievers' words in the Quran seem to imply that Shu'ayb had previously followed their religion (idolatry). Razi explains that this is not the case: Shu'ayb's former religion was a true one, later abrogated by his own mission. This lesson clarifies the theological use of abrogation to defend prophetic integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Read the verse from Surah Al-A'raf (88-89) that the objector uses. Notice the phrase 'after God had saved us from it'.
The verse is the basis of the objection.
2
Identify the objection
Razi states that the disbelievers' claim implies Shu'ayb was once in their religion. Understand why this is problematic.
The objection assumes 'their religion' is idolatry.
3
Study Razi's answer
Razi's answer uses abrogation: that religion was true but later superseded. This explains Shu'ayb's words without implying idolatry.
The key term is 'naskh' (abrogation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الثامنة : قصة شعيب من فإنه تعالى حكى عن الكفار أنهم قالوا: ﴿لَنُخْرِجَنَّكَ يَا شُعَيْبُ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مَعَكَ مِن قَرْيَتِنَا أَوْ لَتَعُودُنَّ فِي مِلَّتِنَا قَالَ أَوَلَوْ كُنَّا كَارِهِينَ قَدِ افْتَرَيْنَا عَلَى اللَّهِ كَذِبًا إِنْ عُدْنَا فِي مِلَّتِكُم بَعْدَ إِذْ نَجَّانَا اللَّهُ مِنْهَا﴾ [الأعراف: ٨٨-٨٩]. وهذا اعتراف من شعيب بأن الله تعالى خلّصه من الكفر، وأيضًا: امتنع من العود إلى تلك الملة، وهذا يدل على أنه كان فيها. والجواب: يحتمل لأن تلك الملة كانت حقة، ثم إن شعيبا كان عليها ثم إنه تعالى نسخها بشريعة شعيب، ثم إن القوم كانوا يطالبون شعيبا بأن يعود إليها.
The eighth story: The story of Shu'ayb. God narrated from the disbelievers that they said: {We will surely drive you out, O Shu'ayb, and those who have believed with you from our city, or you must return to our religion. He said: Even if we were averse? We would have invented against God a lie if we returned to your religion after God had saved us from it} [Al-A'raf: 88-89]. This is an admission from Shu'ayb that God saved him from disbelief, and also he refused to return to that religion, and this indicates that he was in it. The answer: It is possible that that religion was true, then Shu'ayb was upon it, then God abrogated it with the shari'a of Shu'ayb, then the people were demanding Shu'ayb to return to it.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the prophet Shu'ayb. The objector claims that Shu'ayb's words in the Quran imply he was previously an idolater because he says 'after God had saved us from it' and refuses to return to their religion. Razi answers that the religion from which Shu'ayb was saved was a true religion (likely the religion of Abraham or earlier prophets) that was later abrogated by his own prophecy. Thus, Shu'ayb was not an idolater; rather his earlier religion was legitimate, and upon receiving a new shari'a, he left it. The lesson illustrates the principle of abrogation (naskh) in prophethood.

Key Terms
الملة
النسخ
الخلاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shu'ayb was an idolater before prophethood.
Correction: Razi clarifies that Shu'ayb's former religion was a true one, later abrogated, not idolatry.

Checkpoint
How does Razi explain that Shu'ayb's former religion was not idolatry?
A) Shu'ayb never followed any religion before.
B) That former religion was true but later abrogated.
C) The verse is metaphorical.
D) Shu'ayb was forced to follow them.

Key Takeaway
The principle of abrogation (naskh) allows for a prophet to have followed a previous true religion without implying idolatry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The disbelievers twisted Shu'ayb's words to accuse him of idolatry. When have you misinterpreted someone's sincere words because of your own assumptions? How can you train yourself to seek the truth rather than confirm your biases?
?
Shu'ayb refused to return to the old religion even though it was once true. Is there a 'comfortable old way' in your life—a habit, a relationship, or a mindset—that God is asking you to leave behind for His sake? What step will you take today to move forward?

First Objection on Musa: Killing the Coptic
Razi answers the objection that Moses killed a man before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends prophetic infallibility by distinguishing between actions before and after prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible only after prophethood.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the sinlessness of prophets: Moses killed a man before his prophethood. The objector presents a dilemma: if the man deserved death, why did Moses repent? If not, Moses sinned. Razi's answer clarifies the timeline and introduces the possibility of a mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection, dilemma, answer.
The objector cites Quranic verses to support his objection.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objector presents two possibilities: either the Coptic deserved death or not. Trace how each horn leads to a problem if Moses was a prophet.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi gives two points: the event was before prophethood, and it may have been a mistake. Consider why these answers resolve the objection.
The mistake is considered commendable when followed by repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : التمسك بقوله: ﴿فَوَكَزَهُ مُوسَى فَقَضَى عَلَيْهِ﴾ [القصص: ١٥] فنقول : ذلك القبطي إما أن يقال: إنه كان مستحقا للقتل أو ما كان كذلك، فإن كان الأول فلم قال موسى: ﴿هَذَا مِنْ عَمَلِ الشَّيْطَانِ﴾ [القصص: ١٥] ولم قال: ﴿رَبِّ إِنِّي ظَلَمْتُ نَفْسِي فَاغْفِرْ لِي﴾ [القصص: ١٦] ولم قال: ﴿فَعَلْتُهَا إِذًا وَأَنَا مِنَ الضَّالِّينَ﴾ [الشعراء: ٢٠] وإن قلنا إنه ما كان مستحق القتل كان ذلك ذنبا ومعصية. والجواب : أن هذه الواقعة كانت قبل النبوة، وأيضًا فلعله قتله خطأ، والحسن المندوب هو الخطأ والاستغفار عنه.
The first objection: Adherence to His statement: {Then Moses struck him and finished him} [Al-Qasas: 15]. We say: That Coptic man, either it is said he was deserving of killing or not. If the first, then why did Moses say: {This is from the work of Satan} [Al-Qasas: 15], and why did he say: {My Lord, indeed I have wronged myself, so forgive me} [Al-Qasas: 16], and why did he say: {I did it then while I was of the astray} [Ash-Shu'ara': 20]? And if we say he was not deserving of killing, then that was a sin and disobedience. The answer: That incident was before prophethood, and also perhaps he killed him by mistake, and the commendable good is the mistake and asking forgiveness for it.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector uses the story of Moses killing an Egyptian (Coptic) man. They present a dilemma: either the man deserved death or not. If he did, why did Moses express regret and call it Satan's work? If he did not, then Moses sinned. Razi answers that the event occurred before Moses' prophethood, so it is not a reflection on his prophetic sinlessness. Additionally, it may have been a mistake, and seeking forgiveness for a mistake is commendable. This shows the Kalam principle that prophets are infallible only after prophethood.

الخطأ
الاستغفار

Misconception: Moses sinned as a prophet by killing the Egyptian.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the event occurred before prophethood, so it does not violate prophetic infallibility.

A) The Egyptian was a disbeliever deserving death.
B) Moses was not yet a prophet at the time.
C) Moses was commanded by God to kill him.
D) The killing was accidental.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that actions before prophethood do not affect a prophet's infallibility after prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time before you were guided or before you took your faith seriously. Did you ever do something you now regret? How does knowing that even prophets were not infallible before prophethood change how you view your own past mistakes? Will you stop letting shame from your past hold you back from seeking closeness to Allah?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim who has recently repented from a major sin, do you still judge them based on their past? This lesson shows that Allah accepted Musa's mistake and forgave him. Are you as quick to forgive others as Allah is? Commit to letting go of one person's past today.
?
The objector in this lesson tried to use Musa's pre-prophethood action to undermine his authority. Are there areas in your own life where you hold someone's past against them, ignoring that they have changed? Ask yourself: is my judgment based on who they are now or who they used to be? Resolve to treat them according to their present state.

Second Objection on Musa: Reluctance to Prophethood
Razi answers the objection that Moses' request for help shows reluctance to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that Moses' request for help indicates reluctance to prophethood.

Core Idea
Moses did not refuse prophethood; he asked for support.

This passage presents a common objection against Moses' prophethood: that his words 'I fear they will deny me' sound like an excuse to avoid the mission. Razi clarifies that Moses was not making an excuse but rather requesting divine assistance through his brother Aaron. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of prophetic requests.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Understand the objection
The objector quotes Moses' words from the Quran and claims they show 'istifaa' (excuse) from the message. Identify the key term 'استعفاء'.
Istifaa means seeking to be excused or released from a duty.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi denies that it is an excuse. Instead, he says it is a request for God to include his brother as a helper. Notice the word 'معين' (helper).
The answer shifts the interpretation from refusal to a request for support.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters. Asking for help does not imply unwillingness; it shows awareness of the task's difficulty and desire for support.
This protects the integrity of prophetic character.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنِّي أَخَافُ﴾ [الشعراء: ١٠] فلم قال موسى: ﴿إِنِّي أَخَافُ أَن يُكَذِّبُونِ وَيَضِيقُ صَدْرِي وَلَا يَنطَلِقُ لِسَانِي﴾ [الشعراء: ١٢-١٣] وهذا استعفاء من رسالته. والجواب : ليس هذا استعفاء من الرسالة، ولكنه طلب من الله أن يضم إليه أخاه في الرسالة ليكون معينا له على مهمات الرسالة.
The second objection: God said: {Indeed I fear} [Ash-Shu'ara': 10]. Moses said: {Indeed I fear that they will deny me and my breast will tighten and my tongue will not be loosed} [Ash-Shu'ara': 12-13]. This is an excuse from his message. The answer: This is not an excuse from the message, but it is a request to God to join his brother to him in the message to be a helper for him in the tasks of the message.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector argues that Moses' words show he tried to avoid prophethood by making excuses. Razi responds that Moses was not declining prophethood; rather, he was asking God to include his brother Aaron as a co-prophet to assist him. The request for help is not a refusal, but a desire for support in fulfilling the mission.

Key Terms

الرسالة
المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' request for help shows he was reluctant and tried to avoid prophethood.
Correction: Razi explains it was a request for assistance, not an avoidance of duty.

Checkpoint

A) As a sign of weakness.
B) As a valid excuse to avoid prophethood.
C) As a request for assistance in the mission.
D) As a test from God.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that Moses' request for his brother's help is not an excuse but a request for support in his prophetic mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a daunting task from Allah, do you immediately interpret your own fears and requests for help as a sign of weakness or unwillingness, rather than as a legitimate seeking of support? How can Moses' example change your perspective?
?
Are you quick to judge others' requests for assistance as excuses, just as the objector did with Moses? Challenge yourself to think of a time when you assumed someone was avoiding responsibility when they were actually seeking the means to fulfill it better.
?
Moses asked for his brother to help him. In your own life, when Allah commands you to do something difficult, have you honestly asked Him for helpers, or do you try to go alone out of pride? What practical step can you take today to seek support, as Moses did?

Third Objection on Moses: The Condition in the Command
Razi answers the objection that Moses permitted magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Moses' command by showing it implied a condition of truthfulness.

Core Idea
Moses' command was a challenge, not a permission.

This short passage addresses an objection: didn't Moses give permission to the magicians to show magic? Razi's answer is subtle: the command was conditional. By comparing it to the Qur'anic challenge, he shows that Moses was testing the magicians' claim, not endorsing their acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify the condition
The answer says the command was مشروط (conditional). Find the implicit condition: 'if you are truthful'.
3
See the parallel to the Qur'an
Razi compares to verse 2:23 which challenges to produce a surah conditionally. Understand how this parallel supports the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : ولم قال موسى: ﴿أَلْقُوا مَا أَنْتُم مُلْقُونَ﴾ [يونس: ٨٠] وهذا إذن لهم في إظهار السحر؟ والجواب: كان ذلك الأمر مشروطا والتقدير: ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون إن كنتم محقين، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ وَادْعُوا شُهَدَاءَكُم مِّن دُونِ اللَّهِ إِن كُنْتُمْ صَدِقِينَ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٣].

The answer: That command was conditional, and the implicit meaning is: Cast what you will cast if you are truthful, as in God's statement: {Then bring a surah like it and call your witnesses besides God if you are truthful} [Al-Baqarah: 23].
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector claims that Moses' command to the magicians to cast their ropes and staffs was a permission to perform magic. Razi answers that the phrase implicitly includes a condition: 'if you are truthful.' Just as in the Qur'anic challenge to produce a surah, it is conditional upon truthfulness. Thus Moses was not endorsing magic but challenging them to prove their claim.

السحر
الشرط

Correction: Razi shows the command was conditional, challenging the magicians' truthfulness.

C) That they must not harm anyone.

Key Takeaway

Fourth Objection on Musa: Fear of Magic
Razi answers the objection that Moses' fear implies doubt in his miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the Prophet Moses' certainty against the objection that his fear implied doubt about his miracle.

Core Idea
Fear of others' deception is not doubt of divine power.

The objection claims that Moses' internal fear shows he doubted his own miracle. But Razi provides a nuanced answer: Moses was not afraid of losing to magic, but worried that the magicians' impressive deception might confuse ignorant onlookers. This lesson explores that argument and its defense of prophetic certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection and Razi's response.
Focus on the verse citations.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector argues that fear (khīfa) necessarily implies doubt (shakk) about the miracle. Ask yourself why this assumption seems logical.
This is a common assumption in some theological circles.
3
See Razi's reinterpretation
Razi reinterprets the fear as concern for the ignorant, not doubt. He argues that Moses was afraid the magic might confuse some people, not that he doubted God's power.
Notice how Razi shifts the focus from Moses' inner state to his concern for others.
4
Recognize God's reassurance
God's words 'Fear not; indeed you are the superior' are the response to Moses' concern, affirming that his miracle will prevail.
This reinforces that the miracle itself was never in doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة قوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَوْجَسَ فِي نَفْسِهِ خِيفَةً مُوسَى﴾ [طه: ٦٧] وهذا يقتضي كونه شاكًا فيما أتى به. والجواب : لعله إنما خاف لأنه رأى من قوة التلبيس ما أشفق عنده من بقاء تلك الشبهات في قلوب بعض الجهال فأمنه الله تعالى منه وبين حجته للقوم، وهو المراد بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تَخَفْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْأَعْلَى﴾ [طه: ٦٨].
The fourth objection: His statement: {And Moses felt within himself a fear} [Ta-Ha: 67]. This necessitates that he was doubtful about what he had brought. The answer: Perhaps he feared because he saw the power of deception that made him apprehensive for the persistence of those doubts in the hearts of some ignorant people. So God reassured him and made his proof clear to the people, and that is what is meant by His statement: {Fear not; indeed you are the superior} [Ta-Ha: 68].
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector argues that Moses' internal fear shows he doubted his own miracle. Razi reinterprets the fear: Moses was not doubting God's power, but he was concerned that the magicians' strong deception might affect the ignorant onlookers. Thus, God reassured him that he would prevail. This explanation preserves Moses' certainty while acknowledging a natural concern for the audience's faith.

Key Terms
الخيفة
التلبيس
الشبهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a prophet feels fear, it means he doubts his mission.
Correction: Fear can be a natural human reaction to external circumstances (like concern for others' faith) without affecting inner certainty about God's promise.

Checkpoint
What was the reason for Moses' fear according to Razi?
A) He doubted his own mission.
B) He feared the magicians would harm him.
C) He was concerned that the magic might confuse ignorant people.
D) He was afraid of Pharaoh's punishment.

Key Takeaway
Moses' fear was not doubt in his miracle but concern for the effects of deception on the weak-minded, and God's reassurance affirmed his superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see others being deceived by false arguments about Islam, does your fear focus on their weakness or on your own uncertainty? What would it look like to truly trust that God's proof is superior, like Moses did?
?
God told Moses, 'Fear not; you are the superior.' In what area of your life—faith, work, relationships—do you need to hear that same command and promise from God today? How will you act on it?
?
The objector assumed fear equaled doubt. Have you ever judged your own or someone else's fear as a lack of faith? What change in perspective would help you see fear as concern rather than confusion?

First Objection: Moses' Killing of the Copt
Razi presents the first objection about Moses killing the Copt and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the prophethood of Moses (as) regarding his killing of the Copt, and how Imam al-Razi refutes it by noting it occurred before prophethood and was possibly accidental.

Core Idea
Moses' killing of the Copt before prophethood does not invalidate his prophethood.

This lesson examines the first of six objections (shubahat) against the prophethood of Moses (peace be upon him) presented by Razi in his work al-Arba'in. The objection focuses on the incident of Moses killing a Copt, as mentioned in the Quran. Razi presents a dilemma and then provides a clear answer that resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing deeply. Then read the English translation to understand the overall structure of the objection and answer.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses cited.
2
Identify the dilemma
Notice that Razi frames a dilemma: either the Copt deserved death or not. Both alternatives seem to raise problems for Moses' prophethood. Write down the two horns of the dilemma.
This is the core of the objection.
3
Study the answer
Read the answer carefully. Razi gives two reasons: the incident was before prophethood, and it might have been an accident. Understand how these reasons address the dilemma.
Consider why 'before prophethood' is relevant.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this answer resolves the objection. How does it preserve the integrity of Moses' prophethood?
This connects to the concept of 'ismah (infallibility) only after prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The answer states that the incident occurred before Moses was made a prophet, and also that it could have been an accidental killing, thus neither position requires fault on Moses' part after prophethood.

?

?

The Second Objection: Moses' Request for Support
Razi refutes the claim that Moses' request for his brother implied avoidance of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between seeking exemption from prophethood and requesting assistance for its duties.

Core Idea
Asking for help is not the same as asking to be excused.

This lesson tackles a common objection raised against the prophethood of Moses: why did he express fear and ask for his brother's help if he truly accepted prophethood? Imam Razi clarifies that Moses was not trying to avoid the mission but rather sought reinforcement to fulfill it effectively. We will examine the objection and the precise response.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the Quranic verses cited in the objection and note how they seem to suggest Moses' reluctance.
The objection uses Moses' own words against him.
2
Understand the misconception
Realize that the objector interprets 'fear' and 'tightness' as signs of unwillingness to accept prophethood.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
See how Razi reframes the request: Moses asked for his brother to be made a helper, not for exemption.
The key term 'istif'a' (seeking exemption) is explicitly denied.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how seeking assistance is a virtuous act, not a sign of weakness, even for prophets.
This teaches us about prophetic character and reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: قال الله تعالى: ﴿ اذْهَبْ إِلَى الْقَوْمِ الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [الشعراء: ١٠] فلم قال موسى: ﴿ إِنِّي أَخَافُ أَن يُكَذِّبُونِ وَيَضِيقُ صَدْرِي وَلَا يَنطَلِقُ لِسَانِي ﴾ [الشعراء: ١٢-١٣] وهذا استعفاء من رسالته. والجواب: ليس هذا استعفاء من الرسالة، ولكنه طلب من الله أن يضم إليه أخاه في الرسالة ليكون معينا له على مهمات الرسالة.
The second objection: Allah Almighty said, 'Go to the wrongdoing people' [Ash-Shu'ara 26:10]. So why did Moses say, 'Indeed, I fear that they will deny me and my breast will become tight and my tongue will not be fluent' [Ash-Shu'ara 26:12-13]? And this is an exemption from his message. The answer: This is not an exemption from the message, but rather a request to Allah that He include his brother with him in the message to be a helper for him in the tasks of the message.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which claims that Moses' words expressing fear and asking for his brother's assistance indicate he was trying to avoid prophethood. The answer refutes that, explaining that Moses was not seeking exemption but rather asking for reinforcement—namely, that his brother Aaron be appointed as a helper to share the burden of delivering the message.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' request for his brother's help shows he wanted to avoid prophethood.
Correction: Moses was asking for support to fulfill the mission, not to be excused from it.

How does Razi interpret Moses' request for his brother's involvement?
A) As a sign of weakness in faith.
B) As a desire to avoid the mission.
C) As a request for assistance in the mission.
D) As a test from Allah.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request for his brother's help was a request for support, not an attempt to avoid prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel overwhelmed by a responsibility from Allah, do you interpret your need for help as a weakness or as wisdom? What would change if you saw it as a means of support, just as Moses did?
?
Think of a time you were given a task from Allah (like enjoining good or forbidding evil) and you felt your heart tighten. Did you seek help from others, or did you stay silent? How can you follow Moses' example starting today?
?
If someone misunderstood your request for help as unwillingness, how would you respond? More importantly, do you ever misjudge others who ask for support? Will you change your perspective now?

Moses' Conditional Challenge to the Sorcerers
Razi clarifies that Moses' command was conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Moses' command to the sorcerers was a conditional challenge, not an endorsement of sorcery.

Core Idea
Permission with condition is not endorsement.

This lesson addresses the third objection against Moses' prophethood: why did he tell the sorcerers to throw their ropes? Imam Razi clarifies that the command was conditional—'if you are truthful'—similar to the Quranic challenge to produce a surah. This shows Moses was not sanctioning falsehood but issuing a test of truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection and how Razi responds with the concept of conditionality.
Focus on the word 'شرط' (condition) in the Arabic.
2
Compare with the Quranic parallel
Razi draws a parallel to the Quranic challenge in Surah Al-Baqarah. Compare the structure: both are conditional challenges meant to expose falsehood.
Reflect on how conditionality transforms the meaning of the permission.
3
Draw a lesson for prophetic proofs
Consider how this reasoning defends Moses from the charge of permitting sorcery. What does it teach about how prophets challenge falsehood?
This shows a common method in kalam: interpreting ambiguous actions by their context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: ولم قال موسى: ﴿ أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ ﴾ [يونس: ٨٠] وهذا إذن لهم في إظهار السحر؟ والجواب: كان ذلك الأمر مشروطا والتقدير: ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون إن كنتم محقين، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ وَادْعُوا شُهَدَاءَكُم مِّن دُونِ اللَّهِ إِن كُنتُمْ صَدِقِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٣].
The third objection: And why did Moses say, 'Throw whatever you will throw' [Yunus 10:80]? And this is permission for them to display magic. The answer: That command was conditional, and the interpretation is: 'Throw whatever you will throw, if you are truthful,' as in the statement of Allah Almighty: 'Then bring a surah like it and call upon your witnesses besides Allah, if you should be truthful' [Al-Baqarah 2:23].
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
The third objection suggests that Moses' command to the sorcerers to throw their ropes and staffs implied permission to practice magic. Razi responds that Moses' statement is to be understood as conditional—'if you are truthful'—similar to the Quranic challenge to produce a surah. Thus, it was not an endorsement of falsehood but a challenge.

Misconception: Some think Moses sanctioned sorcery by telling the sorcerers to throw their ropes.
Correction: Moses' permission was conditional on their truthfulness, making it a challenge to prove their claim, not an endorsement of falsehood.

Checkpoint

A) That they do it secretly.
B) That they are truthful.
C) That they use only magic.
D) That they do it after him.

Key Takeaway
Moses' command to the sorcerers was conditional on their truthfulness, proving it was a challenge, not an endorsement of magic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Moses' Fear: Concern for the Ignorant, Not Doubt
Razi explains Moses' fear as concern for the ignorant, not doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the infallibility of prophets by reinterpreting Moses' fear as compassionate concern rather than personal doubt.

Core Idea
Moses' fear was for the ignorant, not for himself.

This lesson addresses a key objection against prophetic infallibility: that Moses' fear, as mentioned in the Quran, implies doubt in his divine mission. Imam Razi provides a compelling answer that transforms this apparent weakness into a sign of Moses' deep care for his people. Read on to see how the passage unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the objection-answer structure.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objection cites Quran 20:67 to imply doubt. Razi's answer reinterprets the fear. Identify the two parts and notice how the Quranic verse 20:68 is used as support.
3
Reflect on the theological point
Ask yourself: What does this interpretation reveal about prophetic certainty? How does Razi protect the doctrine of 'isma?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَأَوْجَسَ فِي نَفْسِهِ خِيفَةً مُوسَى ﴾ [طه: ٦٧] وهذا يقتضي كونه شاكًا فيما أتى به. والجواب: لعله إنما خاف لأنه رأى من قوة التلبيس ما أشفق عنده من بقاء تلك الشبهات في قلوب بعض الجهال فأمنه الله تعالى منه وبين حجته للقوم، وهو المراد بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا تَخَفْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْأَعْلَى ﴾ [طه: ٦٨].
The fourth objection: The statement of Allah Almighty: 'And Moses felt within himself a fear' [Ta-Ha 20:67]. And this implies that he was doubtful about what he brought. The answer: Perhaps he only feared because he saw the strength of deception and was concerned about the persistence of those doubts in the hearts of some ignorant people, so Allah assured him and made clear his proof to the people, and that is meant by His statement: 'And fear not; indeed, you are the superior' [Ta-Ha 20:68].
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
The fourth objection claims that Moses' internal fear, mentioned in the Quran, indicates doubt in his own divine signs. Razi's answer reframes the fear as concern for the ignorant who might be deceived by the sorcerers' illusions, not as doubt in God's power. Allah's reassurance confirms that Moses remained certain.

Key Terms
شبهة
خيفة
شك
تلبيس
جهال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' fear indicates he doubted his own prophethood.
Correction: Razi explains the fear was due to concern that the ignorant might be misled by the sorcerers' deception, not due to doubt.

Checkpoint
What was the real reason for Moses' fear according to Razi?
A) He doubted his own prophethood.
B) He thought his magic was weaker.
C) He feared that the ignorant might be misled by the sorcerers.
D) He was afraid of Pharaoh's army.

Key Takeaway
Moses' fear was a sign of his concern for the weak-minded, not a flaw in his certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Moses' fear was not doubt but compassion. What fears in your life are actually disguised as concern for others when they are really about yourself? How can you distinguish them?

The Fifth Objection: Moses Grabbing Aaron's Beard
Razi defends Moses' action as investigation, not punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends prophetic infallibility from the objection of Moses grabbing Aaron's beard by explaining the action as investigation rather than punishment.

Core Idea
Moses' action was investigation, not anger.

This passage addresses a critical objection against prophetic sinlessness. The apparent harshness of Moses towards Aaron seems to imply one of them sinned. Razi provides a historical context and reinterprets the event, showing that Moses intended to investigate discreetly and Aaron's plea was out of compassion, thus no sin occurred.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection presents a dilemma: either Aaron committed a sin to deserve Moses' action, or Moses himself sinned by his harsh action. Then it adds Aaron's plea as further evidence.
3
Understand the historical context
Razi explains that the Israelites suspected Moses of killing Aaron. This context clarifies why Moses acted as he did.
4
See Moses' intent
Moses took Aaron close to examine the incident, not to punish him. The Arabic phrase 'ليدانيه من نفسه ويتفحص' shows his purpose.
5
Reinterpret Aaron's plea
Aaron's plea was out of compassion (إشفاقًا) to prevent people from thinking Moses intended harm. This dissolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة: قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَلْقَى الْأَلْوَاحَ وَأَخَذَ بِرَأْسِ أَخِيهِ يَجُرُّهُ إِلَيْهِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٥٠] فنقول: إن صدر عن هارون ذنب حتى استحق ذلك التأديب - مع أن هارون كان رسولاً من عند الله - فقد صدر الذنب من الرسول، وإلا كان ذلك الإيذاء من حقه ذنبًا، فعلى التقديرين يلزم صدور الذنب عن النبي، وايضًا قال هارون: ﴿ وَلَا تَأْخُذْ بِلِحْيَتِي وَلَا بِرَأْسِي ﴾ [طه: ٩٤] فإن كان فعل موسى صوابا كان نهي هارون خطأ، وإن كان فعل موسى خطأ فقد حصل المطلوب. والجواب: أن بني إسرائيل كانوا في غاية سوء الظن بموسى حتى إن هارون لما غاب عنهم غيبة فقالوا لموسى: أنت قتلته، فلما وعد الله لموسى ثلاثين ليلة وأتمها بعشر وكتب له في الألواح من كل شيء، ثم رجع موسى فرأى في قومه ما رأى فأخذ برأس أخيه ليدنيه من نفسه، ويتفحص عن كيفية الواقعة، فخاف هارون أن يسبق إلى قلوبهم ما لا أصل له فقال - إشفاقًا على موسى: ﴿ وَلَا تَأْخُذْ بِلِحْيَتِي وَلَا بِرَأْسِي ﴾ لئلا يظن القوم أنك تريد أن تضربني وتؤذيني.
The fifth objection: The statement of Allah Almighty: 'And he threw the tablets and took hold of his brother's head, dragging him toward him' [Al-A'raf 7:150]. We say: If a sin emanated from Aaron such that he deserved that discipline—while Aaron was a messenger from Allah—then a sin has emanated from a messenger. Otherwise, that harming from Moses' right would be a sin. On both suppositions, the occurrence of sin from a prophet follows. Also, Aaron said: 'Do not take hold of my beard nor my head' [Ta-Ha 20:94]. If Moses' action was correct, then Aaron's prohibition was wrong; if Moses' action was wrong, then the desired [conclusion] is obtained. The answer: That the Children of Israel had extreme suspicion of Moses, such that when Aaron was absent for a while they said to Moses, 'You killed him.' When Allah promised Moses thirty nights and completed them with ten and wrote for him in the tablets something of everything, then Moses returned and saw in his people what he saw, so he took hold of his brother's head to draw him close to himself and to examine the circumstances of the incident. Aaron feared that what had no basis would rush into their hearts, so he said—out of compassion for Moses—'Do not take hold of my beard nor my head' so that the people would not think that you intend to strike me and harm me.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection concerning Moses grabbing Aaron by the head and beard. The objection argues that either Aaron sinned to deserve it (implying sin from a prophet) or Moses' action was a sin. Additionally, Aaron's plea seems to contradict Moses' action. The answer provides a detailed narrative: the Israelites suspected Moses of killing Aaron during the absence. When Moses returned and saw the people's state, he took hold of Aaron to bring him close and investigate discreetly. Aaron, fearing that the people might misunderstand Moses' intent, asked not to be taken by the beard and head to avoid any false impression of anger or violence. Thus, no sin occurred, and Aaron's plea was not a rebuke but a precaution.

Key Terms
شبهة
ألواح
هارون
سوء الظن
إشفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The apparent harshness of Moses is misinterpreted as anger; Razi explains it as a caring inquiry.
Correction: Moses' action was not a punishment but a careful investigation to understand the situation, and Aaron's plea was out of compassion to avoid misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
Why did Aaron say 'Do not take hold of my beard nor my head' according to Razi?
A) Because he was angry at Moses.
B) Because he feared the people would think Moses intended to harm him.
C) Because he felt guilty.
D) Because Moses was hurting him.

Key Takeaway
Razi reconciles the apparent harshness by showing Moses' intent was investigation and Aaron's plea was compassion, preserving prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone acting harshly, do you immediately assume the worst about their character, or do you, like Moses, seek to draw near and understand the full context? What will you do the next time you are tempted to judge prematurely?
?
Aaron feared for his brother's reputation more than his own comfort. Is there someone in your life today whose reputation you can protect by carefully choosing your words and actions, even when misunderstood?
?
The Children of Israel allowed suspicion to distort their view of Moses. In what areas of your faith have you allowed suspicion to cloud your trust in Allah's messengers or in your fellow believers, and what step will you take this week to uproot that suspicion?

Moses' Words: Objectionable in Appearance
Razi offers three answers to the objection about Moses calling Khidr's act 'objectionable'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three explanations Razi offers for Moses calling Khidr's actions 'objectionable' even though they were divinely ordained.

Core Idea
What seems objectionable may be permissible upon deeper knowledge.

In the story of Moses and Khidr, Moses appears to criticize Khidr's actions. This objection claims Moses was mistaken. Razi defends Moses by providing three interpretations that reconcile Moses' words with the reality of Khidr's divine mission. Let's explore these interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the First Objection
Read the passage carefully to understand the first sub-question: Moses called Khidr's act 'objectionable' when it was not. Then read the three answers.
2
Identify the Three Answers
List the three different explanations Razi gives. Notice how each one shifts the meaning of 'objectionable'.
They are based on appearance, conditional, and expression of wonder.
3
Reflect on the Principle
Consider why Razi needed to provide multiple interpretations. What does this tell you about the Islamic approach to reconciling prophetic statements with divine actions?
It shows the importance of contextual interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة: قصة موسى والخضر قوله: أما موسى فعلى كلامه سؤالان: السؤال الأول: أن موسى قال للخضر: ﴿ لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئًا نُكْرًا ﴾ [الكهف: ٧٤] و ﴿ شَيْئًا إِمْرًا ﴾ [الكهف: ٧١] وذلك الفعل ما كان منكرًا، فكان كلام موسى خطأ. والجواب من ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: المراد من قوله: ﴿ لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئًا نُكْرًا ﴾ هو أن ظاهره منكر، بمعنى أن من نظر إلى ظاهر هذه الواقعة، ولم يعرف حقيقتها حكم عليها بأنها شيء منكر. والثاني: أنا نضمر حرف الشرط والتقدير: إن كنت قتلته ظلمًا، فقد جئت شيئًا نكرًا. والثالث: المراد من المنكر التعجب، فإن من رأى شيئًا عجيبًا جدًا، فقد يقول: هذا شيء منكر. السؤال الثاني: قال موسى: ﴿ أَقَتَلْتَ نَفْسًا زَكِيَّةً بِغَيْرِ نَفْسٍ ﴾ [الكهف: ٧٤] وتلك النفس ما كانت زكية. والجواب: أن موسى ذكر ذلك على سبيل الاستفهام، لا على سبيل الإخبار، وأيضًا لقد جرى الكلام على الظاهر، وهو جائز؛ لقوله: 'نحن نحكم بالظاهر'.
The sixth objection: The story of Moses and Khidr, his statement: As for Moses, regarding his speech there are two questions: The first question: That Moses said to Khidr: 'You have come with an objectionable thing' [Al-Kahf 18:74] and 'an evil thing' [Al-Kahf 18:71], while that act was not objectionable, so Moses' speech was mistaken. The answer is from three aspects: First: The intended meaning of his statement 'You have come with an objectionable thing' is that its outward appearance is objectionable, meaning that one who looks at the outward appearance of this incident, not knowing its reality, would judge it as an objectionable thing. Second: We imply the conditional particle, the interpretation being: 'If you killed him unjustly, then you have come with an objectionable thing.' Third: The meaning of 'objectionable' is wonder, for one who sees something extremely amazing might say: 'This is an objectionable thing.' The second question: Moses said: 'Did you kill a pure soul without a soul?' [Al-Kahf 18:74] while that soul was not pure. The answer: Moses mentioned that as an interrogative, not as an informative statement. Also, the speech proceeded on the outward appearance, and that is permissible because he said: 'We judge by outward appearances.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers the sixth objection, which has two sub-questions from the story of Moses and Khidr. The first sub-question: Moses accused Khidr of doing something objectionable (killing the boy and scuttling the ship). The answer gives three interpretations: (1) It was objectionable in appearance, not in reality; (2) It was conditional—'if you killed unjustly'; (3) 'Objectionable' here may express astonishment at an extraordinary act. The second sub-question: Moses asked if Khidr killed a 'pure' soul, but the boy was not pure (since he was destined to be a disbeliever). The answer: Moses asked a question; he did not assert it as fact. Also, assessments are based on outward appearances, which is the norm in legal judgments.

Key Terms
شبهة
خضر
نكر
إمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses was factually wrong about Khidr's actions being objectionable.
Correction: Moses' words were contextually valid based on appearance, condition, or expression of wonder.

Checkpoint
According to one of Razi's answers, why did Moses call Khidr's action 'objectionable'?
A) Because Khidr had broken the agreement.
B) Because the action appeared objectionable to an outsider.
C) Because Moses knew the inner reality but spoke sarcastically.
D) Because the act was actually sinful.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that prophetic statements must be interpreted in context, not taken at face value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's action as wrong without knowing their deeper reason? How does Moses' example challenge you to withhold judgment until you understand the full context?
?
When you hear something from a scholar that seems objectionable, do you immediately reject it or try to find a valid interpretation? Which approach reflects more faith?
?
If Moses, a great prophet, could be misunderstood, how much more careful should you be in your own speech? Think of a recent time you spoke without considering the context. What would you do differently now?

Moses' Question: A Pure Soul?
Razi answers the objection about Moses calling the soul 'pure'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Moses' question about killing a 'pure soul' by interpreting it as an interrogative based on outward appearance.

Core Idea
A question is not an assertion; judgments are based on apparent facts.

This part of the sixth objection deals with Moses asking Khidr: 'Did you kill a pure soul (nafs zakiyya) without a soul?' The objector claims the soul was not pure, so Moses was wrong. Razi's answer is twofold: Moses was asking a question, not stating a fact, and he was speaking according to the outward appearance of the boy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Second Objection
Read the passage for the second sub-question: Moses's question about the 'pure soul'.
2
Analyze the Answer
Razi gives two points: first, it was an interrogative (not accusatory); second, judgments are based on outward appearances. Understand each.
The phrase 'We judge by outward appearances' is key.
3
Connect to Islamic Legal Principle
Reflect on how this answer aligns with the Islamic legal maxim that rulings are based on apparent conditions, not hidden realities.
This principle is used in many areas of fiqh.

Second: We imply the conditional particle, the interpretation being: 'If you killed him unjustly, then you have come with an objectionable thing.' Third: The meaning of 'objectionable' is wonder, for one who sees something extremely amazing might say: 'This is an objectionable thing.' The second question: Moses said: 'Did you kill a pure soul without a soul?' [Al-Kahf 18:74] while that soul was not pure. The answer: Moses mentioned that as an interrogative, not as an informative statement. Also, the speech proceeded on the outward appearance, and that is permissible because he said: 'We judge by outward appearances.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers the sixth objection, which has two sub-questions from the story of Moses and Khidr. The first sub-question: Moses accused Khidr of doing something objectionable (killing the boy and scuttling the ship). The answer gives three interpretations: (1) It was objectionable in appearance, not in reality; (2) It was conditional—'if you killed unjustly'; (3) 'Objectionable' here may express astonishment at an extraordinary act. The second sub-question: Moses asked if Khidr killed a 'pure' soul, but the boy was not pure (since he was destined to be a disbeliever). The answer: Moses asked a question; he did not assert it as fact. Also, assessments are based on outward appearances, which is the norm in legal judgments.

Key Terms
استفهام
ظاهر
زكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses falsely accused Khidr of killing a pure soul.
Correction: Moses asked a question based on what he saw, which is permissible.

Checkpoint
How does Razi primarily defend Moses' question about the 'pure soul'?
A) By saying the soul was actually pure.
B) By stating it was a rhetorical question.
C) By arguing that Moses spoke on the basis of outward appearance.
D) By claiming Khidr had informed Moses of the boy's impurity.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic figures, like all humans, judge by what appears, and questions are not accusations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you question someone's action, do you often assume they are wrong before hearing their explanation? How can you emulate the prophetic adab of seeking clarification with an interrogative tone rather than accusation?
?
You often judge people by what you see on the surface. Are you willing to admit that your judgments are based on outward appearances, not hidden realities? What would change in your relationships if you truly owned that limitation?
?
Moses, a great prophet, was corrected by Khidr. When you are corrected, does your ego resist or do you humbly accept that your perspective was limited? What concrete step can you take this week to seek correction from someone wiser than you?
