The First Objection on Moses and Its Three Answers
Imam Razi presents the first objection against Moses from the Khidr story and gives three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Moses' statement to Khidr from three angles, showing the depth of prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Moses' words are not errors but require careful interpretation.

This passage addresses a common objection that Moses erred in calling Khidr's actions 'wrong'. Imam Razi provides three nuanced answers to reconcile Moses' words with his prophetic infallibility. Let's explore how these answers defend the integrity of a prophet's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First read the objection: Moses called Khidr's action 'nukra' even though it was divinely commanded. This seems to be an error.
Notice that the objection assumes literal reading.
2
Examine the first answer
The first answer says Moses meant the outward appearance was terrible, not the inner reality. This distinguishes between apparent and real judgment.
This is a common kalam technique.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer implies a conditional: if the killing was unjust, then it was terrible. This shows Moses' statement was hypothetical.
Arabic grammar allows implicit conditions.
4
Examine the third answer
The third answer interprets 'munkar' as astonishment rather than moral condemnation.
This is a linguistic argument about the word's range.
5
Synthesize
Consider how these three answers collectively protect Moses' infallibility while explaining his strong language.
All three are valid interpretations that avoid accusing a prophet of error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : قصة موسى والخضر.
أما موسى فعلى كلامه سؤالان :
السؤال الأول : أن موسى قال للخضر : لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئًا نكرا [الكهف: ٧٤] و شَيْئًا إمرا [الكهف: ۷۱] وذلك الفعل ما كان منكرًا، فكان كلام موسى خطأ.
والجواب من ثلاثة أوجه :
الأول: المراد من قوله : لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئًا نكرا [الكهف: ٧٤] هو أن ظاهره منكر، بمعنى أن من نظر إلى ظاهر هذا الواقعة، ولم يعرف حقيقتها حكم عليها بأنها شيء منكر.
والثاني: أنا نضمر حرف الشرط والتقدير إن كنت قتلته ظلمًا، فقد جئت شيئا نكرا.
والثالث : المراد من المنكر التعجب، فإن من رأى شيئًا عجيبًا جدًا، فقد يقول : هذا شيء منكر.
The sixth doubt: the story of Moses and al-Khidr. As for Moses, there are two questions regarding his speech: The first question: that Moses said to al-Khidr: 'You have indeed done something terrible (nukra)' [Quran 18:74] and 'something terrible (imran)' [Quran 18:71]. But that action was not wrong, so Moses' speech was erroneous. And the answer is from three aspects: First: what is meant by His saying: 'You have indeed done something terrible (nukra)' [18:74] is that its outward appearance was terrible, meaning that whoever looks at the outward appearance of this event, and does not know its reality, would judge it as something terrible. Second: we imply a conditional particle, and the implied meaning is: if you killed him unjustly, then you have done something terrible. Third: what is meant by 'terrible' (al-munkar) is astonishment, for whoever sees something very strange would say: this is something terrible.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt raised by critics regarding the story of Moses and Khidr. The doubt is that Moses called Khidr's actions 'wrong' even though they were not wrong according to God's commands, implying that Moses made an error. Imam Razi refutes this with three answers. The first answer explains that Moses was referring to the outward appearance of the act, not its inner reality. The second answer suggests an implied conditional: if the killing was unjust, then it was wrong. The third answer interprets the phrase as an expression of astonishment, not a moral judgment. This shows that Moses' words do not contradict his infallibility.

Key Terms
الشبهة
المنكر
الظاهر
التعجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses' words were a direct error rather than an interpretation based on appearance or context.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that Moses' statement can be understood in three valid ways that do not imply error, preserving prophetic infallibility.

Checkpoint
What is the second answer to the objection that Moses called Khidr's action 'wrong'?
A) It was wrong in appearance only.
B) It implies a conditional: if the killing was unjust, then it was wrong.
C) It was an expression of astonishment.
D) Moses was mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Moses' words are reinterpreted in three ways to uphold his infallibility, showing that prophetic statements must be understood in context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something in the life of a scholar or a believer that seems wrong, do you instinctively judge them, or do you pause to consider that there may be a hidden wisdom beyond your knowledge? What is one specific situation today where you can practice holding back judgment?

Second Objection on Moses and Its Answer
Imam Razi answers the objection that Moses called the boy 'pure' when he was not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the infallibility of prophets by explaining that Moses' description of the boy as 'pure' was an interrogative statement and a judgment based on outward appearance.

Core Idea
Moses was asking a question, not making a false statement.

Critics sometimes claim that Moses mistakenly called a disbelieving boy 'pure' in the Quran. Imam Razi offers two clear defenses: first, the wording is interrogative, not declarative; second, even if it were a statement, it would be based on the apparent state, which is permissible in Islamic law. This lesson unpacks those arguments and shows how they protect prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the Quranic verse cited and the two-part response by Razi.
The first part of the answer focuses on grammar (interrogative vs. declarative).
2
Understand the first defense: interrogative vs. assertive
Razi argues that Moses' phrase 'Did you kill a pure soul?' is a question, not a statement. Therefore, it does not assert that the boy was pure; it simply asks for an explanation.
In Arabic, the 'hamzah' of interrogation changes the meaning.
3
Understand the second defense: judging by apparent
Even if the phrase were taken as a statement, Moses spoke according to what was apparent outwardly. The Prophet confirmed that human judgments are based on outward appearances.
This is supported by the hadith 'We judge by the apparent.'
4
Reflect on the significance
These defenses show that prophetic statements are always true and that apparent judgments do not contradict infallibility.
This objection is one of many that Razi addresses in his work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: قال موسى أَقَتَلْتَ نَفْسًا زَكِيَّةً بِغَيْرِ نَفْسٍ [الكهف: ٧٤] وتلك النفس ما كانت زكية.
والجواب : أن موسى ذكر ذلك على سبيل الاستفهام، لا على سبيل الإخبار، وأيضًا لقد جرى الكلام على الظاهر، وهو جائز؛ لقوله ﷺ: «نحن نحكم بالظاهر».
The second question: Moses said: 'Did you kill a pure soul without [killing] a soul?' [Quran 18:74]. That soul was not pure. And the answer: that Moses mentioned that by way of inquiry, not by way of assertion. Also, the speech proceeded according to the apparent (al-zahir), and that is permissible because of his ﷺ saying: 'We judge by the apparent.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection, where critics claim Moses described the boy as a 'pure soul' although the boy was a disbeliever. Razi gives two answers: first, Moses was asking a question (interrogative), not making a statement, so no false assertion occurred. Second, Moses was judging according to outward appearances (al-zahir), which is permissible in matters of worldly judgment, as the Prophet Muhammad said: 'We judge by the apparent.'

Key Terms

الظاهر

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Moses was asking a question; he did not assert that the boy was pure. Moreover, he was judging by outward appearance, which is allowed.

Checkpoint

B) Moses was speaking figuratively.
C) Moses was asking a question and judging by appearance.

Key Takeaway
Moses' statement about the boy was a question, not a false assertion, and judging by outward appearance is permissible in Islamic law.

The Objection on Khidr's Fear and Its Answer
Razi answers an objection on Khidr's fear-based killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi resolves the apparent problem of Khidr killing a boy based on fear alone, by appealing to divine command.

Core Idea
Prophets act by divine command, not personal fear.

A critic might object that Khidr's justification for killing the boy – 'we feared' – is insufficient because fear is not certain knowledge. How then could this action be righteous? Imam Razi's concise answer redirects the issue: the prophet was acting on a direct divine command, not mere human conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the Quranic citation and the structure of the objection and answer.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the problem
The objector argues that Khidr's fear (khashyah) cannot justify killing because fear is not knowledge or even strong conjecture (zann). Why would a prophet act on such a weak basis?
This highlights the importance of certainty in prophetic actions.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi responds simply: perhaps God commanded the killing. The word 'perhaps' (la'alla) signals a plausible explanation, not conjecture. The prophet is merely executing a divine order.
This shifts the basis from human fear to divine authority.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this answer protects the integrity of prophets. They do not act on whims or fallible emotions; they follow God's commands, even when the wisdom is hidden.
This reinforces the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (isma) in actions.

This passage addresses an objection against Khidr. The objection is that Khidr justified killing the boy by saying 'we feared', but fear is not certain knowledge, so how could it justify murder? Razi's answer is simple: Khidr was acting on divine command, not on his own fear. The word 'perhaps' (la'alla) indicates a possible explanation that God had commanded the killing, which justifies the act regardless of human fear.

Five Arguments for David's Innocence Before the Story
Imam Razi lists five Qur'anic arguments refuting David's sin from verses before the story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five Qur'anic arguments from verses before the story of David that refute the notion he committed sin.

Core Idea
David's innocence is established by five divine descriptions before the story even begins.

Imam Razi anticipates the objection that the story of David implies sin. He immediately counters by pointing to five verses preceding the story that describe David with attributes incompatible with sin. These five arguments form a powerful defense of his prophethood.

Guided Path
1

The passage is structured as a list, so pay attention to the sequence.
2
Understand each argument individually
Go through each of the five arguments one by one. For each, identify the Quranic verse and how Razi uses it to prove David's innocence.
The first argument focuses on 'strength in religion', the second on 'awwāb', etc.

القصة العاشرة : قصة داود
قوله: ﴿وَهَلْ أَتَاكَ نَبَأُ الْخَصْمِ﴾ [ص: ٢١] واعلم أنه تعالى ذكر فيما قبل هذه القصة وفيما بعدها ما ينفي دلالتها على المعصية أما قبل القصة فمن وجوه :
الأول : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاذْكُرْ عَبْدَنَا دَاوُدَ ذَا الْأَيْدِ﴾ [ص: ١٧] والأيد هو القوة، ولا شك أن المراد منه : القوة في الدين؛ لأن القوة في الدنيا كانت حاصلة في ملوك الكفار، وما استحقوا بها مدحًا، إنما الذي يكون سببًا لاستحقاق المدح هو القوة في الدين، ولا معنى للقوة في الدين إلا العزم الشديد على أداء الواجبات وترك المحذورات، فكيف يليق هذا الوصف بمن لم يملك منع نفسه من الميل إلى الفجور والقتل؟
والثاني : أنه تعالى وصفه بكونه أوابًا، والأواب هو الرجاع إلى ذكر الله تعالى فكيف يعقل فيه أن يكون مواظبًا على أعظم الكبائر؟
والثالث: أنه تعالى قال : ﴿إِنَّا سَخَّرْنَا الْجِبَالَ مَعَهُ يُسَبِّحْنَ بِالْعَشِيِّ وَالْإِشْرَاقِ وَالطَّيْرَ مَحْشُورَةً كُلٌّ لَهُ أَوَّابٌ﴾ [ص: ١٨-١٩] أفترى أنه تعالى سخر له هذه الأشياء ليتخذها وسائل إلى القتل والزنى؟ وقيل : إنه كان محرمًا عليه صيد كل شيء، أفترى أن الطير كانت آمنة منه والمؤمن ما كان ينجو منه في زوجته ومنكوحته؟
الرابع : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَشَدَدْنَا مُلْكَهُ﴾ [ص: ٢٠] ومحال أن يكون المراد منه تشديد الملك بالمال والعسكر مع كونه حرامًا من طريق الدين؛ لأن ذلك صفات من ملوك الكفرة لا من صفات الأنبياء والرسل.
الخامس : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَآتَيْنَاهُ الْحِكْمَةَ وَفَصْلَ الْخِطَابِ﴾ [ص: ٢٠] والحكمة اسم جامع لكل ما ينبغي علمًا وعملاً، فكيف يجوز أن يقول الله : ﴿وَآتَيْنَاهُ الْحِكْمَةَ﴾ مع إصراره على ما يستنكف عنه أخبث الشياطين من مزاحمة أصحابه في الزوج، وفي المنكوح؟
أما الذي بعد القصة فأمور :
The tenth story: the story of David. His statement: 'And has the news of the litigants come to you?' [Quran 38:21]. Know that God, exalted, mentioned before this story and after it what negates its implication of sin. As for before the story, there are several aspects: First: His statement: 'And remember Our servant David, the possessor of strength (al-ayd)' [Quran 38:17]. Al-ayd is strength. There is no doubt that what is meant by it is strength in religion, because strength in this world was present in the kings of the disbelievers, and they did not deserve praise through it. Rather, what is the cause of deserving praise is strength in religion. There is no meaning to strength in religion except firm resolve to perform obligations and avoid prohibitions. So how can this description befit one who could not restrain himself from inclining to immorality and murder? Second: He, exalted, described him as being 'awwāb' (often returning). Al-awwāb is the one who returns to the remembrance of God. So how can it be understood that he persisted in the greatest of major sins? Third: He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, We subjected the mountains to him, glorifying in the evening and at dawn, and the birds gathered, all were awwāb to him' [Quran 38:18-19]. Do you think that God subjected these things to him to take them as means to murder and adultery? And it was said that hunting every animal was forbidden for him. Do you think that birds were safe from him while the believer was not safe from him regarding his wife and his slave-girl? Fourth: His statement: 'And We strengthened his kingdom' [Quran 38:20]. It is impossible that what is meant is strengthening the kingdom with wealth and army while it is forbidden from the path of religion, because that is an attribute of the kings of disbelievers, not of the prophets and messengers. Fifth: His statement: 'And We gave him wisdom and decisive speech' [Quran 38:20]. Wisdom is a comprehensive term for everything that is proper in knowledge and action. So how can it be permissible for God to say: 'And We gave him wisdom' while he insisted on what the most evil of devils would be ashamed of, i.e., competing with his companions regarding the wife and the slave-girl? As for after the story, there are matters:
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage introduces the story of David and the litigants, which critics use to argue that David committed sin. Razi counters by listing five aspects from the Quranic verses before the story that prove David's innocence and high status. The first is that David is described as 'possessor of strength' which means strength in religion, implying he had firm resolve to obey God. The second is that he is 'awwāb' meaning one who constantly turns to God, incompatible with persistence in sin. The third is that God subjected mountains and birds to him, which would be absurd if he used them for evil. The fourth is that God strengthened his kingdom, and such strength cannot be from unlawful means. The fifth is that God gave him wisdom, which is comprehensive of all good, so it would be contradictory for him to commit great sins. Razi then notes that there are also arguments from after the story but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
الأيد
الأواب
الحكمة
فصل الخطاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story of David and the litigants proves that David committed sin (such as adultery or murder).
Correction: Razi shows that the verses before the story describe David with attributes that are incompatible with sin, so the story cannot be interpreted as implying sin without ignoring the context.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does 'ذَا الْأَيْدِ' (possessor of strength) mean in the context of David?
A) Physical strength.
B) Wealth.
C) Strength in religion.
D) Military power.

Key Takeaway
The Quran preemptively defends David's innocence through five attributes that negate any possibility of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi argues that God would not give wisdom to someone persisting in sin. Are you seeking wisdom (e.g., through knowledge, prayer) while knowingly harboring a sin? What is that sin, and what will you do about it before the day ends?

وهي القصة العاشرة : قصة داود العلا قوله: ﴿وَهَلْ أَتَنكَ نَبُوا الْخَصّيمِ ﴾ [ص: ٢١] واعلم أنه تعالى ذكر فيما قبل هذه القصة وفيما بعدها ما ينفي دلالتها على المعصية أما قبل القصة فمن وجوه : الأول : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاذْكُرْ عَبدَنَا دَاوُرَدَ ذَا الأيده الله [ ص: ۱۷] والأيد هو القوة، ولا شك أن المراد منه : القوة في الدين ؛ لأن القوة في الدنيا كانت حاصلة في ملوك الكفار، وما استحقوا بها مدحاً إنما الذي يكون سببًا لاستحقاق المدح هو القوة في الدين، ولا معنى للقوة في الدين إلا العزم الشديد على أداء الواجبات وترك المحذورات، فكيف يليق هذا الوصف بمن لم يملك منع نفسه من الميل إلى الفجور و القتل ؟ والثاني : أنه تعالى وصفه بكونه أواباً، والأواب هو الرجاع إلى ذكر الله تعالى فكيف يعقل فيه أن يكون مواظبا على أعظم الكبائر ؟ والثالث: أنه تعالى قال : إِنَّا سَخَرْنَا الْجَبَالَ مَعَهُ يُسَبِّحْنَ بِالْعَشِي وَالْإِسْرَاقِ وَالطَّير تَحْشُورَةٌ كُلٌّ لَهُ أَوَابُ ﴾ [ص : ۱۸ - ۱۹] أفترى أنه تعالى سخر له هذه الأشياء ليتخذها وسائل إلى القتل والزنى؟ وقيل : إنه كان محرمًا عليه صيد كل شيء، أفترى أن الطير كانت آمنة منه والمؤمن ما كان ينجو منه في زوجته ومنكوحته ؟ الرابع : قوله تعالى: وَشَدَدْنَا مُلكه [ ص: ٢٠] ومحال أن يكون المراد منه تشديد الملك بالمال والعسكر مع كونه حرامًا من طريق الدين؛ لأن ذلك صفات من ملوك الكفرة لا من صفات الأنبياء والرسل. الخامس : قوله تعالى: وَأَتَيْنَهُ الْحِكْمَةَ وَفَصْلَ الخطاب [ص: الآية ٢٠] والحكمة اسم جامع لكل ما ينبغي علمًا وعملاً ، فكيف يجوز أن يقول الله : وَآتَيْنَهُ الحِكْمَةَ مع إصراره على ما يستنكف عنه أخبث الشياطين من مزاحمة أصحابه في الزوج، وفي المنكوح ؟ أما الذي بعد القصة فأمور : أحدها : قوله تعالى : يَدَاوُردُ إِنَّا جَعَلْتَكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ) [ص: ٢٦] وهذا من أجل المدائح وأعظم المناصب، فلو توسط بين أول القصة - وفيه تلك المدائح . وبين آخرها - وفيه ذكر المعصية - لجرى مجرى من يقول : فلان عظيم الدرجة في الدين وعانى المرتبة في طاعة الله يقتل ويزني ويلوط ويسرق وقد جعله الله خليفة لنفسه، وأمر الكافة بالاقتداء به، ولما لم يكن هذا الكلام لائقًا بأحد من العقلاء فهر بأن لا يكون لائقا بكلام الله تعالى أولى. والثاني : قوله : ﴿إِنَّا جَعَلْتَكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ [ص: ٢٦] مرتب على ذكر تلك القصة، وذلك مشعر بأنه إنما وجد هذه الخلافة بسبب هذه القصة ؛ لأن أصحاب أصول الفقه يقولون ترتيب الحكم على الفعل مشعر بتعليل ذلك الحكم بذلك الفعل وهذا يقتضي أن الله تعالى إنما فوض إليه خلافته في الأرض لأجل أنه أقدم على الزنى والقتل، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل. والثالث : أن داود ال قال : وَإِنَّ كَثِيرًا مِنَ الخلطاء لبغِي بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعْضٍ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ امَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ [ ص: ٢٤] فاستثنى الذين أمنوا وعملوا الصالحات عن الحكم، فلو كان الفاعل لذلك البغي هو داود لكان هو قد حكم على نفسه بعدم الإيمان. والرابع أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ لَهُ عِندَنَا لَزُلْفَى وَحُسْنَ مَشَابِ [ ص: ٢٥] لا يلائم القتل والفسق. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن أول هذه القصة وآخرها يشهدان بأن هذه القصة كاذبة باطلة على الوجه الذي يرويه بها أهل الحشو.
And this is the tenth story: the story of Dawud, upon him be peace. His saying: 'Has there come to you the news of the litigants?' [Sad:21]. Know that He, exalted, mentioned before this story and after it what negates its indication of sin. As for before the story, it is from several aspects: First: His saying: 'And remember Our servant Dawud, possessor of strength' [Sad:17]. And 'al-ayd' is strength, and there is no doubt that what is meant is strength in religion; because strength in worldly matters existed in the kings of disbelievers, and they did not deserve praise for it. That which is a cause for deserving praise is only strength in religion, and there is no meaning of strength in religion except firm resolve to perform obligations and avoid prohibitions. So how is this description befitting for one who could not prevent himself from inclining to immorality and murder? Second: He, exalted, described him as being 'awwab', and 'al-awwab' is the one who returns to the remembrance of Allah, the Exalted. So how can it be rational that he was persistent upon the greatest major sins? Third: He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, We subjected the mountains to glorify [Allah] with him at evening and sunrise, and the birds, gathered; each unto him is returning' [Sad:18-19]. Do you think that He, exalted, subjected these things to him to take them as means to murder and adultery? And it was said that hunting of every thing was forbidden to him. Do you think that the birds were safe from him and the believer was not safe from him regarding his wife and his concubine? Fourth: His saying: 'And We strengthened his kingdom' [Sad:20]. And it is impossible that what is meant is strengthening of kingdom by wealth and army while that is forbidden from the way of religion; because those are attributes of the kings of disbelievers, not of the attributes of the prophets and messengers. Fifth: His saying: 'And We gave him wisdom and decisive speech' [Sad:20]. And wisdom is a comprehensive name for everything that is fitting in knowledge and action. So how is it permissible for Allah to say 'We gave him wisdom' while he persists in what the vilest of devils would be ashamed of, such as jostling with his companions in the wife and the concubine? As for after the story, there are matters: First: His saying: 'O Dawud, indeed We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth' [Sad:26]. This is among the greatest praises and highest stations. So if there were inserted between the beginning of the story—which contains those praises—and its end—which contains mention of sin—it would be like one who says: So-and-so is of high rank in religion and lofty station in obedience to Allah, yet he kills, commits adultery, sodomy, and theft, and Allah has made him His vicegerent and commanded all to follow him. Since this speech is not befitting any rational person, it is all the more unbefitting for the speech of Allah, the Exalted. Second: His saying: 'Indeed, We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth' is placed in order after mentioning that story, and this indicates that he only attained this vicegerency because of that story; because the scholars of usul al-fiqh say that ordering a ruling upon an action indicates that the ruling is caused by that action. This would require that Allah, exalted, entrusted him with His vicegerency on earth because he proceeded to commit adultery and murder, and no rational person would say that. Third: That Dawud, peace be upon him, said: 'And indeed many of the partners oppress one another, except those who believe and do righteous deeds' [Sad:24]. So he exempted those who believed and did righteous deeds from the judgment. So if the doer of that oppression was Dawud, then he would have judged himself to be without faith. Fourth: That His saying: 'And indeed, for him with Us is a near position and a good place of return' [Sad:25] is not compatible with murder and immorality. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the beginning and end of this story testify that this story is false and invalid according to the manner that the people of hashw (anthropomorphists) narrate it.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument from the Qur'anic context demonstrating that the story of Dawud and the two litigants cannot imply that Dawud committed a major sin. Razi first presents five arguments from the verses before the story: (1) Allah describes Dawud as 'possessor of strength,' interpreted as religious strength, which is incompatible with inability to control oneself from sin. (2) He describes him as 'awwab' (returning to Allah), inconsistent with persistence in major sins. (3) The subjection of mountains and birds to glorify with him is not befitting a sinful person; moreover, if hunting was forbidden to him, the birds' safety would contradict his sinfulness. (4) Strengthening his kingdom cannot refer to worldly means if those are forbidden; it must be religious strengthening. (5) Bestowing wisdom, which encompasses all good knowledge and action, contradicts commitment of vile acts. Then he presents four arguments from the verses after the story: (1) The high praise of making him a vicegerent would be absurd if inserted between descriptions of sin. (2) The arrangement of the vicegerency verse after the story could imply causation, but that would be absurd (that sin caused his vicegerency). (3) Dawud's own statement exempting believers from oppression would condemn himself if he were the oppressor. (4) The promise of nearness and good return is incompatible with murder and immorality. The conclusion is that both the preceding and following verses prove the story, as narrated by the anthropomorphists, is false and invalid.

Key Terms
الأيد
الأواب
الحكمة
القوة في الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'anic story of the litigants indicates that Dawud committed a major sin like adultery or murder.
Correction: Razi shows that the verses before the story describe Dawud with attributes (strength, piety, wisdom) that are incompatible with such sins.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what does 'possessor of strength' (ذَا الأَيْدِ) mean?
A) Physical strength in battle
B) Strength in religion and firm resolve to obey
C) Wealth and power of kingdom
D) Eloquence in speech

Key Takeaway
The five consecutive verses before the story of the litigants each provide a strong contextual proof that Dawud was free from major sin.

Four Arguments from After the Story for Dawud's Innocence
Razi enumerates four arguments from verses after the story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four contextual arguments from verses after the story of Dawud that prove he did not commit a major sin.

Core Idea
The verses after the story bestow high praise on Dawud—vicegerency, nearness to Allah—that would be absurd if he had sinned.

Imam Razi then turns to the verses following the account of the two litigants. He presents four powerful arguments that use the high honors Allah gives Dawud—such as making him a vicegerent and promising him reward—to show that sin is incompatible with these divine favors. The arguments also highlight logical absurdities if the story were taken literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage listing the four arguments and its English translation. Notice that each argument builds on a verse after the story.
The fourth argument concludes with a summary statement.
2
Examine the vicegerency argument
The first argument says that inserting the vicegerency verse between mentions of sin would be as absurd as praising a sinner. Understand Razi's comparison.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the second argument on causation
The second argument uses the principle that ordering a ruling after an action can imply causation. If vicegerency followed the story, it would imply sin caused it—impossible.
Razi references usul al-fiqh methodology.
4
Analyze the third and fourth arguments
The third argument shows Dawud's own statement would condemn himself if he were the oppressor. The fourth argument points out that a promised good end is incompatible with murder and immorality.
These rely on the internal coherence of the Qur'an.

And this is the tenth story: the story of Dawud, upon him be peace. His saying: 'Has there come to you the news of the litigants?' [Sad:21]. Know that He, exalted, mentioned before this story and after it what negates its indication of sin. As for before the story, it is from several aspects: First: His saying: 'And remember Our servant Dawud, possessor of strength' [Sad:17]. And 'al-ayd' is strength, and there is no doubt that what is meant is strength in religion; because strength in worldly matters existed in the kings of disbelievers, and they did not deserve praise for it. That which is a cause for deserving praise is only strength in religion, and there is no meaning of strength in religion except firm resolve to perform obligations and avoid prohibitions. So how is this description befitting for one who could not prevent himself from inclining to immorality and murder? Second: He, exalted, described him as being 'awwab', and 'al-awwab' is the one who returns to the remembrance of Allah, the Exalted. So how can it be rational that he was persistent upon the greatest major sins? Third: He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, We subjected the mountains to glorify [Allah] with him at evening and sunrise, and the birds, gathered; each unto him is returning' [Sad:18-19]. Do you think that He, exalted, subjected these things to him to take them as means to murder and adultery? And it was said that hunting of every thing was forbidden to him. Do you think that the birds were safe from him and the believer was not safe from him regarding his wife and his concubine? Fourth: His saying: 'And We strengthened his kingdom' [Sad:20]. And it is impossible that what is meant is strengthening of kingdom by wealth and army while that is forbidden from the way of religion; because those are attributes of the kings of disbelievers, not of the attributes of the prophets and messengers. Fifth: His saying: 'And We gave him wisdom and decisive speech' [Sad:20]. And wisdom is a comprehensive name for everything that is fitting in knowledge and action. So how is it permissible for Allah to say 'We gave him wisdom' while he persists in what the vilest of devils would be ashamed of, such as jostling with his companions in the wife and the concubine? As for after the story, there are matters: First: His saying: 'O Dawud, indeed We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth' [Sad:26]. This is among the greatest praises and highest stations. So if there were inserted between the beginning of the story—which contains those praises—and its end—which contains mention of sin—it would be like one who says: So-and-so is of high rank in religion and lofty station in obedience to Allah, yet he kills, commits adultery, sodomy, and theft, and Allah has made him His vicegerent and commanded all to follow him. Since this speech is not befitting any rational person, it is all the more unbefitting for the speech of Allah, the Exalted. Second: His saying: 'Indeed, We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth' is placed in order after mentioning that story, and this indicates that he only attained this vicegerency because of that story; because the scholars of usul al-fiqh say that ordering a ruling upon an action indicates that the ruling is caused by that action. This would require that Allah, exalted, entrusted him with His vicegerency on earth because he proceeded to commit adultery and murder, and no rational person would say that. Third: That Dawud, peace be upon him, said: 'And indeed many of the partners oppress one another, except those who believe and do righteous deeds' [Sad:24]. So he exempted those who believed and did righteous deeds from the judgment. So if the doer of that oppression was Dawud, then he would have judged himself to be without faith. Fourth: That His saying: 'And indeed, for him with Us is a near position and a good place of return' [Sad:25] is not compatible with murder and immorality. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the beginning and end of this story testify that this story is false and invalid according to the manner that the people of hashw (anthropomorphists) narrate it.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument from the Qur'anic context demonstrating that the story of Dawud and the two litigants cannot imply that Dawud committed a major sin. Razi first presents five arguments from the verses before the story: (1) Allah describes Dawud as 'possessor of strength,' interpreted as religious strength, which is incompatible with inability to control oneself from sin. (2) He describes him as 'awwab' (returning to Allah), inconsistent with persistence in major sins. (3) The subjection of mountains and birds to glorify with him is not befitting a sinful person; moreover, if hunting was forbidden to him, the birds' safety would contradict his sinfulness. (4) Strengthening his kingdom cannot refer to worldly means if those are forbidden; it must be religious strengthening. (5) Bestowing wisdom, which encompasses all good knowledge and action, contradicts commitment of vile acts. Then he presents four arguments from the verses after the story: (1) The high praise of making him a vicegerent would be absurd if inserted between descriptions of sin. (2) The arrangement of the vicegerency verse after the story could imply causation, but that would be absurd (that sin caused his vicegerency). (3) Dawud's own statement exempting believers from oppression would condemn himself if he were the oppressor. (4) The promise of nearness and good return is incompatible with murder and immorality. The conclusion is that both the preceding and following verses prove the story, as narrated by the anthropomorphists, is false and invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the story of the litigants as narrated by anthropomorphists (ahl al-ḥashw) is true and indicates Dawud sinned.
Correction: Razi shows that the verses after the story contain high praise and logical implications that refute any sin.

Checkpoint
What principle from uṣūl al-fiqh does Razi use in the second argument?
A) The general applies to all cases
B) Ordering a ruling after an action can imply causation
C) The literal meaning is primary
D) Abrogation of earlier verses

Key Takeaway
The four verses after the story provide decisive contextual evidence that Dawud was innocent, as they bestow honors incompatible with sin and expose logical contradictions in the sin narrative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah appointed Dawud as vicegerent despite the accusations of sin, what does that tell you about how Allah elevates those whom He loves? Have you ever misjudged someone based on a story you heard about them? How does this lesson challenge you to seek the full context before forming an opinion about a believer?
?
Razi argues that the surrounding verses make the sin narrative impossible. In your own life, are there sins you have allowed yourself to believe are compatible with your identity as a believer? This lesson shows that true nearness to Allah and sin are incompatible in the end. What sin have you been making excuses for that you now need to abandon to be worthy of His nearness?
?
The fourth argument states that a promise of nearness and good return is incompatible with murder and immorality. Do you live as though your actions reflect your ultimate destination? If your own actions were judged by the same logic, would your life's narrative point to nearness to Allah or distance from Him? What specific change will you make today to align your deeds with the honor Allah has given you as His servant?

Four Quranic Proofs Refuting the Story of David
Razi presents four Quranic arguments refuting the false story about David.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses four Quranic arguments—from textual congruity, principles of jurisprudence, David's own speech, and divine praise—to disprove the false story about David.

Core Idea
The Quran itself refutes the false story about David through textual and logical consistency.

Razi confronts a fabricated story about the Prophet David (peace be upon him) that claimed he committed grave sins. Rather than relying on external sources, he turns to the Quran itself, marshaling four distinct arguments that demonstrate the story's impossibility. This lesson examines each proof and the principles of interpretation Razi employs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Try to locate each of the four proofs Razi presents.
The proofs are clearly numbered in the text.
2
Understand the first proof: incongruity of praise with sin
Razi argues that if the story of sin were true, the Quran’s praise of David as a vicegerent in the same context would be absurd.
This proof relies on common sense: a wise speaker would not praise a sinner in that manner.
3
Grasp the second proof: the uṣūl al-fiqh principle of 'illah
Razi applies the principle that arranging a ruling after an action implies causation. If vicegerency followed the story, it would reward sin, which is impossible for Allah.
This shows Razi's use of legal theory in theological debate.
4
Analyze the third proof: David's own exception clause
David says that oppressors are excepted from those who believe and do good. If he were the oppressor, he would have judged himself as lacking faith.
This is a logical deduction from David's own words in the Quran.
5
Reflect on the fourth proof: the promised near position is incompatible with sin
The verse promising David a near station and good return cannot be reconciled with murder and immorality.
This proof contrasts the high divine honor David receives with the low actions attributed to him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : قوله تعالى : يَا دَاوُودُ إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ [ص: ٢٦] وهذا من أجل المدائح وأعظم المناصب، فلو توسط بين أول القصة - وفيه تلك المدائح . وبين آخرها - وفيه ذكر المعصية - لجرى مجرى من يقول : فلان عظيم الدرجة في الدين وعانى المرتبة في طاعة الله يقتل ويزني ويلوط ويسرق وقد جعله الله خليفة لنفسه، وأمر الكافة بالاقتداء به، ولما لم يكن هذا الكلام لائقًا بأحد من العقلاء فهر بأن لا يكون لائقا بكلام الله تعالى أولى. والثاني : قوله : ﴿إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ [ص: ٢٦] مرتب على ذكر تلك القصة، وذلك مشعر بأنه إنما وجد هذه الخلافة بسبب هذه القصة ؛ لأن أصحاب أصول الفقه يقولون ترتيب الحكم على الفعل مشعر بتعليل ذلك الحكم بذلك الفعل وهذا يقتضي أن الله تعالى إنما فوض إليه خلافته في الأرض لأجل أنه أقدم على الزنى والقتل، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل. والثالث : أن داود قال : وَإِنَّ كَثِيرًا مِنَ الخُلَطَاءِ لَيَبْغِي بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعْضٍ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ [ ص: ٢٤] فاستثنى الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات عن الحكم، فلو كان الفاعل لذلك البغي هو داود لكان هو قد حكم على نفسه بعدم الإيمان. والرابع أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ لَهُ عِندَنَا لَزُلْفَى وَحُسْنَ مَآبٍ [ ص: ٢٥] لا يلائم القتل والفسق. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن أول هذه القصة وآخرها يشهدان بأن هذه القصة كاذبة باطلة على الوجه الذي يرويه بها أهل الحشو.
First: His statement, 'O David, indeed We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth' [Ṣād 38:26]. This is among the greatest praises and highest dignities. So if it were inserted between the beginning of the story—which contains those praises—and its end—which contains mention of sin—it would run like one who says: 'So-and-so is great in rank in religion and high in station in obedience to God, [yet] he kills, fornicates, sodomizes, and steals, and God has made him His vicegerent and commanded all to follow him.' Since this speech would not befit any rational person, it is even more fitting that it not befit the speech of God. Second: His statement, 'We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth,' is arranged upon the mention of that story, and this indicates that he only attained this vicegerency because of this story; for the scholars of uṣūl al-fiqh say that the arrangement of a ruling upon an action indicates that the ruling is caused by that action. This would entail that God delegated His vicegerency to him on earth because he committed fornication and murder—which no rational person would say. Third: David said, 'And indeed, many of the partners oppress one another, except those who believe and do righteous deeds' [Ṣād 38:24]. He excepted those who believe and do righteous deeds from the judgment. So if the doer of that oppression was David himself, then he would have judged himself as lacking faith. Fourth: His statement, 'And indeed, for him with Us is a near position and a good place of return' [Ṣād 38:25] is incompatible with murder and immorality. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the beginning and end of this story testify that this story, as related by the people of ḥashw, is false and invalid.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the false story about David. Razi provides four arguments from the Quran itself. First, he notes that the verse praising David as a vicegerent is placed in the middle of the story; if the story included sin, this praise would be incongruous, like praising a criminal. Second, he uses the principle of uṣūl al-fiqh that arranging a ruling on an action indicates causation; if David's vicegerency were caused by his sin, that would be absurd. Third, David's own statement in the story excepts believers from oppression; if he were the oppressor, he would have excluded himself from faith. Fourth, the verse promising David a near position is incompatible with murder and immorality. Thus the story is false.

Key Terms
حشو
تعليل الحكم
أصول الفقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quranic narrative about David implies he committed sin, and that the story is true.
Correction: Razi shows that the Quran itself provides four arguments that the story is false and fabricated by ignorant narrators (ahl al-ḥashw).

Checkpoint
What is the second proof Razi gives against the false story about David?
A) The verse praising David is placed incongruously.
B) The arrangement of vicegerency after the story implies it was caused by sin.
C) David's exception clause in his own speech excludes him from faith if he is the oppressor.
D) The promised near position is incompatible with immorality.

Key Takeaway
The false story is refuted by the Quran’s own internal consistency, logical principles, and the very words of David.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a story about a prophet that seems to diminish his status, do you automatically accept it, or do you, like Razi, immediately measure it against the Quran’s own internal consistency? What story have you accepted without checking?
?
Razi argues that God’s praise cannot be juxtaposed with sin. Where in your own life do you mix praise of God with actions that contradict His commands? How will you stop that contradiction today?
?
If David’s own words exclude oppressors from faith, and you claim to follow the prophets, what oppression in your heart or actions have you made peace with—and what will you do to remove it so that your faith is not contradicted?

The Correct Interpretation: Intruders, Not Angels
Razi presents the interpretation that the incident was about intruders, not angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretation of the verses about Prophet David's encounter with the litigants, and why it is considered more literal and appropriate than the popular story.

Core Idea
The literal reading preserves prophetic integrity and avoids attributing falsehood to angels.

This passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'in presents a crucial methodological lesson in Quranic interpretation. By offering an alternative explanation for the incident in Surah Sad, Razi demonstrates how the principle of interpreting the Quran according to its apparent meaning (zahir) protects the honor of the prophets and avoids problematic theological implications. Here, Razi argues that the "litigants" were actually intruders who invented a lawsuit, not angels, thereby avoiding the need to attribute lies to angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very dense argumentative style.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Razi contrasts the 'people of verification' interpretation with the popular story. Find the two interpretations and see how he argues for the first.
3
Examine the two deviations
Razi lists two ways the popular story deviates from the apparent meaning. Identify them and understand why each is problematic.
The two deviations are: (1) angels lying, (2) interpreting ewes as women.
4
Analyze the three ambiguous words
Razi mentions three words that the popular story relies on: fitna, istighfar, ghufran. Note that he says they do not prove the story.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider the principle of preferring the apparent meaning (zahir) and why Razi finds it important in this context. This is a methodological lesson in tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أهل التحقيق ذكروا وجوها كثيرة في تأويل الآية : الأول: أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهَلْ أَتَاكَ نَبَأُ الْخَصْمِ إِذْ تَسَوَّرُوا الْمِحْرَابَ [ص: ٢١] حكاية عن جماعة تسوروا قصره قاصدين قتله والإساءة إليه في أهله وماله، تسوروا قصره في وقت ظنوا أنه غافل، فلما رآهم داود خافهم لما تقرر في العرف أنه لا يتسور أحد دار غيره من غير أمره إلا لقصد السوء من قتل النفس أو سرقة المال وخصوصًا إذا كان صاحب الدار شخصا معظمًا، فلما رأوه مستيقظًا انتقض عليهم تدبيرهم وخافوا فاخترع بعضهم عند ذلك الخوف خصومة لا أصل لها، وزعم أنهم إنما قصدوه لأجلها دون ما توهمه فقالوا : خَصْمَانِ بَغَى بَعْضُنَا عَلَى بَعْض [ص: ٢٢] ثم ادعى أحدهما على الآخر مالا ، فقال : إِنَّ هَذَا أَخِي لَهُ تِسْعٌ وَتِسْعُونَ نَعْجَةً وَلِي نَعْجَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ [ص: ٢٣] قال داود : لَقَدْ ظَلَمَكَ بِسُؤَالِ نَعْجَتِكَ إِلَى نِعَاجِهِ [ص: ٢٣] . واعلم أن حمل الآية على ما ذكرناه حمل الكلام على ظاهره، أما حملها على القصة المشهورة فإنه يقتضي العدول عن الظاهر من وجهين : الأول : أن الملائكة لما قالوا خَصْمَانِ بَغَى بَعْضُنَا عَلَى بَعْض ) [ ص: ٢٢] كان هذا كذبًا ؛ لأنه لم يبغ أحد من الملائكة على الآخر، وإسناد الكذب إلى اللصوص أولى من إسناده إلى الملائكة. الثاني : قوله : إِنَّ هَذَا أَخِي لَهُ تِسْعٌ وَتِسْعُونَ نَعْجَةً وَلِي نَعْجَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ [ ص: ٢٣] هذا أيضًا كذب والقوم يحملون النعاج على النسوان وهو عدول عن الظاهر، وأما على قولنا فقد نسبنا هذا الكذب إلى اللصوص، وهو أولى من نسبته إلى الملائكة. وبالجملة : فليس في الآية لفظ يشهد بصحة ما ذكره من القصة إلا ثلاث : أحدها : قوله: ﴿وَظَنَّ دَاوُودُ أَنَّمَا فَتَنَّاهُ [ ص: ٢٤] وذلك لأن لفظ الفتنة يوهم الابتلاء. وثانيها : قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاسْتَغْفَرَ رَبَّهُ ﴾ [ ص: ٢٤] وذلك يوهم صدور الذنب منه. وثالثها : قوله : فَغَفَرْنَا لَهُ ذَلِكَ ﴾ [ ص: ٢٥] واعلم أن شيئًا من ذلك لا يدل على أقوالهم.
Then the people of verification mentioned many interpretations of the verse. The first: His statement, 'And has there come to you the news of the litigants, when they climbed the prayer chamber?' [Ṣād 38:21] is a narrative about a group who scaled his palace, intending to kill him and harm his family and property. They scaled his palace at a time when they thought he was unaware. When David saw them, they feared him, because it is established in custom that no one enters another's house without permission except with evil intent—either murder or theft—especially when the owner is a venerated person. When they saw him awake, their plan collapsed and they feared, so one of them invented on the spot a baseless lawsuit, claiming that they came only for that reason and not for what David suspected. They said: 'We are two litigants, one of us has wronged the other' [38:22]. Then one claimed against the other property, saying: 'Indeed this brother of mine has ninety-nine ewes, and I have one ewe' [38:23]. David said: 'He has wronged you by demanding your ewe in addition to his ewes' [38:24]. Know that interpreting the verse according to what we have mentioned is to interpret it according to its apparent meaning. As for interpreting it according to the famous story, it requires deviation from the apparent in two ways: First, when the angels said 'two litigants wronged each other' [38:22], this would be a lie, because no angel wronged another. Attributing falsehood to thieves is better than attributing it to angels. Second, his saying, 'Indeed this brother of mine has ninety-nine ewes, and I have one ewe' [38:23], this is also a lie, and the people interpret 'ewes' as women, which is a deviation from the apparent. According to our view, we have attributed this lie to thieves, which is better than attributing it to angels. In summary: there is no word in the verse that testifies to the truth of what they mentioned of the story except three: first, his statement, 'And David thought that We had tempted him' [38:24], because the word fitna suggests trial; second, his statement, 'So he sought forgiveness of his Lord' [38:24], which suggests the occurrence of sin from him; third, his statement, 'So We forgave him that' [38:25]. But know that none of that indicates their claims.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative interpretation of the verses about David. The 'people of verification' (ahl al-taḥqīq) offer many interpretations; the first is that the 'litigants' were actually intruders who climbed David's palace with evil intentions, but when caught, they invented a lawsuit. This interpretation takes the verses literally, whereas the popular story forces a figurative interpretation. Then Razi identifies three ambiguous words that the popular story relies on: 'fitna', 'istighfar', and 'ghufran'. However, he asserts that even these do not support the false story, as he will explain.

Key Terms
أهل التحقيق
ظاهر
عدول عن الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mention of 'temptation' (fitna) and 'forgiveness' (ghufran) in the verses proves that Prophet David committed a sin.
Correction: Razi argues that these words are ambiguous and do not necessarily indicate sin; they could refer to trials or other meanings. The literal interpretation of the lawsuit story avoids any implication of sin.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the first deviation from the apparent meaning required by the popular story?
A) Interpreting 'litigants' as angels.
B) Interpreting 'ewes' as women.
C) Claiming that David committed murder.
D) Saying that David was not a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Interpreting the Quran according to its apparent meaning (zahir) protects the honor of the prophets and avoids theological pitfalls, as demonstrated by Razi's exegesis of the story of David.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic story that seems to imply a prophet made a mistake, do you immediately accept the popular interpretation, or do you pause and ask if there is an alternative that respects their honor? How will you change your reading habits after this lesson?
?
Razi argues that attributing a lie to angels is worse than attributing it to thieves. Think of a time you spread information without verifying it. How does this principle of guarding the honor of Allah's creation apply to your own speech and sharing of news?
?
The lesson shows that even well-known stories can be based on weak evidence. Examine your heart: are you attached to a particular interpretation because it is dramatic or emotionally satisfying, even if it contradicts the apparent meaning? Are you willing to let it go for the sake of truth?

Razi's Interpretation of Fitna and Istighfar in David's Story
Razi explains the meanings of fitna and istighfar in David's story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the words 'fitna' and 'istighfar' in the story of David do not imply sin but rather mean test and intercession respectively.

Core Idea
Fitna means test, not sin; istighfar can be for others.

In defending the prophets from sin, Imam Razi examines the Quranic story of David. He shows that two key words – fitna and istighfar – are often misunderstood. This passage clarifies their true meanings using linguistic and contextual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Start by reading the Arabic and its translation to get a sense of Razi's argument. Notice how he introduces two aspects.
The passage is dense but straightforward.
2
Understand Razi's definition of fitna
Razi interprets 'fitna' as a test (imtihan), not temptation to sin. He explains that David's restraint increased his status.
3
Examine the flexibility of istighfar
Razi argues that 'istighfar' (seeking forgiveness) does not necessarily imply personal sin. He cites Quranic examples of prophets and angels seeking forgiveness for others.
This refutes the claim that David sinned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : أما قوله: ﴿وَظَنَّ دَاوُودُ أَنَّمَا فَتَنَّاهُ [ص: ٢٤] فالمعنى : امتحناه واختبرناه، فإنه لما أساء الظن بهم، فهل يعاجلهم بالعقوبة أم لا ؟ ثم إنه لكمال سلطته وقوة مملكته لم يعاجلهم بالعقوبة، ولم ينتقم منهم، ولم يضربهم، فكان ذلك سببًا لازدياد منصبه في الحكم والدين. وأما قوله : فَاسْتَغْفَرَ رَبَّهُ ﴾ [ص: ٢٤] فليس في الآية أن ذلك الاستغفار لنفسه أو لغيره، ألا ترى أنه تعالى حكى عن الملائكة بأنهم يستغفرون للذين آمنوا ؟ وقال أولاد يعقوب : يَا أَبَانَا اسْتَغْفِرْ لَنَا ذُنُوبَنَا ﴾ [يوسف: ٩٧] وقال تعالى للنبي ﷺ : ﴿وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ وَلِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ﴾ [محمد: ١٩] وإذا كان ذلك كذلك
And evidence for it from several aspects: The first aspect: As for His statement, 'And David thought that We had tempted him' [38:24], the meaning is: We tested and tried him; for he thought ill of them, [to see] whether he would hasten to punish them or not. Then he, because of the perfection of his authority and the strength of his kingdom, did not hasten to punish them, nor did he take revenge from them, nor did he strike them. So that became a cause for the increase of his station in judgment and religion. As for His statement, 'So he sought forgiveness of his Lord' [38:24], there is nothing in the verse indicating that this seeking forgiveness was for himself or for others. Do you not see that God related that the angels seek forgiveness for those who believe? And the sons of Jacob said, 'O our father, ask forgiveness for us for our sins' [Yusuf 12:97]. And God said to the Prophet ﷺ, 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believing men and believing women' [Muhammad 47:19]. So when this is the case...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the two ambiguous words. First, 'fitna' is interpreted as a test or trial, not a temptation to sin. David was tested to see if he would punish the intruders immediately; he showed restraint, which increased his status. Second, 'istighfar' (seeking forgiveness) does not necessarily mean he sinned; it could be for others, as proven by Quranic examples where prophets and angels seek forgiveness for believers. Thus, these words do not support the claim that David sinned.

Key Terms
فتنة
استغفار
امتحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'istighfar' always implies personal sin.
Correction: Razi shows that 'istighfar' can be intercession on behalf of others, as in the Quranic examples he cites.

Checkpoint
How does Razi interpret David's 'asking forgiveness' (استغفار) in the verse?
A) It proves David committed a major sin.
B) It may have been on behalf of others, not himself.
C) It shows David regretted his mistake.
D) It indicates David was not a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that fitna means test and istighfar can be intercession, so these words do not prove sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult test like David's, do you react with haste and anger, or with the restraint that increased his rank? What would it look like for you to practice that restraint today?
?

Razi's Three Interpretations of Dawud's Istighfar
Razi presents three interpretations absolving Dawud of sin and states this is the correct view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three interpretations of Dawud's istighfar that preserve prophetic infallibility and his argument that the Quranic verses do not indicate sin.

Core Idea
Razi's three interpretations show that Dawud's istighfar was not for sin but for intercession, regret, or self-admiration.

Imam Razi, a master of tafsir and kalam, tackles the story of Dawud (AS) in Surah Sad. Critics have used this story to suggest prophets can commit sins, but Razi offers three powerful interpretations that not only absolve Dawud but also deepen our understanding of prophetic mercy and humility. In this lesson, we will explore each interpretation and see how Razi defends prophetic infallibility without distorting the Quranic narrative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the flow and the three distinct interpretations.
Notice that Razi begins with 'probably' (احتمل), indicating he is offering possible interpretations.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Razi presents three theological explanations for Dawud's istighfar. Find each one and note how each avoids attributing a sin to Dawud.
The first interpretation is about intercession, the second about regret over a suspicion, the third about self-admiration.
3
Connect to Quranic context
Observe how Razi links his first interpretation to the surrounding verses: the vicegerency verse and the command to Muhammad to be patient.
This contextual argument strengthens his interpretation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi ends by asserting that this view – that there is no indication of sin – is the clear truth. Consider why he is so emphatic.
He is defending the dogma of isma (prophetic infallibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتمل أن يكون المراد أن القوم لما أقدموا على ذلك الفعل المنكر لم يعاجلهم داود بالعقوبة، بل أظهر الحلم، وزاد على ذلك طلبه من الله تعالى أن يعفو عنهم وأن يغفر لهم. وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَغَفَرْنَا لَهُ ذَلِكَ﴾ [ص: ٢٥] فيحتمل أن يكون المراد: فغفرنا لأجل حرمته، ولبركة شفاعته ذلك الفعل المنكر الذي أتى به أولئك المتسورون، وهذا التأويل الذي ذكرناه ينطبق عليه القرآن ولا نحتاج فيه إلى إسناد الكذب إلى الملائكة وحمل النعاج على النسوان. ثم إنه يليق به أن يذكر عقيب قوله: ﴿إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ﴾ [ص: ٢٦] فمن بلغت رحمته وشفقته على الرعية إلى هذا الحد كان اللائق برحمته أرحم الراحمين تفويض خلافة الأرض إليه، ويليق به أن يأمر محمداً عند تأذيه من قومه بأن يقتدي به، وهو قوله في أول الآية: ﴿اصْبِرْ عَلَى مَا يَقُولُونَ وَاذْكُرْ عَبْدَنَا دَاوُدَ ذَا الْأَيْدِ﴾ [ص: ١٧]. ومن طلب الحق وأنصف علم أن ما ذكرناه هو الحق الصريح. الوجه الثاني: لعل الاستغفار إنما كان لأن القوم لما تسوروا داره ظن بهم أنهم يقصدون قتله، ثم إنه لما لم تظهر الأمارات الدالة على أن ذلك الظن حق ندم على ذلك الظن، فكان الاستغفار بسببه. الوجه الثالث: أنها لما هضم نفسه ولم يؤذهم ولم ينتقم منهم مع القدرة التامة دخله شيء من العجب بسبب كمال حلمه فكان الاستغفار منه لأن العجب من المهلكات. عندي. فهذا قول من يقول: لا دلالة في الآية على إثباته بشيء من الزلات، وهو الحق.
Possibly the intended meaning is that when the people committed that reprehensible act, Dawud did not hasten to punish them, but rather showed forbearance, and added to that his request from Allah to pardon them and forgive them. As for His statement, the Exalted: 'So We forgave him that' [Sad: 25], the intended meaning could be: We forgave for the sake of his honor and the blessing of his intercession that reprehensible act committed by those who climbed over the wall. This interpretation we have mentioned is consistent with the Qur'an, and we do not need therein to attribute lies to the angels or to interpret the ewes as women. Then it is befitting that it be mentioned after His statement: 'Indeed We have made you a vicegerent on the earth' [Sad: 26]. For one whose mercy and compassion for his subjects reaches this extent, it is befitting for the Most Merciful of the merciful to entrust the vicegerency of the earth to him. And it is befitting that He command Muhammad, when he is harmed by his people, to follow his example, and that is His statement at the beginning of the verse: 'Be patient over what they say, and mention Our servant Dawud, the possessor of strength' [Sad: 17]. Whoever seeks truth and is fair knows that what we have mentioned is the clear truth. The second aspect: Perhaps the seeking of forgiveness was only because when the people climbed over his wall, he thought they intended to kill him, then when the signs indicating that thought was correct did not appear, he regretted that thought, so the seeking of forgiveness was because of it. The third aspect: When he humbled himself and did not harm them nor take revenge despite complete ability, some self-admiration entered him due to the perfection of his forbearance, so the seeking of forgiveness was from that, because self-admiration is one of the destructive things. In my view. So this is the statement of one who says: There is no indication in the verse of attributing any lapse to him, and that is the truth.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a tripartite interpretation by Imam Razi of the story of Prophet Dawud (David) in Surah Sad, specifically the incident where two disputants climbed over the wall of his sanctuary. Razi's primary goal is to defend the prophetic infallibility (isma) by arguing that the verse does not imply any sin, major or minor, on the part of Dawud. He begins by offering the first interpretation: The reprehensible act was committed by the people who trespassed, and Dawud's istighfar (seeking forgiveness) was actually an intercession for them—Allah forgave them out of honor for Dawud. This avoids attributing lies to the angels (by interpreting the 'ewes' as women) and maintains consistency with the Qur'an. Next, Razi connects this interpretation to the context: after the verse declaring Dawud a vicegerent (khalifa), it is fitting to mention his mercy; likewise, Muhammad is commanded to follow Dawud's example of patience. The second interpretation: Dawud's istighfar was due to his initial suspicion that the trespassers intended to kill him, a suspicion that proved unfounded, so he regretted it. The third interpretation: After showing extreme forbearance, Dawud felt a hint of self-admiration (ujb), which is a destructive trait, hence his istighfar. Razi concludes that this view—that the verse contains no indication of sin—is the correct one.

Key Terms
استغفار
زلة
عصمة
العجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi denies the apparent meaning of the Quranic text, but he is offering alternative interpretations to preserve prophetic infallibility while fully affirming the Quranic narrative.
Correction: Razi does not deny the text; he provides plausible interpretations that align with the Quran and the principle of isma.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's third interpretation, what caused Dawud to seek forgiveness?
A) His failure to punish the trespassers
B) His initial suspicion of the trespassers
C) Self-admiration (ujb) due to his forbearance
D) His premature judgment in the case

Key Takeaway
Razi's three interpretations demonstrate that the Quranic story of Dawud is consistent with prophetic infallibility, and that seeking forgiveness does not necessarily imply sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something good, do you ever feel a hint of self-admiration? How does Dawud's seeking forgiveness for ujb challenge you to constantly check your intentions? What will you do today to guard against this hidden disease?
?
Razi says Dawud's istighfar might have been intercession for others. Think of a person you have wronged or a community you are part of. When was the last time you sincerely asked Allah to forgive them? What is stopping you from making that prayer right now?
?
If you are in a position of authority—even as a parent, teacher, or leader—how do you react when someone offends you? Dawud showed forbearance and then sought forgiveness for any self-admiration. Which is harder for you: forgiving others or forgiving yourself for feeling proud of your forgiveness? Commit to one concrete action this week to develop humility in leadership.

First Interpretation: Relief at Uriah's Death
First view on Dawud's minor sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of Dawud's minor sin: feeling relief upon hearing of Uriah's death due to attraction to his wife.

Core Idea
Dawud's minor sin was a fleeting relief, not a major transgression.

In this lesson, we explore the first of five explanations offered by scholars who maintain that Prophet Dawud (AS) committed a minor sin. This view focuses on his human reaction to the news of Uriah's death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand the first interpretation.
Notice the subtlety: the sin is an internal feeling.
2
Identify the sin
The sin is described as a lessening of grief due to attraction. Consider why this is deemed a minor sin.
3
Reflect on prophetic infallibility
Think about how this interpretation preserves the principle of 'ismah while acknowledging a human flaw.
Prophets are protected from major sins, but minor lapses are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من سلم دلالة الآية على الصغيرة فلهم فيه وجوه: أحدها: أنه كان عالماً بحسن امرأة أوريا، فلما سمع أنه قتل قل غمه لميل نفسه إلى نكاح زوجته، فعوتب عليه. وثانيها: أن أهل زمان داود كان يسأل بعضهم بعضاً أن ينزل له عن امرأته ليتزوج بها، إذا أعجبته وكان ذلك جائزاً فيما بينهم، واتفق أن عين داود وقعت على امرأة أوريا، فأحبها، فسأله النزول عنها، فاستحيى أن يرده ففعل، فتزوجها أم سليمان، فقيل له: إنك مع ارتفاع قدرك وكثرة نسائك ما كان يليق بك أن تسأل رجلاً ليس له إلا امرأة واحدة أن ينزل عنها، بل كان الواجب عليك قهر النفس. وثالثها: أن أوريا خطبها ثم خطبها داود، فكان ذنبه أنه خطب على خطبة أخيه مع كثرة نسائه، وأهلها اختاروه. ورابعها: أن داود كان مشتغلاً بعبادة ربه فأتاه رجل وامرأة يتحاكمان، فنظر إلى المرأة ليعرفها بعينها وليحكم عليها أو لها، وذلك مباح، فمالت نفسه إليها، ففصل بينهما، وعاد إلى عبادة ربه واستولى عليه الفكر في أمرها، وصار مانعاً له من الاشتغال بعبادته فعوتب. وخامسها: أن الصغيرة منه إنما حصلت بسبب العجلة في الحكم قبل التثبت، وهو قوله: ﴿لَقَدْ ظَلَمَكَ بِسُؤَالِ نَعْجَتِكَ إِلَى نِعَاجِهِ﴾ [ص: ٢٤] بل كان الواجب عليه أن يسمع الدعوى من أحد الخصمين ثم يسأل الآخر عما عنده فيها. ومن قال بهذا الوجه قال إن الخوف الذي حصل بسبب دخولهما عليه في غير الوقت اللائق بالدخول عليه أنساه التثبت والتحفظ.
And as for one who accepts that the verse indicates a minor sin, they have several aspects regarding it: First: That he (Dawud) was aware of the beauty of Uriah's wife, so when he heard that Uriah was killed, his grief lessened because his soul inclined to marrying his wife, so he was reprimanded for that. Second: That the people of Dawud's time used to ask each other to cede his wife for marriage if he admired her, and that was permissible among them. It happened that Dawud's eye fell on Uriah's wife, so he loved her and asked him to cede her. Uriah was too shy to refuse him, so he did, and Dawud married her, the mother of Sulayman. Then it was said to him: 'With your high status and many wives, it was not befitting for you to ask a man who has only one wife to cede her; rather, you should have subdued your own soul.' Third: That Uriah sought her hand, then Dawud sought her hand, so his sin was that he proposed over the proposal of his brother while having many wives, and her family chose him. Fourth: That Dawud was occupied with the worship of his Lord, then a man and a woman came to him for judgment. He looked at the woman to know her exactly and to judge for or against her, which was permissible, but his soul inclined to her. He judged between them, returned to worship, but thoughts about her overtook him and prevented his devotion to worship, so he was reprimanded. Fifth: That the minor sin from him was due to haste in judgment before verification, and that is His statement: 'He has certainly wronged you in asking for your ewe to add to his ewes' [Sad: 24]; rather, it was obligatory on him to hear the claim from one of the two litigants, then ask the other what he had concerning it. The one who says this view says that the fear that occurred due to their entering upon him at an inappropriate time made him forget verification and caution.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five alternative interpretations held by scholars who acknowledge that the verse about Dawud implies he committed a minor sin (saghira). Each interpretation tries to explain the nature of that minor sin while still upholding the general principle of prophetic infallibility. First: Dawud's sin was a feeling of relief upon hearing of Uriah's death because he was attracted to his wife, for which he was rebuked. Second: In that society, it was customary to ask for a wife to be ceded, but Dawud's status made it unbecoming for him to ask a man with only one wife to cede her; he should have controlled his desires. Third: Dawud proposed to a woman who was already engaged to Uriah (his 'brother' in faith), which is forbidden as proposing over a brother's proposal, especially given his many wives. Fourth: While judging a case, Dawud's gaze at a woman led to a distraction that interfered with his worship, so he was rebuked for failing to guard his thoughts. Fifth: In the actual judgment, Dawud ruled too hastily against the one who had only one ewe without hearing the other party's defense, due to being unsettled by the unexpected entry of the litigants. This haste before full verification was the minor sin. Razi merely presents these views without endorsing any, as his own position is the one stated in the previous lesson.

Key Terms
الصغيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what was Dawud's minor sin?
A) He committed adultery with Uriah's wife
B) He felt relief at Uriah's death due to attraction
C) He proposed over a brother's proposal
D) He was hasty in judgment

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation suggests Dawud's minor sin was an emotional reaction of relief due to his attraction to Uriah's wife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a secret relief when a difficulty was removed from your path, even if it came at another's expense? How does Dawud's reprimand make you accountable for your own hidden reactions? Will you sincerely repent for any such relief today?
?
If a prophet of Allah was reprimanded for an internal feeling of relief, what does that reveal about the level of accountability for your own heart's inclinations? What one inner feeling do you need to bring before Allah in tawbah right now?
?
The passage says Dawud's soul inclined to something that led to a reprimand. What does your soul incline towards that you have been ignoring? Commit to monitoring one such inclination this week and turning from it before it becomes a sin.

Second Interpretation: Asking for a Wife to Be Ceded
Second view on Dawud's minor sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of Dawud's minor sin: asking Uriah to cede his wife despite his own high status.

Core Idea
Dawud's sin was asking a man with one wife to cede her, unbecoming of his station.

This lesson covers the second view, which focuses on the social impropriety of Dawud's request rather than any sin of desire. It examines the custom of ceding wives in that era.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the custom mentioned.
2
Understand the sin
The sin is not the request itself (which was permissible) but that Dawud, with many wives, asked a man with one wife to cede her.
It highlights a lack of self-restraint.
3
Consider the context
The custom allowed such requests, but Dawud's status made it improper.
Prophets are models of propriety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for one who accepts that the verse indicates a minor sin, they have several aspects regarding it: First: That he (Dawud) was aware of the beauty of Uriah's wife, so when he heard that Uriah was killed, his grief lessened because his soul inclined to marrying his wife, so he was reprimanded for that. Second: That the people of Dawud's time used to ask each other to cede his wife for marriage if he admired her, and that was permissible among them. It happened that Dawud's eye fell on Uriah's wife, so he loved her and asked him to cede her. Uriah was too shy to refuse him, so he did, and Dawud married her, the mother of Sulayman. Then it was said to him: 'With your high status and many wives, it was not befitting for you to ask a man who has only one wife to cede her; rather, you should have subdued your own soul.' Third: That Uriah sought her hand, then Dawud sought her hand, so his sin was that he proposed over the proposal of his brother while having many wives, and her family chose him. Fourth: That Dawud was occupied with the worship of his Lord, then a man and a woman came to him for judgment. He looked at the woman to know her exactly and to judge for or against her, which was permissible, but his soul inclined to her. He judged between them, returned to worship, but thoughts about her overtook him and prevented his devotion to worship, so he was reprimanded. Fifth: That the minor sin from him was due to haste in judgment before verification, and that is His statement: 'He has certainly wronged you in asking for your ewe to add to his ewes' [Sad: 24]; rather, it was obligatory on him to hear the claim from one of the two litigants, then ask the other what he had concerning it. According to the second interpretation, what was the main issue with Dawud's action?
A) He committed adultery

Key Takeaway

?

?

?
If Dawud's asking for another's wife was unbecoming of his rank, what does your own behavior say about your consciousness of Allah's presence in your life? What will you change so that your actions match your claimed faith?

Third Interpretation: Proposing Over a Brother's Proposal
Third view on Dawud's minor sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third interpretation of Dawud's minor sin: proposing to a woman already engaged to Uriah.

Core Idea
Dawud's sin was proposing over a brother's proposal, which is discouraged.

This lesson presents the third view, which interprets Dawud's sin as violating the etiquette of engagement. It highlights the prohibition of proposing over another's proposal, especially for a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic and translation.
2
Identify the sin
The sin is 'proposing over the proposal of a brother.' This refers to Uriah having already proposed.
This is forbidden in Islam.
3
Consider the implication
Dawud's many wives made this even more inappropriate.
Prophets should be examples of good conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
