Razi's Response: Interpreting Away the Gharaaniq Incident
Razi provides a response to the Gharaaniq objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defends the Prophet's infallibility by offering multiple interpretations of the key verse.

Core Idea
Razi shows that the word 'umniyyah' can mean recitation or intention, each leading to harmless explanations.

In response to the Gharaaniq objection, Imam Razi provides a hermeneutical solution. He argues that the problematic narration can be understood in different ways depending on the meaning of the word 'al-umniyyah'. This lesson explores his two-fold interpretation and its implications for the integrity of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Understand Razi's approach
Razi first clarifies that the word 'al-umniyyah' in the verse has two possible meanings: recitation (qira'ah) and intention ('azimah). This ambiguity is key to resolving the objection.
2
First interpretation: umniyyah as recitation
If 'umniyyah' means recitation, Razi offers two sub-explanations: (a) the 'gharaneeq' are angels, and the recitation was from angelic scriptures, which was later abrogated; (b) the phrase is actually an interrogative denial, not an affirmation.
3
Second interpretation: umniyyah as intention
If 'umniyyah' means intention, then the verse refers to Satan's whispers into the Prophet's thoughts, not his recitation. Allah then abrogates those whispers and guides him away from them.
4
Reflect on the defense of prophethood
Notice that in all cases, the Quranic text itself remains untouched. Razi's response protects the integrity of revelation and the Prophet's infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن قَبْلِكَ مِن رَّسُولٍ وَلَا نَبِيٍّ إِلَّا إِذَا تَمَنَّى أَلْقَى الشَّيْطَانُ فِي أُمْنِيَّتِهِ﴾ [الحج : ٥٢] يروى أنه لما رأى إعراض قومه عنه، شق عليه ذلك وتمنى أن يأتيه من الله ما يتقرب به إلى قومه، فأنزل الله تعالى سورة النجم فقرأها رسول الله حتى بلغ إلى قوله : ﴿أَفَرَءَيْتُمُ اللَّاتَ وَالْعُزَّى * وَمَنَاةَ الثَّالِثَةَ الْأُخْرَى﴾ [ النجم : ۱۹ - ۲۰] ألقى الشيطان على لسانه : تلك الغرانيق العلى منها الشفاعة ترتجي فلما سمعت قريش ذلك فرحوا وقالوا : قد ذكر آلهتنا بأحسن الذكر، فلما أمسى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جاءه جبريل وقال : تلوت على الناس ما لم آتك به، فحزن لذلك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وخاف من الله خوفا شديداً، فنزل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن قَبْلِكَ مِن رَّسُولٍ ...﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٥] .

والجواب : أنا قد ذكرنا في التفسير الكبير كل الوجوه المذكورة في هذه الآية، وحاصل الكلام يرجع إلى أن الأمنية يمكن تفسيرها بالقراءة، وبالعزيمة، فإن فسرناها بالقراءة فلها وجهان:
أحدهما : أن يقال: إن الغرانيق هم الملائكة، وقد كان ذلك قرآنًا منزلا في صحف الملائكة، فلما توهم المشركون أن الله تعالى يريد آلهتهم نسخ الله تلاوته.
الثاني : أن يقال : المراد منه الاستفهام على سبيل الإنكار، كأنه قال: أشفاعتهن ترتجى؟ وأما إن فسرناه بالخاطر وتمني القلب كان المعنى أن النبي كلما تمنى ما كان يتمناه من الأمور وسوس الشيطان إليه بالباطل، يدعوه إلى ما لا ينبغي، ثم إن الله تعالى ينسخ ذلك ويهديه إلى ترك الالتفات إلى وسوسته.
The second objection: They clung to His statement, Exalted is He: 'And We did not send before you any messenger or prophet except that when he recited [or wished], Satan threw into his recitation [or wish]' [Al-Hajj: 52]. It is narrated that when he saw his people turning away from him, that distressed him and he wished that Allah would bring him something by which to draw near to his people. So Allah, Exalted is He, revealed Surat An-Najm, and the Messenger of Allah recited it until he reached His statement: 'Have you considered Al-Lat and Al-Uzza * And Manat, the third, the other?' [An-Najm: 19-20]. Satan cast upon his tongue: 'Those are the high swans; their intercession is sought.' When Quraysh heard that, they rejoiced and said: 'He has mentioned our gods in the best remembrance.' When evening came, Jibril came to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and said: 'You recited to the people what I did not bring to you.' So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, became sorrowful and feared Allah greatly. Then the verse was revealed: 'And We did not send before you any messenger...' [Al-Anbiya': 25].

The answer: We have mentioned in 'At-Tafsir Al-Kabir' all the aspects mentioned in this verse. The sum of the discussion returns to the fact that 'al-umniyyah' can be interpreted as 'recitation' (al-qira'ah) and as 'intention' (al-'azimah). If we interpret it as recitation, it has two aspects:
One: It is said that 'al-gharaneeq' are the angels, and that was Quran sent down in the scriptures of the angels. When the polytheists imagined that Allah, Exalted is He, intended their gods, Allah abrogated its recitation.
Two: It is said that what is meant by it is an interrogative in the sense of denial, as if He said: 'Is their intercession sought?' And if we interpret it as thought and wish of the heart, the meaning is that whenever the Prophet wished what he wished of matters, Satan would whisper falsehood to him, calling him to what is not proper. Then Allah abrogates that and guides him to disregarding his whisper.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents a famous objection against the Prophet's infallibility, known as the 'Gharaaniq' incident. The objectors cite the verse from Surat Al-Hajj (52) and a narration that Satan inserted words praising pagans into the Prophet's recitation of Surat An-Najm. This would imply that Satan can tamper with revelation. Razi's answer is twofold. First, he notes that the key term 'umniyyah' can mean either 'recitation' (قراءة) or 'intention/thought' (عزيمة). If we take it as recitation, there are two explanations: (1) the 'gharaneeq' (high swans) refer to angels, and the recitation was originally part of angelic scriptures; when polytheists misinterpreted it, Allah abrogated its recitation. (2) The phrase is actually an interrogative denial ('Is their intercession sought?'), not an affirmation. If we take 'umniyyah' as thought, then the meaning is that whenever the Prophet wished for something, Satan would whisper evil suggestions, but Allah would abrogate those thoughts and guide him to ignore them. Thus, the incident does not involve Satanic alteration of the Quran itself.

Key Terms
أمنية
قراءة
عزيمة
غرانيق
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Gharaaniq incident proves that the Quranic text was subject to Satanic alteration.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the incident can be interpreted in ways that do not involve any change to the Quranic text, thus preserving both the Quran's integrity and the Prophet's infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what are the two possible meanings of 'al-umniyyah' in the verse?
A) Recitation and intention
B) Prayer and fasting
C) Reading and writing
D) Dream and vision

Key Takeaway
Razi's response shows that the Gharaaniq incident, even if taken as historical, does not compromise the integrity of the Quran or the Prophet's infallibility when properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you first heard the Gharaaniq story, did it ever shake your trust in the Quran's preservation? Be honest: why did that doubt feel so compelling, and how does Razi's careful word-study challenge you to be more rigorous in your own approach to controversial claims? This week, pick one doubtful claim about Islam you have encountered and do the same kind of semantic investigation before accepting it.
?
Razi shows that the same word can be interpreted in drastically different ways. In your own life, are there verses you habitually interpret in a way that supports your comfort or convenience rather than seeking the truth? What is one verse you need to re-examine with the same intellectual honesty Razi displays? Commit today to look it up in a reputable tafsir and adjust your understanding.
?
The Prophet was subjected to satanic whispers that he was to disregard. What whispers from your own ego or society tempt you to deviate from the truth? How can you, like the Prophet, 'disregard' those whispers and hold fast to what Allah has revealed? Identify one specific whisper that has been troubling you and write down a concrete action to counter it this week.

Razi's Three Interpretations of the Prophet's Forgiveness Verse
Razi refutes the seventh objection with three interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three interpretations Razi provides to refute the objection that the Prophet (peace be upon him) had sins based on Surah Al-Fath verse 2.

Core Idea
Razi gives three interpretations to show the verse does not imply actual sin.

Razi addresses the seventh objection which cites the verse 'That Allah may forgive you your sins' as evidence that the Prophet committed sins. He provides three distinct answers, showing that the verse refers to before prophethood, to the community's sins, or to sins committed against the Prophet. Each interpretation protects the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (isma).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection based on Surah Al-Fath 2. Recognize how it could be misunderstood to suggest the Prophet had sins.
The objection uses the literal wording to challenge isma.
2
Examine the first interpretation
Razi's first answer: the verse refers to before prophethood or to 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal). Understand the principle that what is a sin for ordinary people may be a minor shortcoming for the righteous.
This uses the saying 'Hasanat al-abrar sayyiat al-muqarrabin.'
3
Examine the second and third interpretations
Second answer: 'your sin' means the sin of your community, as a leader is credited with his followers' actions. Third answer: 'dhanb' is a verbal noun added to the object, meaning sins committed against the Prophet, forgiven for his sake. See how these preserve infallibility.
The third is supported by Quran 8:33.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله تعالى : {لِيَغْفِرَ لَكَ اللَّهُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ وَمَا تَأَخَّرَ} [الفتح: ٢]. والجواب عنه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه محمول على ما قبل النبوة أو على ترك الأفضل كما يقال : "حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين". وثانيها : أن المراد من قوله : {وَمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ} من ذنب أمتك فإن الرجل المعتبر إذا أحسن بعض خدمه أو أساء فإنه يقال له : أنت فعلت ذلك، وإن لم يكن هو الفاعل لذلك الفعل. وثالثها : أن الذنب مصدر، ويجوز إضافته إلى الفاعل والمفعول، والمراد ليغفر لأجلك ولأجل بركتك ما تقدم من ذنبهم في حقك وما تأخر، ويقرب منه قوله تعالى : {وَمَا كَانَ اللَّهُ لِيُعَذِّبَهُمْ وَأَنتَ فِيهِمْ} [الأنفال: ٣٣].
The seventh objection: His saying, exalted is He: 'That Allah may forgive you what has passed of your sin and what will come.' [Al-Fath 2]. The answer to it is from several aspects: First: it is interpreted as referring to before prophethood or to leaving the best, as it is said: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones.' Second: that the meaning of His saying 'what has passed of your sin' is from the sin of your community, for a man of consideration, if he benefits some of his servants or harms, it is said to him: 'You did that,' even if he is not the doer of that act. Third: that 'sin' (dhanb) is a verbal noun, and it is permissible to add it to the agent or the object, and the intended meaning is that for your sake and for your blessing He forgives what has passed of their sin regarding you and what will come, and close to it is His saying: 'And Allah would not punish them while you are among them.' [Al-Anfal 33].
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection raised against the Prophet (peace be upon him) based on the verse 'That Allah may forgive you what has passed of your sin and what will come.' The objection implies that the Prophet had sins requiring forgiveness. Razi provides three answers. First, he interprets the verse as referring to the period before prophethood, when the Prophet was not yet sinless, or to 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal), which is not a sin but a lesser choice. He supports this with the saying 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones,' meaning that actions considered virtuous for ordinary people might be seen as shortcomings for those close to Allah. Second, Razi explains that the pronoun 'your' (ka) in 'your sin' may refer to the sin of the Prophet's community, as one is metaphorically said to have done something done by those under his charge. Third, he notes that 'dhanb' (sin) is a verbal noun that can be attributed to the agent (the Prophet himself) or the object (the sin committed against him), and the intended meaning is that forgiveness is granted for the sake of the Prophet's blessing upon his community. He supports this with the verse 'And Allah would not punish them while you are among them,' indicating that the Prophet's presence prevents punishment. Thus, the verse does not prove the Prophet committed actual sins.

Key Terms
ذنب (dhanb) sin or shortcoming
عصمة (isma) infallibility
تأويل (ta'wil) interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that the Prophet committed actual sins.
Correction: Razi shows the verse is interpreted in three ways that do not imply actual sin: pre-prophethood, metaphorical, or referring to others' sins.

Checkpoint
In the first answer to the seventh objection, how does Razi explain 'what has passed of your sin'?
A) It refers to sins before Islam
B) It means the sins of the Prophet's community
C) It is interpreted as before prophethood or leaving the best
D) It means the Prophet never had any sin

Key Takeaway
Razi's three interpretations show that Surah Al-Fath 2 does not conflict with prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Prophet's 'sin' is interpreted as 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal), what 'best' actions have you left undone, using the excuse that they are not obligatory? How does this make you accountable before Allah?
?
Razi shows that the Prophet is honored by having the sins of the community attributed to him. How does this challenge your own desire for personal credit and taking responsibility for others' failures?
?
If the Prophet needed forgiveness from Allah (in the interpretation of tark al-afdal), how much more do you need it? What specific sin will you confess to Allah today, and what step will you take to turn back to Him?

Answering the Objection from Surah Abasa
Razi answers the eighth objection from Surah Abasa by interpreting the rebuke as concerning tark al-afdal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the Prophet's infallibility by interpreting the divine rebuke in Surah Abasa as concerning leaving the best, not sin.

Core Idea
A divine rebuke does not imply sin; it can guide to a better choice.

When critics point to the verse 'He frowned and turned away' as a flaw in the Prophet, Razi offers a simple but powerful defense. He explains that the rebuke (عتاب) is not for a sin, but for leaving the better option. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
The Arabic is concise; the translation mirrors it.
2
Identify the distinction
Understand that 'عتاب' (rebuke) is different from condemnation for sin. Razi says it is 'ترك الأفضل' (leaving the best), which is not a sin.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
This defense preserves the doctrine of 'isma (infallibility) from sin while acknowledging that prophets can have relative preferences.
The key is that leaving the best is not a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : {عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى أَن جَاءَهُ الْأَعْمَى} [عبس: ١-٢]. والجواب: يحمل هذا العتاب على ترك الأفضل.
The eighth objection: His saying, exalted is He: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him' [Abasa 1-2]. The answer: This rebuke is interpreted as concerning leaving the best.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection based on the verse 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him,' which appears to rebuke the Prophet for his reaction to the blind man Ibn Umm Maktum. The objection suggests this implies a fault. Razi answers simply: this rebuke (itab) is interpreted as concerning 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal). That is, the Prophet's action was not a sin but a lesser choice—he could have handled the situation differently, and the divine admonishment was to guide him to the better option. Thus, it does not contradict his infallibility in matters of sin.

Key Terms
عتاب (itab) rebuke
ترك الأفضل (tark al-afdal) leaving the best

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet sinned by frowning at the blind man.
Correction: Razi explains it was not a sin but a less optimal action, and the rebuke was to guide him to the better choice.

Checkpoint
How does Razi explain the rebuke in Surah Abasa?
A) It was a sin that required repentance
B) It was an admonishment for leaving the best
C) It was not addressed to the Prophet
D) It was abrogated

Key Takeaway
Razi defends prophetic infallibility by interpreting the Surah Abasa rebuke as guidance toward a better choice, not punishment for sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Allah points out a shortcoming in your life through a trial or a verse, do you immediately assume it is a punishment for sin, or do you consider that He might be guiding you to a better choice? Will you change your perspective today?
?
Is there an area in your life where you are content with merely avoiding sin, while Allah is calling you to strive for the best? What is holding you back from that higher standard?
?
The Prophet was rebuked for not choosing the best course. How do you respond when someone corrects you for a lesser action, even if it wasn't a sin? Do you accept it as guidance or become defensive?

Razi's Refutation of the Ninth Objection from Surah Al-An'am
Razi refutes the ninth objection from Surah Al-An'am.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that the verse 'Do not drive away...' implies the Prophet drove away the poor, by clarifying that it is a prohibition and a divine proof.

Core Idea
The verse prohibited an action, not described one.

In defending Prophethood, Razi addresses an objection based on Quranic wording. Critics claimed the Prophet intended to drive away poor believers, but the verse forbids it. Razi shows the verse was a command, not a report. Let's examine his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the ninth objection quoting Surah Al-An'am 52. Note what the objector claims.
The objector says the Prophet was told not to drive away, implying he wanted to.
2
Identify Razi's answer
Read Razi's response. He distinguishes between a prohibition and an action. The verse forbids an act, not describes an act.
Key: 'It is not apparent that he drove them away'.
3
Understand the wisdom
Razi explains the reason for the prohibition: disbelievers asked him to drive away the poor. The verse became a proof for him to refuse.
This shows divine support.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this defense protects the Prophet's character and refutes the objection without denying the verse's command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : {وَلَا تَطْرُدِ الَّذِينَ يَدْعُونَ رَبَّهُم بِالْغَدَاةِ وَالْعَشِيِّ} [الأنعام: ٥٢]. والجواب : ليس في الظاهر أنه طردهم وإنما فيه النهي عن طردهم، وحكمة هذا النهي أن جمعًا من الكفار طلبوا منه طرد الفقراء، فالله تعالى أنزل هذه الآية لتكون حجة له في الامتناع عن قبول قولهم.
The ninth objection: His saying, exalted is He: 'And do not drive away those who call upon their Lord in the morning and the evening' [Al-An'am 52]. The answer: It is not apparent that he drove them away, but rather in it is the prohibition of driving them away, and the wisdom of this prohibition is that a group of disbelievers asked him to drive away the poor, so Allah, exalted is He, revealed this verse to be a proof for him in refusing to accept their statement.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage addresses the ninth objection based on the verse 'And do not drive away those who call upon their Lord...' The objection suggests that the Prophet might have intended to drive away the poor believers, but the verse prohibits him. Razi clarifies that the verse does not state that the Prophet actually drove them away; rather, it contains a prohibition against doing so. The background wisdom is that the disbelievers asked the Prophet to drive away the poor followers so that they might come and listen. Allah revealed this verse to provide the Prophet with a divine argument to refuse their request, not because he had actually done it. Thus, the verse does not imply any wrongdoing on the part of the Prophet.

Key Terms
طرد (tard) driving away
حجة (hujja) proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that the Prophet drove away the poor believers.
Correction: The verse is a command not to drive them away, not a report that he did so. It was revealed in response to the disbelievers' request, providing the Prophet with a divine proof to refuse.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the purpose of the prohibition in Surah Al-An'am?
A) The Prophet had driven away the poor
B) To provide the Prophet a proof against the disbelievers' request
C) To show that the poor are sinful
D) To abrogate an earlier command

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Do not drive away' is a divine command, not an accusation, and serves as a proof for the Prophet's stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been pressured by others to exclude someone who is 'weak' or 'poor' for the sake of worldly benefit? How does this verse give you the courage to refuse, knowing it is a divine command?
?
Do you realize that the Prophet was never accused of wrongdoing; rather, the verse was a preemptive proof. How does this change your understanding of Allah's care for His messengers, and what does it demand of you in defending the honor of the Prophet?
?
What worldly expectations or social pressures have tempted you to act against clear divine commands? Since this verse shows that even a potential suspicion is addressed, are you willing to examine your own actions and seek Allah's proof before yielding to human demands?

Answering the Objection from Surah At-Tawbah on the Prophet's Repentance
Razi interprets the verse about Allah's forgiveness of the Prophet as concerning leaving the best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that the Prophet's repentance implies sin by interpreting it as leaving the best.

Core Idea
Repentance in the Quran does not always indicate sin—it can be for leaving the best.

In this passage, Razi addresses a common objection against prophetic sinlessness based on Quran 9:117. The objector argues that repentance (tawba) is always preceded by sin, so the Prophet must have sinned. Razi provides a concise answer rooted in theological nuance. This lesson unpacks his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector cites the verse and draws the conclusion that repentance implies sin.
The objection is the tenth in a series.
2
Understand the logical structure
The objection is: tawba is always preceded by sin → the Prophet's tawba → therefore, he sinned. Grasp this reasoning.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi says the verse is 'carried on leaving the best.' This means the Prophet's action was not a sin but a lesser optimal choice. Forgiveness (tawba) here is metaphorical or relates to such minor shortcomings.
This preserves the doctrine of prophetic infallibility.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how interpreting tawba as tark al-afdal resolves the objection without admitting sin. This is a key hermeneutical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة العاشرة : قوله تعالى : {لَقَد تَابَ اللَّهُ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ} [التوبة: ١١٧] والتوبة مسبوقة بالذنب. والجواب : أنه محمول على ترك الأفضل.
The tenth objection: His saying, exalted is He: 'Allah has certainly forgiven the Prophet' [At-Tawbah 117], and repentance is preceded by sin. The answer: It is interpreted as concerning leaving the best.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage addresses the tenth objection based on the verse 'Allah has certainly forgiven the Prophet' from Surah At-Tawbah. The objection states that repentance (tawba) implies a prior sin, so this verse indicates the Prophet committed a sin. Razi answers by saying this verse is interpreted as concerning 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal). That is, what the Prophet did was not a sin but an action that was less than the optimal choice, and the forgiveness (or acceptance of repentance) is metaphorical or relates to such minor shortcomings. Thus, it does not imply actual sin.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba) repentance
ترك الأفضل (tark al-afdal) leaving the best

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's repentance in the Quran proves he committed a sin.
Correction: Repentance can be for leaving the best, not necessarily for a sin.

Checkpoint
How does Razi interpret the verse 'Allah has certainly forgiven the Prophet'?
A) The Prophet committed a major sin
B) It means Allah honored the Prophet
C) It is about leaving the best
D) It is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by interpreting the verse as concerning leaving the best, thereby preserving the Prophet's sinlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent, do you only feel remorse for clear sins? Or do you also feel the weight of moments when you chose the merely permissible over the excellent—even when no sin was committed? How does the Prophet's repentance for leaving the best challenge your own standard of accountability? Will you today make a specific intention to repent not only from sins but from every moment you settled for less than what Allah deserves?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) repented for not choosing the best option. Reflect on the last time you made a choice that was permissible but not the best. Did you feel any need to turn back to Allah? If not, what does that say about your awareness of excellence in your faith? How can you cultivate a heart that is sensitive to even the slightest falling short of what is most pleasing to Allah?

Answering the Objection from Seeking Forgiveness
Razi interprets the command to seek forgiveness as for leaving the best or hypothetical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends prophetic sinlessness by interpreting the command to seek forgiveness as either for leaving the best or as a hypothetical assumption.

Core Idea
Prophet's seeking forgiveness is for leaving the best or hypothetical, not sin.

Razi addresses an objection based on the Qur'anic command for the Prophet to seek forgiveness. Critics argue this implies the Prophet committed sins. Razi provides two interpretations that protect the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness. This passage explains those answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the verse and hadith cited by the objector. Note that the command to seek forgiveness is presented as evidence for sin.
The verse is from Surah Ghafir, and the hadith mentions seeking forgiveness seventy times daily.
2
Identify the two answers
Razi gives two interpretations: (1) it refers to 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal), and (2) it is on the hypothetical assumption 'I have sinned' (taqdir: adhantu).
Both answers avoid implying actual sin.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Ask yourself why these answers preserve the Prophet's sinlessness. Consider that 'leaving the best' is not a sin, and the hypothetical case does not assert actual sin.
This is a standard defense in kalam.
4
Consider the incomplete text
Note that the Arabic text ends abruptly with 'adhantu' (I sinned). The full answer likely continues, but the meaning is clear from what is given.
This lesson is based on the available fragment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الحادية عشرة : قوله تعالى : {وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ} [غافر: ٥٥] وفي الحديث: «وإني لأستغفر الله في اليوم والليلة سبعين مرة». والجواب أنه محمول على ترك الأفضل، أو يكون على تقدير : أذنبت.
The eleventh objection: His saying, exalted is He: 'And seek forgiveness for your sin' [Ghafir 55] and in the hadith: 'And indeed I seek forgiveness from Allah seventy times in a day and a night.' The answer: It is interpreted as concerning leaving the best, or it is on the assumption: 'I have sinned' (adhantu).
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage addresses the eleventh objection based on the verse 'And seek forgiveness for your sin' and the hadith where the Prophet says he seeks forgiveness seventy times daily. The objection argues that commanding the Prophet to seek forgiveness implies he had sins. Razi provides two answers. First, it is interpreted as concerning 'leaving the best' (tark al-afdal), meaning the Prophet's action was not sinful but less than optimal, and seeking forgiveness is for such minor shortcomings. Second, he suggests it could be on the assumption (taqdir) of 'I have sinned' (adhantu), meaning the phrase is hypothetical—as if to say, if one were to sin, then seek forgiveness. The text ends here, but the explanation remains that the Prophet's seeking forgiveness is not proof of actual sin.

Key Terms
استغفار (istighfar) seeking forgiveness
تأويل (ta'wil) interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding the Prophet to seek forgiveness proves he committed sins.
Correction: Razi explains that the command can be interpreted as for leaving the best or as a hypothetical, neither of which implies actual sin.

Checkpoint
What are the two interpretations Razi gives for the command to seek forgiveness?
A) It is for major sins and minor sins
B) It is for leaving the best or on a hypothetical assumption
C) It is for the community, not the Prophet
D) It was abrogated by later verses

Key Takeaway
Razi defends prophetic sinlessness by interpreting the command to seek forgiveness as relating to leaving the best or as a hypothetical assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the Prophet ﷺ sought forgiveness even for leaving what is best. How often do you settle for what is merely permissible when you could choose the best? What is one specific area of your life where you can replace a permissible action with a better one today?
?
If the Prophet's seeking forgiveness was for hypothetical shortcomings, what does your own constant need for forgiveness reveal about your actual sins? Are you truly repentant, or do you take forgiveness for granted?
?
The lesson shows that even the highest of creation humbly sought forgiveness. Does your pride prevent you from seeking forgiveness as often as you should? What excuse will you give Allah for neglecting istighfar?

Objection 7: The Verse of Forgiveness and Three Answers
Imam al-Razi presents the seventh objection and gives three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends prophetic infallibility by interpreting Quran 48:2 through three distinct answers.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of the Prophet does not imply sin; it refers to leaving the better or is figurative.

The seventh objection against prophetic infallibility cites Quran 48:2, which speaks of Allah forgiving the Prophet's sins. This seems to directly contradict the doctrine of 'ismah. Imam al-Razi offers three carefully reasoned responses, each showing that the verse can be understood in a way consistent with prophetic sinlessness. Let's explore these answers and see how they preserve the principle of infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and the Verse
Start by reading the Arabic text of the objection and the verse it cites. Notice how the objection uses the apparent meaning of the verse to challenge 'ismah.
The verse is from Surah al-Fath, verse 2.
2
Understand the First Answer: Before Prophethood or Leaving the Better
The first answer explains that 'forgiveness' either refers to pre-prophethood actions (which are not sinful after prophethood) or to 'leaving the better' (tark al-afdal), a high standard where minor lapses are called 'sins' only relatively.
This uses the principle: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones.'
3
Analyze the Second Answer: Figurative Attribution of Sin
The second answer interprets 'your sin' as the sins of the Prophet's nation, using the analogy of a leader being credited with the actions of his followers.
4
Examine the Third Answer: Verbal Noun and Forgiveness for the Prophet's Sake
The third answer treats 'sin' as a verbal noun that can be attributed to the object, meaning sins committed against the Prophet are forgiven for his sake, similar to Quran 8:33.
5
Reflect on the Overall Principle
After reviewing all three answers, consider how they collectively defend 'ismah without denying the verse. Each answer offers a different angle, showing the depth of Islamic scholarly interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله تعالى لِيَغْفِرَ لَكَ اللهُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ وَمَا تَأَخَّرَ) [الفَتْح : ٢] . والجواب عنه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه محمول على ما قبل النبوة أو على ترك الأفضل كما يقال : "حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين " . وثانيها : أن المراد من قوله : ومَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ ﴾ [الفَتْح : ٢] من ذنب أمتك فإن الرجل المعتبر إذا أحسن بعض خدمه أو أساء فإنه يقال له : أنت فعلت ذلك، وإن لم يكن هو الفاعل لذلك الفعل. وثالثها الذنب مصدر، ويجوز إضافته إلى الفاعل والمفعول، والمراد ليغفر لأجلك ولأجل بركتك ما تقدم من ذنبهم في حقك وما تأخر، ويقرب منه قوله تعالى : ل وَمَا كَانَ اللهُ لِيُعَذِّبَهُمْ وَأَنتَ فِيهِمْ ﴾ [الأنفال : ٣٣] ﴾ .
The seventh objection: His statement, exalted is He, 'That Allah may forgive you what has passed of your sin and what will come' [al-Fath 48:2]. The answer to it is from several aspects: One of them: that it is carried upon what was before prophethood or upon leaving the better, as it is said: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the bad deeds of the near ones.' And the second: that the meaning of His statement 'what has passed of your sin' is of the sin of your nation, for if a respected man treats some of his servants well or badly, it is said to him: You did that, even if he is not the doer of that deed. And the third: 'sin' is a verbal noun, and it is permissible to add it to the agent or the object, and the intended meaning is: for your sake and for your blessing, He forgives what has passed of their sin against you and what will come, and close to it is His statement, exalted is He: 'And Allah would not punish them while you are among them' [al-Anfal 8:33].
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection against prophetic infallibility (ismah) based on Quran 48:2, which speaks of forgiveness for the Prophet. Imam al-Razi offers three answers. First, the verse refers to sins before prophethood or to 'leaving the better' (tark al-afdal), a category where an action is not sinful but is below the best, and such lapses are considered 'sins' only relative to the high station of prophets. Second, the phrase 'your sin' could mean the sins of his nation, as a leader is credited with his followers' actions. Third, the word 'sin' (dhanb) is a verbal noun that can be attributed to the agent or the object; here it means sins committed against the Prophet, and forgiveness is granted for his sake, similar to the principle in Quran 8:33 where the Prophet's presence prevents punishment.

Key Terms
عصمة
ترك الأفضل
ذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Quran 48:2 proves the Prophet committed sins, contradicting the doctrine of 'ismah.
Correction: The verse does not necessarily imply actual sins; it can be understood as referring to pre-prophethood actions, leaving the better, figurative attribution to the nation, or sins committed against the Prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer in this passage, what does the forgiveness in Quran 48:2 refer to?
A) Sins committed after prophethood
B) Matters before prophethood or leaving the better
C) Sins of the entire human race
D) The Prophet's future sins

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi shows that Quran 48:2 can be reconciled with prophetic infallibility through three distinct but complementary interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short, do you dismiss it as 'just leaving the better', or does the Prophet's perfection shame you into raising your standard? What one area of your life have you been settling for 'good enough' when you could strive for excellence?
?
If the Prophet is credited with the actions of his nation, what does your daily behavior say about him? Are you living in a way that honors him, or are you adding to his burden?
?
The verse says Allah does not punish a people while the Prophet is among them. Do you truly feel the Prophet's presence in your life? If not, what must change so that his barakah is not wasted on you?

The Eighth Objection: Surah 'Abasa and the Response
Imam al-Razi presents the eighth objection and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from Surah 'Abasa (80:1-2) is answered by the concept of 'leaving the better' (tark al-afdal).

Core Idea
Prophets are rebuked not for sin, but for leaving the better.

This lesson addresses a common objection raised against the sinlessness of prophets: the verse in which Allah rebukes the Prophet for frowning at the blind man. The answer lies in a subtle distinction between sin and 'leaving what is better.' Understanding this distinction is essential for defending prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the verse cited in the objection: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him.' Notice the apparent reproach.
2
Understand the challenge
Why does this verse seem to imply a fault? The objection claims it contradicts prophetic sinlessness.
The objector assumes any reproach implies sin.
3
Learn the answer
Imam al-Razi's answer: the reproach is for 'leaving the better' (tark al-afdal), not for a sin.
Tark al-afdal is a permissible action that is less excellent.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this preserves sinlessness: prophets are expected to choose the best, but a minor shortfall is not a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى أَن جَاءَهُ الْأَعْمَى [عبس: ٢.١]. والجواب: يحمل هذا العتاب على ترك الأفضل.
The eighth objection: His statement, exalted is He: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him' [Abasa 80:1-2]. The answer: This reproach is carried upon leaving the better.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection: Quran 80:1-2 rebukes the Prophet for frowning at the blind man Abdullah ibn Umm Maktum. The objection is that this rebuke implies a fault. The answer is concise: this reproach is not for a sin but for 'leaving the better' (tark al-afdal), a term in kalam meaning an action that is permissible but less excellent. Prophets are expected to choose the best course; if they choose a lesser good, they may be gently corrected, but this does not imply sin.

Key Terms
عتاب
ترك الأفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves the Prophet committed a sin.
Correction: The reproach is for leaving the better, not for sin.

Checkpoint
How does Imam al-Razi answer the objection from Surah 'Abasa?
A) He denies the event occurred
B) He says it was a sin but forgiven
C) He interprets the reproach as being about leaving the better
D) He says the verse was abrogated

Key Takeaway
The reproach in Surah 'Abasa is for leaving the better, not for sin, preserving the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose a permissible but lesser action over a better one, do you feel the gentle reproach of your conscience? How will you respond tomorrow when faced with a similar choice?
?
The Prophet, despite his high status, was reminded to choose the best. What does this teach you about your own effort to pursue excellence in your worship and dealings?
?
Are there areas in your life where you settle for what is merely permissible while ignoring the better? What is one specific change you can make today?
